![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/4dd83bf3b85878142537cc7a5e272707b597611a3255391ae3944bdb8616766f.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a3303bf0bdb01644584bdb081bd90fbf49c2725f84e351f4ec916be880010f1e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6bb8c79d8f7d125b62d97444f5412c8fed77f2ff4e76c8a3acdc99a9965f449c.jpg)  
Figure 31.9 Prepared burning surfaces. (a) A thin section from the constructed substrate of a Neolithic hearth (Turkey). The hearth is paved with fragments of basalt (ba) and mudbrick (mb). The mudbrick fragments are rubified and cracked, likely as a result of heating. Box indicates the location of (b) and (c). Incident light. (b) At magnification, ashes are visible between the basalt and the mudbrick (arrow). PPL. (c) Same view as (b), XPL. The edges of the vesicular basalt fragment are scalloped in morphology and contain coatings of mudbrick (arrow). The mudbrick coating juxtaposed against the ash-filled crack indicates that the mudbrick was pressed against the basalt while it was still wet, and the crack formed at a later point. (d) A low magnification panoramic image of a hearth that contains at least two burnt residue layers, the upper associated with a prepared substrate. The lower hearth (BR1) is filled with ashes sourced from both wood (BR1a) and dung (BR1b). A large fragment of heated mudbrick (mb) is also visible. The substrate of the lower hearth was not sampled. The lower hearth is capped with a layer of plaster that provided the substrate (CS2) for the upper hearth, which also contains burnt materials (BR2). Neolithic, Turkey. PPL. (e) Photomicrograph of the plaster substrate, CS2. The plaster layer is overlain by wood ashes that, like BR1, contain inclusions of rubbed mudbrick (mb). PPL (f) Same view as (e), XPL. The plaster is calcareous and contains inclusions of volcanic glass.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6d1145318ab094563e0e4f5bd3908f99d402d987d7c0c8142fa198c62e1667d7.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d4f5cbe625b041ce546f1f584afbd17f3efc3bfdffe22ef5d7594c57f5c0b997.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/654d618738b7912dc6db03876398b8c073f3e0b7ab4e9cee84df506df8f98724.jpg)

Pit structures involve excavation of the substrate (see Figure 31.8d) and burial of the ignited fuel in order to facilitate combustion under reducing conditions. Thus, the resulting combustion feature is characterized by burnt residues filling a depression with a concave combustion substrate whose fabric depends on the nature of the excavated ground (natural soil, sediment, occupation surface, diverse stratified succession, etc.). Micromorphological descriptions of the content and substrate of several large pit fires from Hayonim Cave can be found in Meignen et al. (2007). By nature of their life histories, which include intentional excavation and removal of burnt materials, some degree of reworking will always be present in these features. For example, micromorphological analysis of a cooking pit fire from an abandoned Hadza camp (Tanzania) showed the presence of sediment rip-up clasts derived from the scooping out of ashes (MalloI et al. 2007). Likewise, charcoal burning pits documented by Zerboni et al. (2013: Figure 2) exhibit both intact and disturbed areas.

The function of pit features may be reconstructed based on morphology and the presence of inclusions. Some types of pit features can function as simple kilns. For example, experimental lime 'pit kilns' documented

by Goren & Goring-Morris (2008) lack constructed walls. Micromorphological samples of these features contain partially burnt limestone rock fragments. Berna et al. (2007) also documented the presence of a copper casting pit at the site of Tel Dor. Micromorphological analyses of the feature, which indicated the presence of rubified sediment, charcoal, glass, and phytoliths, were supplemented by FTIR analyses of the sediments and elemental analyses, the latter which indicated high concentrations of copper. Roasting pits and their inclusions have been sampled and described by Goldberg (2000: Figure 7).

Fire installations are the most morphologically and structurally complicated forms of contained combustion features. Like destruction layers, the first step in the analysis of fire installations is their observation in the field. Fire installations can be produced from a wide variety of materials (see Gur-Arieh et al. 2014), and can have highly variable morphologies. Micromorphological analyses of these features and nearby architectural elements can aid in understanding how firing occurred, which types of fuels were used, how the structures were maintained, and in some cases, their function. For example, Gur-Arieh et al. (2014) used micromorphological

observations of altered substrates and walls to determine whether the structures were internally or externally fuelled. Matthews (2010: Figure 3) observed the presence of two types of fuels (wood and dung) as well as heated materials sourced from the walls and ceiling of a Neolithic oven. Elsewhere within the site, these materials are also present in rake-out deposits in middens, which implies that the reworked materials source from ovens (Shillito & Matthews 2013). In other contexts, Matthews has observed ochre inside ovens and in rake out, which she interprets as ritual use of the features (Matthews 2005). She has also utilized micromorphological analyses of oven walls to understand their history of construction and rebuilding, including multiple layers of plaster that indicate refurbishing of the surfaces. Analyses of nearby walls can be informative about the conditions of burning and the environment within the structures when installations are located in enclosed spaces. For example, Goodman-Elgar (2008: Figure 8f) illustrates the microscopic characteristics of soot layers on mudbrick walls located near an interior cooking installation.

The function(s) of fire installations may be reconstructed by the presence of inclusions (metal slag, ceramic fragments, lime lumps, etc.) combined with the feature context (domestic versus nondomestic). For example, Ismail-Meyer et al. (2013) identified a ceramic firing area on the basis of micromorphological samples that contained abundant charcoal mixed with both unburnt clay and materials used as temper. Finally, Angelucci (2008: Figure 7) documents micromorphological characteristics of a Roman cremation furnace, which include the composition of the mud-brick walls, and the presence of ashes and burnt bones inside.

# 31.6 Stratigraphy and Fabric of Physically Reworked Combustion Structures

Combustion features that have been physically reworked – either through human action or nonhuman processes – can be highly variable in morphology. Their depositional histories, including the agent and degree of reworking, may be inferred from morphology in the field and aspects of microscopic fabric and structure. Features that are reworked by geological and biological processes can be used to understand processes that likely impacted other materials at the site, as well as aspects of the local environment. In contrast, human reworking can be exceptionally informative about structuring of activities, cleaning and maintenance of the site, or storage and utilization of byproducts of combustion (Mentzer 2014). For example, using composition, fabric, and structure,

micromorphologists have documented reworked combustion features such as ash dumps (Goldberg et al. 2009), ash storage containers (Matthews 2010, Milek 2012); and utilization of ashes in plaster (Mentzer & Quade 2013: Figure 3), in agriculture (Huisman et al. 2012) and as a cleaning product and byre bedding additive (Milek 2012).

In the field, archaeologists often hypothesize that features are reworked when combustion residues are present but internal stratigraphy is absent (Schiegl et al. 2003; Mentzer 2014). However, field identification can be difficult. For example, at Tabun C (Israel), combustion features that displayed visible layering reminiscent of in situ combustion features exhibited micromorphological characteristics suggestive of extensive reworking (Albert et al. 1999).

The distance over which combustion residues can be reworked and redistributed is variable, from several millimetres or less, to several meters or more. Sherwood (2008) calls deposits that have been reworked at the location where they were burned 'mixed burned deposits'. Mentzer (2014) suggests that small-scale reworking of combustion features may be indicated by an increase in porosity or the presence of larger voids relative to the typical packing voids exhibited in intact hearths (e.g., Mallol et al. 2007). Dibble et al. (2009) also suggest that some fine materials within combustion features can be subjected to gravitational settling, which would redistribute combustion residues within a feature over a few millimetres or centimetres.

# 31.6.1 Geological Reworking

Combustion residues that have been reworked by higher energy processes may display characteristics such as rounding or laminations and textural sorting (Figure 31.10).

Water and wind activity removes certain burned components and sorts the remainder by size and density. These effects can be visible in thin section. Goldberg (2001) reported rounded fragments of charcoal within laminated Middle Palaeolithic deposits in Kebara (Israel) and interpreted these deposits as having formed through low-energy flowing water. Similarly, Araujo et al. (2008) report laminated, and likely waterlain ashes from the Paleoindian site of Lapa das Bolieras (Brazil). At Dust Cave (USA) Homsey & Capo (2006) and Sherwood (2001) reported charcoal 'stringers' which exhibited textural sorting, grading and the absence of silt-sized components, likely indicative of reworking by water. At Obi-Rakhmat (Uzbekistan) Mallol et al. (2009) used the presence of redoximorphic features and a matrix composed of 'clotted' calcite to infer that combustion residues had been reworked by spring activity. Some of

the finer grained combustion residues documented at this site were contained in laminated silty clay coatings and infillings (Figure 31.10a). Finally, Villagran et al. (2011) have investigated the effects of low-energy water flow on combustion residues in an experimental context.

Deposits associated with colluvial and slope processes typically lack the textural sorting and fine laminations found in water-lain or aeolian deposits. Rather, these deposits exhibit a more heterogenous composition, and can display key micromorphological characteristics, such as grain rounding and coating (Miller 2015). In colluvial systems, combustion residues and components behave as other sedimentary components within the deposit, and therefore will often be intimately mixed with geogenic materials (Figure 31.10b). In aeolian systems, the ash might be blown away, while denser components of the burnt residue layer may remain in situ or be slightly translocated and later incorporated into the underlying substrate through by trampling or sedimentation (Figure 31.10c).

Cryoturbation can also influence the preservation of combustion features. Given that ash is typically composed of silt-sized grains of micritic calcite, one can expect, following Van Vliet-Lanoë (2010) that platy and lenticular microstructures and cappings will develop when ash-rich combustion features are subjected to freeze-thaw processes (Figure 31.10d). Coarser components of combustion features, such as sand- and gravel-sized fragments of charcoal, burnt bone, or even cemented ashes, can exhibit rounding and also the development of stratified cappings around the grains (van Vliet-Lanoë 2010: Figures 9a and 15b). In Hohe Fels (Germany) Goldberg et al. (2003) and Schiegl et al. (2003) report rounded, sand-sized fragments of burnt and unburnt bones, some of which exhibit stratified coatings, which they interpret as evidence of cryoturbation of dumped, burnt materials.

# 31.6.2 Biological Reworking

Biological reworking of combustion features results in the formation of microscopic characteristics typical of bioturbation (Figure 31.11), such as filled biogalleries or burrows (Figure 31.11a), faecal pellets (Figure 31.11b), some of which can contain combustion residues (Wattez 1992; Albert et al. 1999; Goldberg et al. 2009), extensive homogenization, granular structure (Figure 31.11c), and the presence of channels or channel microstructure. Feeding activities may also lead to loss of some burnt materials. Charcoal fragments (typically smaller than two mm) are a food source for earthworms (Ponge et al. 2006; Topoliantz & Ponge 2005). Bioturbation does not always influence different portions of the same combustion

feature equally (Figure 31.11d). Differential bioturbation of ashes relative to charcoal may result when certain burrowing animals avoid ashy, alkaline deposits. Similarly, when ashy lens become cemented, they can prove to be impenetrable to burrowing animals (Mentzer 2014). Finally, larger animals have been known to excavate and disturb combustion features, either as a result of denning activities, or during scavenging of food items (Camaros et al. 2013). The signatures of these types of behaviours have not been studied using micromorphology; however, it is likely that some animal disturbances could appear similar to human activities such as sweeping and rake out (see below, section 31.6.3).

# 31.6.3 Human Reworking

Following use of a fire, humans can intentionally or unintentionally redistribute the burnt residues (Figure 31.12), a process that has been relatively well studied from a micromorphological perspective. Open hearths that lack prepared substrates may be situated in close proximity to, or overlapping with, other activity areas. Therefore these features can be subject to passive reworking, such as trampling, or active reworking as a result of maintenance. Experiments by Miller et al. (2010) subjected open hearths to rake out, dumping and trampling. The experimenters noted that raking led to textural sorting of the removed materials, since larger fragments of burnt bone and charcoal remained in the hearth, whereas the finer-grained ash, charcoal and burnt bone were redeposited. Raked-out materials exhibited an open microstructure, while the scooped and dumped deposits also showed open microstructures, but comprised a wider range of grain sizes compared to the better sorted, raked-out deposit. The experimenters concluded that these characteristics may vary in different archaeological contexts and therefore it may be difficult to distinguish between sweeping and dumping on the basis of micromorphological characteristics alone. Rather, they suggested that proximity of combustion residues to a rubified or altered substrate may possibly help determine if a combustion feature was raked out, or if it was swept out and dumped. Comparable results were obtained in experiments conducted by Mallol et al. (2013b). However, Meignen et al. (2007) suggest that hearth rake out can be identified by larger fragments of combusted materials (and also possibly nonburnt materials) that are heterogeneously dispersed and that exhibit a roughly horizontal orientation. Likewise, Homsey & Capo (2006) argue that the presence of horizontal charcoal stringers implies hearth rake out, assuming that this activity took place before complete combustion of the fuel. They contrast this to ash dumps, which they argue would have taken place after complete combustion.

Figure 31.10 Combustion features reworked by geological agents. (a) Combustion residues reworked by water. Obi Rakhmat Middle Palaeolithic site, Uzbekistan. PPL. (b) A colluvial deposit contains reworked aggregates of burnt materials mixed with other sediment. The burnt materials are sourced from different areas of the site, as evidenced by the presence of rounded aggregates of cemented ashes (CA) mixed with loose, uncemented ashes. Upper Paleolithic (UP), Turkey. PPL. (c) Subrounded char and burnt bone fragments reworked by wind. Mandrin Middle Palaeolithic site, France. PPL. (d) A cryoturbated combustion feature is composed largely of dumped burnt and unburnt bone. ![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/cbd32cf120d4d751e4a211ef6bdded779f6ff28213241642f04d2dd18c2536e2.jpg)

Schiegl et al. (2003) identified a Gravettian midden-like deposit of dumped, burnt bone on the basis of the following characteristics: subrounded, sand-sized grains of burnt and unburnt bone, which were adjacent to one another; lack of a heat-altered substrate; clear bedding with some beds and lenses containing mostly burnt bone, and others containing unburnt bone; high porosity of the deposit; and general lack of evidence for compaction or in situ snapping or crushing of bone. Extensive ash dumps identified using similar characteristics have been reported from other Middle and Upper Palaeolithic contexts (Goldberg 2001; Goldberg 2003; Meignen et al. 2007).

Trampling (Figure 31.12a) can also impact the fabric and structure of combustion features, through horizontal

and vertical displacement of combusted materials, compaction of combustion features, and snapping and crushing of burnt bone (see also Figure 31.8a and b; Miller et al. 2010; Mallol et al. 2013b; Miller et al. 2013).

The process of human reworking of materials from constructed features has also been studied at a variety of sites. Constructed features, which include prepared surfaces and installations, if used frequently will require regular cleaning. Evidence of cleaning activities may be found in dumped materials in urban middens. In these settings, observations of the type of fuel as well as inclusions of heated mudbrick and plaster can be used to determine their source. Furthermore, the fabric and structure, in particular the porosity and orientation of inclusions, can provide information about the relationship

(a) An intact combustion feature, composed of a rubified substrate (CS) overlain by two layers of burnt residues (BRa and BRb), is partially destroyed by a large burrow visible on the right side of the image. The burrow infilling contains fragments of burnt bone and charcoal mixed with unburnt materials and exhibits higher porosity relative to the nearby intact sediments. MSA open hearth, South Africa. Postdepositional cementation has contributed to the preservation of the ashy matrix in the burnt residue layer. In contrast, the rare inclusions of charcoal exhibit evidence of localized partial destruction by insect activity, evidenced by fragmentation and faecal pellets. UP, Turkey. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/e7e49e3079c074356cb7c649aa976ea50bc63b22c79a5841f825c4be045ff3c6.jpg)

of dumping areas to other activities (see Figure 31.12d and e; Matthews 2010; Shillito & Matthews 2013). Matthews (2010) notes that swept and trampled materials from installations, like those from open hearths, exhibit size sorting and fragmentation, and also contain few articulated phytoliths. Alternatively, burnt materials removed from installations may be utilized for other purposes, such as fertilizing fields (Huisman et al. 2012); for producing a variety of materials that include lye, soap, glass, and plaster (Mentzer & Quade 2013); or as absorbents of odour and moisture in rooms and stables

(Milek 2012). In the latter example, intentional dumping or spreading of ashes on floors can generate thick layers of reworked materials that later become compacted and develop platy structure as a result of human foot traffic (Milek 2012: Figure 6). Burnt materials removed from constructed features may be used directly, or stored for later use. According to Milek (2012), thin sections from an ethnohistorical ash storage area contained discrete layers of ashes of different type. Ash storage bins have also been described by Matthews (2010). Finally, in some cases, the source and mode of anthropogenic reworking

may be difficult to establish. In a multianalytical study at Tel Dor, Berna et al. (2007) documented the presence of reworked material, evidenced in thin section by inclusions of clay crusts that are randomly oriented, paired with high temperature mineral phases that indicated a probable source from an oven or other type of industrial setting.

# 31.7 Microscopic Evidence for Chemical Diagenesis of Intact and Reworked Combustion Structures

Most combustion features encountered in archaeological sites have undergone some degree of chemical diagenesis. Indeed, the formation of calcite in ashes from calcium oxide is a form of chemical diagenesis that occurs within combustion features following burning. Only combustion features in highly arid conditions escape this process. In all other settings, the type and degree of diagenesis is dependent on the parent materials, as well as aspects of the substrate, overburden, and groundwater, including dissolved components and pH. Diagenesis can occur syndepositionally or postdepositionally, especially when combustion features are on or near the ground surface. Deep and rapid burial minimizes ongoing alteration, and therefore evidence for diagenesis can be informative about past environmental and chemical conditions within the site. Diagenetic processes can remove materials, alter or replace materials, or introduce new minerals to combustion features.

Careful observation of each of these types of processes can lead to further interpretation of the significance of diagenesis in terms of the preservation of other archaeological materials and environmental conditions following models provided by Karkanas et al. (2000) and Karkanas (2010).

# 31.7.1 Mineral Removal

Chemical removal of minerals and other substances from combustion features occurs when acidic or alkaline waters percolate through the site as a result of rainfall, dripwater, or groundwater activity.

Calcite dissolution impacts in particular the ash component of combustion features (Figure 31.13). Relative to other calcareous materials in archaeological sites, ashes are especially susceptible to dissolution under acidic conditions due to their high surface to bulk ratio (Friesem et al. 2014b). This process may be recognized in thin section by the overall absence of ashes with the exception of localized preservation in sheltered areas (Figure 31.13a–c)

or dissolution of more resistant forms of carbonate (Figure 31.13d).

Silica dissolution occurs under alkaline conditions (Fraysse et al. 2009; Cabanes et al. 2011). Like calcareous ashes, the phytolith component of ashes is also differentially impacted by silica dissolution relative to other siliceous materials due to their high surface area and initially disorganized (opaline) crystal structure. Remnants of dissolved phytoliths may be one type of material termed 'siliceous aggregates' that are recognized in thin section by their porous structure and optical characteristics (Figure 31.13e and f; see also Schiegl et al. 1996).

# 31.7.2 Mineral Replacement

Replacement of burnt materials is evidenced in combustion features by calcite recrystallization and secondary phosphatization. Calcite recrystallization occurs under high moisture conditions with neutral to basic pH. Illustrations of different stages of this process are provided in Figure 31.14 (see also Bergadà et al. 2013: Figure 8d; Shahack-Gross et al. 2014: Figure 5d, e). Cementation is evidenced by welding of individual aggregates into a lithified mass (Figure 31.14a, c), while recrystallization results in variable loss of morphological definition of the rhombic aggregates. Although the morphologies of the individual ashes may be lost, overall fabric, including laminations or the presence of woody tissues, is preserved (Figure 31.14d; also Mentzer 2014: Figure 13b).

Secondary phosphatization occurs under high moisture conditions in the presence of a source of phosphorus, such as guano. The process is evidenced by precipitation of phosphatic minerals such as apatite in the form of nodules or crusts (Figure 31.15a and b). More advanced stages of phosphatization may be evidenced by either complete dissolution of ashes (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010: Figure 2) or their volumetric replacement by phosphate minerals, as well as recrystallization of bone inclusions (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010: Figure 1). These types of transformations are best identified using additional analyses, such as micro-FTIR (Figure 31.15d, but see Karkanas 2010 for examples of some minerals in thin section). Charcoal decomposition can occur under high moisture conditions and high pH (Cohen-Ofri et al. 2006). This process has been documented in Holocene agricultural soils and the replacement of charcoal by clay with preservation of the original morphology has been reported (see Huisman et al. 2012: Figure 6).

# 31.7.3 Mineral Precipitation

Secondary mineral formation, which results in material gains to combustion features may also impact their preservation. For example, the precipitation of other

Figure 31.13 Dissolution. (a) A partially decalcified bioturbated deposit contains a variety of burnt materials sourced from ritual fires. In this image, ashes are abundant and locally preserved in a sheltered region between two fragments of limestone (LS) fire-cracked rock. The rock fragments likely physically and chemically protected the ashes from dissolution. Iron Age open hearth, Greece. PPL. (b) Same view as (a), XPL. The sheltered zone between the limestone fragments is clearly visible as an area with higher birefringence. Ashes are present within the sedimentary matrix here, as well as inside of the pores of a fragment of burnt spongy bone (arrow). (c) Detail of the ashes inside the fragment of spongy bone; dark areas are bone (B). XPL. (d) In other portions of the deposit, ashes are entirely absent despite the presence of other burnt residues. Microscopic evidence for decalcification of more resistant materials suggests that ashes are not preserved. Here, a fragment of chert (Ch) exhibits a decalcification rind (arrow). XPL. (e) Siliceous aggregates in a layer of bedding. These materials appear to have formed from phytoliths because their original morphologies are locally preserved, however most of the material visible is amorphous and porous. MSA maintenance fire, South Africa. PPL. (f) Same view as (e), XPL. Siliceous aggregates are isotropic.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/c90692128b050dc5cab898e251cd3e2a27339ae7d488ffd8bf804bfde18ca7af.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3a748cbe65474d728fd685148fddc8697f7f02e25413849770a0903e316ce314.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/32ca4bff5bdce972c957436e8002a2800d119accdeae4cc9d17b30ccb44867f1.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/c1a12f34d58780d3044211623a069ad6d5a6416a4db9d18c320afb362cc465a5.jpg)  
Figure 31.14 Cementation and recrystallization. (a) Recrystallization of ashes along a void forming a hypocoating. Recrystallization results in a decrease in porosity and a loss of individual rhomb morphology. The fresh ashes that comprise the rest of the matrix are visible in the upper right corner. UP open hearth, Turkey. PPL. (b) While fully cemented or recrystallized ashes are nearly impervious to subsequent postdepositional mixing processes, partially cemented deposits, as in (a) can be impacted by bioturbation. MP open hearth, Turkey. PPL. (c) Cemented, but not fully recrystallized ashes contain 'ghosts' of rhombic aggregates (arrow) despite a massive structure. UP open hearth, Turkey. XPL. (d) While recrystallized and cemented ashes do not contain rhombic 'ghosts', other features such as plant tissues and clay intercalations may be visible. When these features are common in better preserved ash layers elsewhere within a site, their presence can help in the identification of massive calcite as former ashes. However, interpretation should proceed cautiously as calcareous plant tissues can form via other pathways (e.g., tufa). MP open hearth, Turkey. XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b77df02efa5e1c5ee660a9ed9662665029403d3e1c4f0cde2f96770c3ad64d93.jpg)

minerals, in particular secondary salts, can cause mechanical fracturing of charcoal (Figure 31.15e and f).

# 31.8 Synthesis

The final step in analysis of combustion features using micromorphology is synthesis of the observations. Figure 31.16 illustrates the synthesis of analyses of a simple open hearth with a prepared burning surface. In this example, the form and pavements were clear in the field (Figure 31.16a), and the context was a domestic interior space; however, other aspects of the function were

unknown. Based on micromorphological analyses of the burnt residues, possible functions and fuel sources were identified (Figure 31.16b,c). In a more complicated sequence (Figure 31.17), additional analytical tools were necessary to reconstruct the life history of the combustion feature. Namely, the microfacies concept (Courty 2001) aided in the classification of different microstratigraphic units by composition, fabric and structure. These characteristics also aided in associating each microfacies type with depositional and postdepositional processes. Furthermore, the same microfacies units, when identified throughout a site, can be used to understand vertical and lateral variation in different, recurring processes,

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/bc0ff020c186df910718909e719b7aa8f26cbcbff0232c7999dd70b24a22ca84.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3098ddc66181692e8bb8b4e6e79daf2c99ffbbb802324fb7d735496c7d5f781c.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/7cf539ebf42955f4a818636e06159a3ffb7b423c9bf0e8c16b108ab89df2e931.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/cdcb747d66ba66afcc83a6e55e9291790209eb8bbce69a2f49abeac550a06ebd.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/79c80bb31a39d323b3b25254d47122b807d76e859346d0050637f807eac33fdc.jpg)  
Figure 31.15 Secondary mineral formation. (a) A layer of ashes contains diffuse zones (arrow) and nodules (N) of yellow secondary phosphate. MSA, South Africa. PPL. (b) Same view as (a), XPL. Certain secondary phosphate minerals are isotropic in XPL and fluoresce under blue light (not pictured). Here, only the nodules are fully isotropic. (c) The upper  $0.5 - 1\mathrm{mm}$  of a layer of ashes fluoresces under blue light. This response can be due to both secondary phosphatization and recrystallization. UP, Turkey. (d) Additional analyses can help in distinguishing between recrystallization and the formation of new minerals. Here,  $\mu$ -FTIR measurements on the materials pictured above indicate the presence of both calcite and apatite (orange spectrum) in the phosphatized ash layer in (a/b), and only calcite (blue spectrum) in (c). (e) A piece of charcoal has been mechanically fragmented in place by the formation of a secondary mineral. MSA, South Africa. PPL. (f) Same view as (e), XPL. The characteristic blady crystal habit and low-order grey interference colours identify this mineral as gypsum.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a494bd9bcb5f8e718d7da6b452d73d0c7d9418d1c4d0511f7cb5fc2cdc3639ec.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/8333e63477e7b844042024788969632024e1cbf661a6051ae30379d3ebbffd9b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/17da34d01767a2c9f3577ef60211b70a7c9a6cd03a7e1efc608d877128ec9c99.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6017fb4f5636b24e68c41509c96ae3932ebc3eae59a0463d47e28361c1e17d40.jpg)  
Figure 31.16 Reconstructing the form and function of a combustion feature. (a) A photograph of a Neolithic stone-paved hearth. The approximate location of a micromorphology sample (outline) is beneath an upper level of paving stones, in an area that appears to be a lower hearth with a concave, possibly paved, substrate. (b) The lower hearth contains inclusions of burnt bone, which suggests that it was used for cooking. In this image, the matrix is composed of wood ashes with rare fragments of charcoal. PPL. (c) Dung was also used as a fuel in this hearth, as evidenced by aggregates of burnt dung. The aggregate pictured here contains both burnt spherulites (round, silt-sized material) and heated, but not fully combusted, calcium oxalate aggregates (arrow). PPL.

including human activities. In this example, ashy sediments are interbedded with red geogenic sediment (Figure 31.17a). The sequence can be divided into units comprising three main microfacies types (Figure 31.17 b-g). Next, the discrete characteristics of each unit can be interpreted in terms of anthropogenic or geogenic primary depositional processes, with variable degrees of secondary cementation and reworking by insects and downslope movement. The final synthesis (Figure 31.17h) reveals that two intact hearths are each overlain by geogenic deposits.

# 31.9 Conclusion

Combustion structures are among the most commonly occurring anthropogenic (and rarely, nonanthropogenic) features in archaeological sites from all regions and almost all time periods. Yet, micromorphological analyses of combustion features have not been conducted on a systematic basis. Most published studies relate to open hearths in Palaeolithic contexts, or experimental and ethnographic studies that are pertinent to their interpretation. Only in Palaeolithic settings are micromorphological analyses considered to be a standard avenue of investigation for combustion features and pyrotechnological behaviour. In contrast, with the exception of fumiers and to some extent, features in Near Eastern tells, there are few systematic studies of combustion features from recent prehistoric or historic periods. This lack of focus is surprising given the common occurrence of kiln remains and the ubiquity of industrial ceramic, metallurgic and glass production reported in the literature. Likewise, experimental replication of pyrotechnologies, such as pottery and glass production and metal smelting, and material analysis of their byproducts are numerous, while the discarded remains of the fire installations themselves receive little interest.

In the future, micromorphological research on combustion structures would benefit from more experimental and ethnoarchaeological work that could provide middle range datasets for interpretation of combustion feature compositions and microfabrics. We suggest expanding upon previous analyses of open hearths and the effects of postdepositional human actions on them (trampling, sweeping, rake out, etc.), as well as other types of combustion structures, including earth ovens and industrial kilns. Ethnoarchaeological research would be particularly useful to reconstruct the impacts of taphonomic agents on combustion features in a wider range of environmental settings. Finally, the microstratigraphic investigation of combustion structures should be carried out using a multitechnique approach.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/4ffb972d0b36c5d12b05a65a5a3f405e65c49373cd2e8be1889f6e3ae74509a8.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/aae4d61dc0d3b787b1cf82db751a135dccfd21e0e4e59be8193e456c481b6d76.jpg)  
Figure 31.17 Combustion feature life histories. (a) A sequence of stacked combustion features (UP, Turkey) present a more complicated formation sequence. Here, the microfacies concept (Courty 2001) was applied in order to relate the formation of these features to other features within the site. The three main microfacies types (MF-I, MF-II, and MF-V) are indicated on the left side of the panoramic composite image of the sequence (subtypes indicated by lowercase letters and numbers relate to fabric/structure and degree of cementation; see Mentzer 2011). Localized domains of additional microfacies subtypes are also labelled. (b) MF-IIa2 is characterized by massive ashes that are variably cemented, yet preserve local rhombic aggregates (arrow), laminations and plant tissues that indicate that they are in place. MF1 is interpreted as the burnt ashy residue layer of an intact open hearth. PPL. (c) The lower unit of MF-IIa2 contains a fragment of bone, which has not been heated above  $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , as indicated by  $\mu$ -FTIR measurements conducted directly on the thin section (inset). XPL. (d) A passage feature (arrow) in the upper unit of MF-IIa2 indicates that the ashes have been impacted by postdepositional bioturbation. The fabric of the layer, but not its overall composition, has been affected. The uppermost portion of the underlying unit of MF-lb2 is visible at the base of the image. This portion of the unit varies in colour, with the uppermost sedimentary aggregates (SA) appearing dark red in PPL. (e) In another area, insect bioturbation has impacted both the fabric and composition, as well as the structure of the layer. PPL. This burrow contains faecal pellets composed of ashes mixed with red clay, as well as rounded aggregates of cemented ashes (inset; XPL). This sediment is classified as MF-IIc2, a bioturbation subfacies with localized cementation. (f) MF-lb2 is characterized by geogenic sediment composed of red clay with inclusions of quartz silt. Cementation by secondary calcite is variable and is expressed either as diffuse matrix impregnation or as void coatings (arrow) and infillings. MF-lb2 is interpreted as the dominant geogenic sedimentation regime in the site. (g) MF-Vd2 is characterized by mixed aggregates of geogenic and anthropogenic sediment, including rounded aggregates of cemented ashes, dark and light red clay aggregates, randomly oriented clay curls (CC), and rounded, sand-sized fragments of unburnt bones (b). MF-Vd2 is interpreted as colluvial material containing geogenic and anthropogenic components in secondary position with variable secondary cementation. (h) The final interpretation of this sample is as a sequence of two intact open hearths interbedded with geogenic materials, including a colluvial deposit. The burnt residues and combustion substrates are overlain on the panoramic image shown in (a). The lower hearth is overlain by a thin geogenic deposit of red clay aggregates that form the combustion substrate for the upper hearth, as evidenced by the darkened aggregates in (d). The upper hearth is impacted by postdepositional bioturbation, and is locally truncated and overlain by a colluvial deposit containing reworked combustion materials. The colluvial deposit is in turn capped by a geogenic layer that has been partially cemented by secondary calcite. The functions of the hearths are unknown, as inclusions within them are rare, save for a single fragment of bone.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a22e2478fdc37e78d55dd220745252f766dd32cb23fa1d1dc8a0ddd5ed893b6e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/de15a93915599a1daf066a6467b5225d5d4f470f52f6debb8ddc429b3650dc7c.jpg)

Integration of micromorphology with other high resolution techniques, including micro-FTIR (Berna & Goldberg 2008; Berna et al. 2012), XRF (Mentzer & Quade 2013), organic petrology (Taylor et al. 1998;

Ligouis 2006) and organic chemistry (Sistiaga et al. 2014) will provide robust datasets that will contribute towards a broader understanding of archaeological combustion features.

# 32

# European Dark Earth

Cristiano Nicosia<sup>1</sup>, Yannick Devos<sup>1</sup>, and Richard I. Macphail<sup>2</sup>

<sup>1</sup> Centre de Recherches en Archéologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Bruxelles, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Institute of Archaeology, University College London, London, United Kingdom

# 32.1 Introduction

The expression 'dark earth' does not correspond to a soil classification name nor should it imply a unequivocal archaeological interpretation. The term refers to darkcoloured, poorly stratified, nonpeaty material containing anthropogenic remains. More specifically, the expression 'dark earth' is often used to address thick units found in Roman or post-Roman urban contexts observed predominantly, if not exclusively, in Europe (on the geographical distribution of dark earth see Nicosia et al. 2013; Borderie et al. 2014). It should not be confused with black chernozemic soils of Central Europe (see for example Gerlach et al. 2006) or with 'Amazonian dark earth' or 'Terra Preta do Indios' (see Arroyo-Kalin 2017, this book; Lehmann et al. 2003). For a detailed discussion on the definition of European 'dark earth' the reader is referred to Macphail (1994), Macphail et al. (2003), Galinié (2004), Devos et al. (2009), Fondrillon (2009), and Nicosia & Devos (2014).

In the soil micromorphological literature, studies concerning European dark earth or dark layers or black soil appeared in Britain since the beginning of the 1980s (Macphail 1981, 1983; Macphail & Courty 1985). In Italy, where the expression terre nere appeared during the 1980s (Brogiolo et al. 1988; Brogiolo 2011), micromorphology has been employed in Ferrara (Cremaschi 1992; Cremaschi & Nicosia 2010), Firenze (Nicosia et al. 2012), and Padova (Nicosia et al. 2017). In France, micromorphological studies on terres noires date back to the early 1990s (Cammas et al. 1995; Cammas 2004); other studies were carried out by Gebhardt (1997), Borderie (2011), Borderie et al. (2014) and Macphail (2014). The earliest studies on dark earth in Belgium have been carried out since 1996, first in the city of Ghent (Ervynck et al. 1999; Stoops et al. 2001) and later on in Brussels (Devos et al. 2007, 2009, 2011a, 2013a, 2013b, 2016). In recent years, a

number of articles based on comparisons between dark earth contexts in different European countries were published (Macphail et al. 2003; Nicosia et al. 2013; Macphail 2014; Nicosia & Devos 2014; Borderie et al. 2014). These publications made large use of soil micromorphology to tackle interpretive problems in dark earth.

# 32.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedures

The micromorphological study of dark earth requires a specific sampling strategy (Figure 32.1). The homogeneous appearance of this type of material implies that most of the internal limits and discontinuities are obscured at the macroscopic scale. Therefore, continuous sampling of the whole sequence is recommended, possibly with partial overlapping of successive samples. Moreover, it is very important to record on the field the nature of the lower boundary of the dark earth, and to sample it alongside the underlying layer(s) or horizon(s). This allows for the comprehension of the type and rate of the processes involved in the initiation of dark earth formation (e.g., weathering of once-stratified archaeological layers such as walls and floors, slow accretion, massive dumping, mixing due to agricultural activities). Samples of the upper boundary will on the other hand reveal some of the postdepositional phenomena (e.g., erosion, truncation, pedogenesis), and possibly last use of space (e.g., herding on dark earth 'soils'; Macphail 2010). Given the marked lateral variability of dark earth, more than one profile should be described and sampled. Bulk samples for physico-chemical, botanical or microarchaeological analyses should be collected in close association with micromorphology samples (Figure 32.1). During sampling, care should be taken to note visibly bioturbated areas (e.g., tree-throws, large biogalleries), recognizable

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/e684ac929e43126c1005d49e624bb564e0730f43853e00e63a091c68967b036c.jpg)  
Figure 32.1 Interdisciplinary field sampling, including pollen/ microfossil sampling in addition to Kubiana box and column for soil micromorphology (Roman amphitheatre - London Guildhall Yard East - see Macphail et al. 2008). Three arena floor/dark earth locations were sampled and studied in detail. Bulk chemical samples were subsampled from the monoliths in order to allow exact correlation between samples. Here, samples were taken through arena sands, the junction between the last constructional phase (mortar floor) and the earliest dark earth, and through late Roman dark earth up into the early medieval dark earth.

negative structures (e.g., pits, burials, robber trenches) and structures, such as walls, 'floating' in dark earth. All these associated contexts provide information on the continuity of landscape and use of space. 'Floating' archaeology, for example surfaces, may well provide insights into land use associated with dark earth formation, and should not be ignored (Galinié et al. 2007; Borderie et al. 2014). The sampling strategy and research questions should be decided together with the field archaeologists. In fact, they provide crucial contextual data and can direct the micromorphologist towards the most relevant or promising areas of the excavation.

The first step in dark earth thin section analysis is the identification of most evident differences and boundaries within each thin section set. Secondly, full standard descriptions according to reference terminologies (for instance Stoops 2003 or Bullock et al. 1985) should be made, the data being eventually organized into soil

microfabric types (Macphail & Cruise 2001; Cremaschi & Nicosia 2010). Thin section counting (e.g. visual estimate, point, single item or areal counting) can provide useful information. It should be taken into account that the interpretation should be based on the covariation in quantity of a set of different features and/or inclusions down the sequence, rather than just the variation of one single feature/inclusion.

# 32.3 Features Associated with Natural Formation Processes

Dark earth is the outcome of the interplay between human activities and natural syn- and postdepositional processes. These will be outlined below.

# 32.3.1 Features Associated with Secondary Mineral Formation

Pedofeatures observed in dark earth are mostly the outcome of the formation of carbonates, Fe/Mn (hydr) oxides and/or phosphates. The most common carbonate pedofeatures are typic calcite nodules and hypocoatings, the calcite deriving from the natural parent material (e.g. calcareous alluvium) or from dissolution of ashes, plaster or mortar (see Figure 32.2). In contexts where calcium carbonate cannot derive from the natural substratum, such neoformed minerals inform us about the disappearance of calcareous components formerly present in the deposit. This ultimately gives us information on the soil environment (e.g. water regime, acid or alkaline conditions). In the same way, Fe/Mn nodules, impregnations of the groundmass, hypo- and quasicoatings inform us

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/4c0fee3e59ad02eed335af9faba71b452bb08a19a2bbf73f605bee2a0e09be21.jpg)  
Figure 32.2 Partially weathered mortar fragment. Note the locally darker aspect of calcite in the binding agent. Poor Clares, Brussels, Belgium. XPL.

about the environmental conditions during or after dark earth formation. Moreover, Fe/Mn pseudomorphs of roots or other vegetal tissues and the staining of organic matter provide information on the organic matter status.

Secondary phosphates (see Figure 32.3a-e) commonly occur as crystal intergrowths, nodules, infillings of vivianite (indicator of waterlogged conditions), Ca or Ca-Fe phosphates. The formation of these pedofeatures is linked to the availability of phosphorus from the decomposition of plant materials, excrements, urine, ashes, bones or fish bones, or from phosphorus naturally present in the local soils.

Under certain circumstances, pedofeatures such as carbonate or phosphate nodules can be identified as being either in situ reworked by bioturbation or digging or additions associated with dumping or nightsoiling, for example. The input of new material into an accumulating dark earth deposit can also be recognized. For example, the addition of fresh/unweathered waste may be indicated by renewed earthworm activity evidenced by the presence of 'fresh' earthworm granules (compared to weathered ones), and better preserved and more diverse pollen types (compared to poorly preserved ruderal pollen typical of dark earth) (Scaife 1980; Greig 1982; Macphail & Cruise 1993; Macphail 2010). Where intensive occupation continues after the formation of dark earth, phosphate contamination may for example occur because of leakage from cesspits (Macphail & Linderholm 2004) or even from modern septic tanks. In such cases, an understanding of the archaeological context is crucial along with lateral controls.

# 32.3.2 Features Deriving from

# Biological Activities

One of the most characteristic features of dark earth is the high degree of bioturbation observable in thin section. Such process can be inferred from the thoroughly reworked fabric deriving from the development of root systems and/or from the activity of soil fauna such as moles, rodents, earthworms, ants and enchytraeids (Figure 32.4a and b). Specific traces are the formation of typical microstructures (e.g., basic ones given by organomineral excremental microaggregates, channel, granular or crumby microstructures), the presence of inorganic inclusions of biogenic origin (earthworm granules, slug plates) and of passage features (bowlike features, excrement infillings) (see Kooistra & Pulleman 2010). The activity of roots and soil fauna also results in the masking of the original stratification, by erasing boundaries and sedimentary structures and causing the random distribution of natural and anthropogenic components. Fragmentation of different eco- and artefacts is another byproduct of biological activity in the soil, especially

visible in the large quantity of finely comminuted charcoal, charred vegetal fragments (Figure 32.4a, b), and phytoliths (Figure 32.4c) dispersed in the groundmass of dark earth.

# 32.3.3 Weathering of Calcareous Materials (According to the Para-Rendzina Model of Macphail 1994)

According to the general explanatory model proposed by Macphail (Macphail 1994; Macphail et al. 2003), dark earth can be the outcome of the pedogenetic transformations acting on originally well-stratified urban archaeological deposits. The result is a 'soil' with characteristics similar to natural soils classified as calcareous Brown Earths or Para-Rendzinas (Duchaufour 1983), with the difference that in dark earth the calcareous parent material corresponds to mortar and plaster fragments derived from destruction, robbing and decay of buildings and monuments. As dark earth soil 'matures', humic and carbonic acids may partially decalcify biogenic calcite: such weathering features indicate the development of a stable land surface (see section 32.3.5). In this scenario, earthworm granules can display signs of being weakly weathered, showing a 'darkening' of their colours under PPL and the corrosion of their outer boundaries (Courty, et al. 1989). In the same way, the poor preservation of pollen grains, due to oxidation and biological activity can be used to detect weathering processes acting on dark earth (see Macphail 1981; Macphail & Cruise 1993).

# 32.3.4 Weathering of Earth-Based Construction Materials

Dark earth can form from the breakdown of earth-based building 'clay' or 'loam', such as loess (silt loam) and brickearth (fine sandy silt loam - see Goldberg & Macphail 2006), and from the decalcification of sand-tempered lime plaster and stone-tempered mortar (see Figure 32.2). Weathering can thus release clay, silt, sand, and gravel into dark earth, alongside calcium carbonate (often redeposited as hypocoatings down profile, for example; see Macphail & Courty 1985; Macphail 1994). Building debris can also contribute to the distributions of small artefact within dark earth, as revealed through microarchaeological analyses (Sidell 2000; Fondrillon 2009).

# 32.3.5 Soil Formation on Grassland, Pasture and Abandoned Areas

Part of the dark earth results from soil formation on grassland, pasture or abandoned areas in urban or proto-urban contexts. Typical features are enhanced organic matter, biogenic porosity and earthworm granules.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/364a42bf47f23f3ea4f9e64285a09941180358ca6b48568ba65d810fa8d7bfc8.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/193bd24446bfa20212d265f7b8ca3bfb5a035efa0e8ab41f0fd761c4aa672119.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/2bca230c3083ce29c9114103300cb3050fc98aa34cda2524449a8e20beafa5fa.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/8c1c62a00315313059a6175bead3beea80c7fc43c7714d8e153efad5bbf19a05.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a1b20a9ffc97965bfede9ef4ff7cc96071cbda985ca4e26cc6867f86c7af4903.jpg)  
Figure 32.3 (a) Heterogeneous dark earth of rapid dump/middening origin, composed of quartz and glauconitic sands, anthropogenic inclusions of very fine and coarse charcoal, strongly burned (fused) sand (arrow) and dung traces (e.g., dark brownish area in centre). Yellowish brown staining (see (b)) and some void coatings are due to iron phosphate contamination (see (c)) from ensuing occupation and latrine waste disposal. Pevensey Castle, East Sussex, UK. PPL. (b) As (a), in XPL. Note decreased brightness where there is iron phosphate staining and humification of dung residues. (c) X-ray microprobe combined Si-Fe-P element map of an area of an uncovered thin section. Example of iron phosphate contaminated early medieval dark earth. Generally, iron-stained sands are green, while iron phosphate void infillings are purple, and Fe-P matrix staining and hypocoatings are whitish purple. (d) Typic phosphate (vivianite) nodule. Note the dotted appearance of the fine material  $(< 20\mu m)$  due to the presence of humus and microcharcoal fragments. PPL. (e) Calcareous orthic typic nodule (A) surrounded by thin orange (PPL) limpid discontinuous coating (B). SEM/EDS measurements (see diagram) revealed that area B is composed mainly of Fe  $(41.8\%)$ , P  $(22.8\%)$ , and Ca  $(17.7\%)$ . Uffizi gallery complex, Florence, Italy (see Nicosia et al. 2012). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/76549efd0ec519e9917fe02f30217c248521a0c4d90615b7a524daa27107672d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d451392387272abaa59d2d63cfecf36ac13e3e9f6271a0b8fce91ec83a22d49e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d199562420ef62f572b99e015be2383587931c2bf204a4b37e6755fbc3b019b2.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ad35ae66eed3d2e3be79265c8f148c5d97ee19fbec577205b87f15e8d5835952.jpg)  
Figure 32.4 (a) Fill of a small mammal burrow discernible in dark earth deposits. Note the fragmentation and chaotic distribution of charcoal and charred vegetal fragments, a typical micromorphological characteristic of dark earth. Uffizi gallery complex, Florence, Italy (see Nicosia et al. 2012). PPL. (b) Organo-mineral excremental aggregates pointing to mesofaunal bioturbation of the horizon. In the centre a coprolite can be observed. Note also the presence of a bone fragment in the right upper part. The horizon has been identified as being the result of dumping of building detritus, domestic and construction waste. The homogeneous character of the horizon is mainly due to bioturbation by mesofauna. Poor Clares, Brussels, Belgium. PPL. (c) Randomly distributed and unoriented microcharcoal fragments and phytoliths (arrows), pointing to the highly disturbed character of dark earth. Note also the dotted appearance of the fine material  $(<10\mu m)$  due to the presence of humus and fine charcoal. Poor Clares, Brussels, Belgium. PPL.

The latter can show traces of weathering and partial dissolution, which might be used as indicators of decalcification processes (see above). Phytoliths are mostly isolated or locally clustered (Figure 32.5), showing no preferential orientation and belonging mostly to the Pooidea grass subfamily (Macphail 1981; Devos et al., 2009, 2013a, 2013b). Pollen may also indicate pasture (Greig 1982, 2004). Compared to the substratum, only a slight accumulation of anthropogenic material may be noted. Stock concentrations have been indicated by the presence of herbivore dung (e.g. No. 1 Poultry and the Guildhall, London – Macphail et al. 2008; Figure 32.6). However, robust identification often requires the

combination of micromorphological data with physicochemical and microfossil (phytoliths, pollen) analyses (Macphail & Linderholm 2004; Devos et al. 2009, 2013a).

# 32.3.6 Natural Sedimentation and Erosion in Dark Earth Sequences

On some sites dark earth formation has lasted for several centuries (see for example Fedi et al. 2007; Cantini et al. 2008; Nicosia et al. 2012). Hence, in sites located within active alluvial settings, it is likely that alluvium became incorporated and homogenized within dark earth. Such mechanism has been identified for example in the site of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/dee09986306693798b5f9f2bd5374d528385b6b7f1bb39b2f61f95650de737d3.jpg)  
Figure 32.5 Cluster of dendritic phytoliths, typical for crops. The phytoliths are not in anatomical position, pointing to perturbation. Hôtel d'Hoogstraeten, Brussels, Belgium (see Devos et al. 2013a). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ad5c024f1595a92fdfd587605dd53e3b6952951c95d97c197a67b43453b39e18.jpg)  
Figure 32.6 Junction between Roman / post-Roman dark earth and early medieval deposits testifying to the reoccupation of the Roman amphitheatre – London Guildhall Yard East area (see Macphail et al. 2008). Dung (D) from animal pounding within the relict arena area has been burrowed down into the underlying more minerogenic dark earth ‘soil’. The ‘new’ dung (and phosphate)-enriched soil records a wider and better preserved pollen spectra (including wetland species), compared to the underlying dark earth. PPL.

Corso Porta Reno in Ferrara (northern Italy - see Cremaschi & Nicosia 2010). Here, an alternating sequence of dark-coloured occupation deposits and cm-thick alluvium layers was not homogenized by bioturbation and anthropic activities, thus not ultimately resulting in dark earth. This was due to persisting waterlogged conditions and fast sedimentary accretion. The presence of 'flood coatings' (sensu Brammer 1971; Figure 32.7) can be regarded as another indicator of flooding during dark earth formation (as for example in

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a9373529d2413273d7720ae9ae03e0e81fc8364925abac7eb7b557a3d0e24b2e.jpg)  
Figure 32.7 Compound layered link capping ('flood coating' sensu Brammer 1971) on a weathered mortar fragment at the base of a dark-earth deposit. This pedofeature suggests periodic flooding during dark earth formation. Uffizi gallery complex, Florence, Italy (see Nicosia et al. 2012). XPL.

the site of the Uffizi gallery complex in Florence, Italy - see Nicosia et al. 2012). The decayed earth-based constructions of shortlived (ca. 100 years) late Antique Tarquimpol, France, were sealed by colluvium from the eroded surrounding ramparts (Macphail 2014).

Micromorphology alone does not always permit us to identify the natural input of new sediments (alluvium, colluvium or aeolian input) but it could be effectively coupled with particle size distribution index analysis (Langohr et al. 1976; Devos et al. 2009, 2013a, 2013b). In Macphail (2003) grain-size analysis was employed to distinguish inputs from weathering of building materials versus alluvium. Erosion, as well as truncation, can be hardly discerned relying solely on thin section analysis. It can be sometimes detected on the field when abrupt boundaries (e.g. in dark earth overlain by alluvium or by later construction units) are visible or when a 'gap' is revealed by high-resolution  $^{14}\mathrm{C}$  dating along the sequence (see for example Fedi et al. 2007; Nicosia et al. 2012).

# 32.4 Features Associated with Human Activities

# 32.4.1 Dumping In, and From, Different Activity Areas (e.g., Domestic House Sweepings, Industrial Residues, Latrine Waste)

Dumping is an activity commonly identified within dark earth. The latter has in fact often developed from midden deposits, for example in open areas or within abandoned house shells (see for example Macphail 1994;

Table 32.1 Typology and origin of dumped material commonly found in dark earth (based on Macphail 1994; Fondrillon 2007; Devos et al. 2011b).  

<table><tr><td>Origin of the dumped material</td><td>Characteristics</td></tr><tr><td>Domestic waste</td><td>Mixture of ash, charcoal, charred seeds, burnt and unburnt bone, eggshell, ceramics, normal and vitrified phytoliths</td></tr><tr><td>Cess/latrine waste</td><td>Carnivore/omnivore coprolites, sometimes mixed with ash (as in ‘nightsoiling’), parasite eggs, reworked aggregates of underlying soil, seeds (mineralized), Fe-stained vegetal residues, phosphatic nodules, crystal intergrowths and crusts; Ca-P typifies human cess, Ca-Fe/Mn-P has a broader faecal origin</td></tr><tr><td>Construction/destruction waste</td><td>Mortar and mortar-making byproducts, plaster, earth-based construction materials, brick and tile, stone, charcoal, turf, sediments (e.g. for ground raising)</td></tr><tr><td>Industrial/artisan waste (involving hearths and furnaces)</td><td>Metal and fuel-ash slags, vitrified ceramics, metal fragments and metal oxidation products (e.g., lead/lead oxide/lead carbonate), charcoal, coal, ash, burned and vitrified soil fragments, vitrified phytoliths, glass; under hydromorphic conditions iron slags can develop into amorphous iron (hydr)oxide nodules</td></tr><tr><td>Artisan waste (butchery, leather working etc.)</td><td>Leather, bone, phosphate nodules</td></tr><tr><td>Animal waste and manure</td><td>Mineralized carnivore (dog) and omnivore (human) coprolites; dominantly organic omnivore (pig) and herbivore (cattle, sheep/goat, horse) coprolites and associated stabling waste; domestic and cess/latrine waste/night-soil (see above).</td></tr></table>

Cammas 2000; Fondrillon 2007). Micromorphology can contribute to determining the origins of dumped materials (see Table 32.1). Domestic waste is generally the outcome of activities such as house sweeping, hearth functioning and maintenance, and food preparation. The maintenance and periodic emptying of cesspits and latrines implies the release of large quantities of phosphate-rich material, which can be used in conjunction with domestic waste for manuring purposes (see section 32.4.2). Construction and/or destruction rubble is often found in areas subjected to squatting or to the reuse of materials from pre-existing buildings. However, the presence of construction/destruction debris in dark earth is often due to the disintegration of derelict buildings. The waste resulting from metal production and working contains materials heated at high temperatures, generally higher than those encountered in domestic contexts (see Berna et al. 2007; Angelini et al. 2017, this book). Butchery and leather-working waste have been reported in Stoops et al. (2001) from the dark earth in the centre of Ghent, Belgium, and is a typical component at the London Guildhall (Macphail et al. 2008). Ubiquitous latrine and butchery waste dumping can be a response to the breakdown of urban regulations (Macphail 2010).

# 32.4.2 Cultivation and Manuring

In general, the micromorphological features on which cultivation in dark earth contexts is inferred are the same used in other ancient agricultural soils (see Deák et al. 2017, this book; Figure 32.8a and b). Cultivation is one of

the mechanisms that can aid the transformation of evident archaeological stratigraphy into dark earth, for example through horticulture after the Boudiccan rebellion (AD 60-61) at London and Colchester (Macphail 1994). Here, this was through horticultural 'double digging' where cultivation trenches were dug into destruction debris and mixed with dung. It is also the case that early medieval cultivation within once-urban areas can be recognized spatially alongside occupation, developing into viticulture. This is the case at St. Julien, Tours (eleventh century), where a homogeneous, totally bioturbated microfabric developed (Fondrillon 2007, 2009; Galinie et al. 2007). In England, examples of urban cultivation (Canterbury, Leicester) tend to be medieval in date, and soils, either homogenized by gardening or ploughed (major mixing and textural pedofeature formation), show far higher manuring inputs compared to Roman cultivated soils. They also record far higher levels of organic matter (determined by LOI), phosphate, and enhanced magnetic susceptibility resulting from the additions of domestic, latrine, and burnt waste (Goldberg & Macphail 2006, Table 9.1a; Macphail & Crowther 2007a, 2007b).

In Brussels, the identification of agricultural activities relies on the observation of traces witnessing thorough reworking of a topsoil (e.g., textural pedofeatures, absence of laminations - Figure 32.8a and b, highly fragmented, randomly distributed and unoriented anthropogenic inclusions - Figure 32.9). The phytolith study carried out directly on the thin sections (see Vrydaghs et al. 2017, this book) permitted the identification of several crop markers. Evidence for manuring is given by

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a1942b292bc65f72aefc776cce7635ff381a047d7c3d18600a7b7b0dab711136.jpg)  
Figure 32.8 (a) Layered clay coating composed of brown dusty clay and silt-sized quartz. In combination with a random distribution of highly fragmented inclusions, this kind of pedofeature points towards severe mixing / reworking. PPL. (b) Dusty clay coating (C) and infilling (I). These textural pedofeatures witness soil loosening and disturbance in a dark earth interpreted as a former urban garden soil. Hôtel d'Hoogstraeten, Brussels, Belgium (see Devos et al. 2013a). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b0111142043c588c92cdf0e253582c4e139b97bf54f4a2e7c67830026ea88034.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/1c65d0f1cde2c23f6373154ff89bcb203cefd99ada1462bed6e606e642717d96.jpg)  
Figure 32.9 Intrusive soil fragment pointing to mixing of dark earth. Poor Clares, Brussels, Belgium. PPL.

the presence of anthropogenic materials (e.g. faecal material such as latrine waste and coprolites, charcoal, pottery - Figure 32.10a, b and c). This is confirmed by chemical data showing enhanced values of phosphorus, organic matter, and of exchangeable basic cations (Devos et al. 2009, 2011a, 2011b, 2013a).

# 32.4.3 Other Human Activities

Beside the activities described above, there are others that characterize the urban archaeological record and should be kept into account when dealing with

formation processes of dark earth. These correspond for example to soil extraction or quarrying, to the excavation of wall foundations, post holes, beam slots, robber trenches, pits, graves etc. (see Carver 1987). All of the above mentioned activities result in the mechanical reworking and homogenization of the deposit, but their visibility is often very limited in the field. For example, negative structures filled with dark earth-like material can be visible when they cut through underlying layers or the natural substratum, their upper boundaries 'disappearing' into the dark earth layer. In the same way, clusters of archaeological materials (e.g. potsherds, slags, brick fragments) or even human remains often appear to 'float' in dark earth, without a clearly identifiable negative structure boundary. The presence of graves in once-urban space is another clue to indicate the breakdown in urban regulations. Such Late Roman burials were recorded at the Courage Brewery site, Southwark, London and Deansway, Worcester (Cowan 2003; Dalwood & Edwards 2004).

Thin sections can be of little help in distinguishing between these different activities resulting in dark earth reworking and homogenization. In general, the backfill of these structures can present a loose, well-separated microstructure, reflecting the dumping process. The latter is also testified by the juxtaposition of reworked aggregates with different origins (e.g. natural substratum, dark earth-like materials, other stratigraphic units). The presence of traces of bioturbation suggests that soil fauna and roots preferentially drill through the loose backfill.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/1461ba371869d6bc51addf6ab3ded089d52b637abcc430c99b5819a4f056a722.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/73d7d5ed2d493355b23cf73b1a97d2aa4d078c61de00846a623e129a4b465f6f.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a8094c857cb433a4948c71e4950c033bc281652933e788fb2981bf7e9589271b.jpg)  
Figure 32.10 (a) Coprolite fragment. The presence of abundant faecal material, in combination with highly fragmented and randomly distributed anthropogenic components (pottery, charcoal, etc.) points to manuring of the soil. Poor Clares, Brussels, Belgium. PPL. (b) Phosphatic aggregate incorporating (burnt?) eggshell (arrow) and charcoal fragments. Interpreted as a proof of manuring by means of a mixture of excrements and domestic residues (e.g., 'nightsoiling') in a dark earth. Note thoroughly bioturbated (excremental) fabric. Metz, Lorraine, France. PPL. (c) Phosphatic nodule with partly oxidized vivianite and Fe/Mn neoformations, possibly deriving from cess / latrine waste. Phytoliths, nematode eggs and weathered bone fragments are often observed inside such nodules. Example from dark earth interpreted as the result of urban horticulture with manuring practices. Metz, Lorraine, France. PPL.

# 32.5 Concluding Remarks

The lack of internal stratification of dark earth at the macroscale is particularly problematic for urban archaeologists since it clashes with the concept of 'one stratigraphic unit equals one action' (Harris 1989). However, soil micromorphology has proven that, in many cases, dark earth represents a palimpsest of different activities and natural processes, which can still be identified despite dark earth often being subjected to thorough homogenization. Although soil micromorphology has

proven a powerful tool in elucidating the use of space and the cultural activities associated with dark earth formation, its combination with other analytical methods is advocated. Specifically, thin-section study can be integrated with soil physico-chemical analyses, instrumental analyses (e.g. electron microprobe, SEM/EDS - see Wilson 2017, this book), phytolith/pollen analysis, microarchaeology, and spatial analysis of artefact distribution. Taking into account the complexity of dark earth, its study requires a systematic analytical approach. Beginning with appropriate research questions, it

encompasses quality sampling, knowledge of natural processes acting in these specific contexts, and the application of solid interpretive models drawn from archaeological excavations.

On the whole, the application of the analytical methods of earth sciences to European dark earths contributed to shed light on deposits interpreted as the result of generic 'abandonment' of cities, or that were interpreted as gardens, backfill units, or even as alluvium (see Yule 1990; Macphail et al. 2003; Brogiolo 2011). Thirty years of such analyses allowed us to understand that in many cases, dark earths are rather the outcome of a continuity of use of the urban space. This continuity is marked by markedly different, more distinctly 'rural' conditions, with respect to the previous times. This evidence provides important insights in the framework of the debate on the

continuity of use in postclassical urban or proto-urban centres across Europe.

Micromorphology and the other analytical tools of earth sciences clarified also the role of natural sedimentation in the accretion of urban stratigraphy and in the formation of dark earth. This is of particular significance for cities located in alluvial environments (see Heimdahl 2005; Cremaschi & Nicosia 2010; Nicosia et al. 2012), and suggests the need for a broader, regional or supraregional perspective when approaching dark earths. Combining different diagnostic features with different analytical techniques, and at different scales (i.e. from regional to microscopic) can also help overcome the issue of the equifinality of many micromorphological features. Equifinality hampers robust interpretation, as it implies that the same feature can arise as a result of quite different processes and activities.
