33

# Amazonian Dark Earths

Manuel Arroyo-Kalin

Institute of Archaeology, University College London, London, United Kingdom

# 33.1 Introduction

Amazonian dark earths (ADEs) are expanses of pre-Columbian anthropic soils mostly reported at, or close to, the land surface of Tertiary Age, nonflooding terrain of eastern and southern Amazonia (Smith 1980; WinklerPrins & Aldrich 2010; Arroyo-Kalin 2014a). They were first recorded by nineteenth-century scientific explorers and naturalists, who noted the dark colour of their topsoil and the presence of abundant archaeological pottery shards on their surface (Myers et al. 2003). Amazonian dark earth expanses, which occur in regions with potential or actual vegetation ranging from woody savannah to dense aseasonal rainforest, range from  $< 1$  to  $80\mathrm{ha}$  in surface area and are today frequently used for crop cultivation (Glaser et al. 2001). They form on different soil types and, in general, show higher retention of organic matter, higher black carbon contents, more neutral pH and a higher nutrient status compared to the soil mantles on which they have formed (Lehmann et al. 2003; Glaser & Woods 2004; Woods et al. 2009; Glaser & Birk 2011). Similar anthropic soils have been reported in neighbouring regions beyond the Amazon basin, such as the Orinoco basin and the Guyanas (Vacher et al. 1998; Versteeg 2003; Ochoa 2010; Brancier et al. 2014).

Amazonian dark earths are best understood as the outcome of pedogenetic processes conditioned by land surfaces that have been enriched and transformed by past inhabitation practices (Arroyo-Kalin 2014a). This is borne out by archaeological research, which shows that most ADE expanses are large, open-air archaeological sites marking the location of large pre-Columbian settlements (Nimuendajú 1949; Hilbert 1968; Eden et al. 1984; Heckenberger et al. 1999; Kern et al. 2004; Arroyo-Kalin 2010a; Neves 2013; Schmidt et al. 2014). Recent discussion has emphasized the importance of the broad distribution of ADEs as a proxy for significant pre-Columbian

environmental impact on the Amazon basin (Denevan 2001; WinklerPrins & Aldrich 2010; Arroyo-Kalin 2012; Schmidt et al. 2014; McMichael et al. 2014). In these assessments, as well as other studies emphasizing their fertility for crop cultivation (e.g. Glaser et al. 2001; Steiner et al. 2007), the rubric ADE generally refers to terras pretas de indio ('[Amer]indian dark earths'). The latter are the paradigmatic dark, thick, fertile anthropic soil expanses with abundant embedded pottery fragments mentioned in the opening paragraph (Figures 33.1, 33.2 and 33.3). However, under the rubric of Amazonian dark earths we may also include other anthropic soils reported within Amazonia and in neighbouring lowland regions, for instance artefact-rich anthropic brown earths lacking the distinctively dark colour of terras pretas (e.g. Andrade 1986; Mora et al. 1990; Myers 2004; Schaan et al. 2009; Walker 2011; Brancier et al. 2014; Arroyo-Kalin 2014b) and thick anthropic brown soils that lack archaeological remains yet show physicochemical parameters intermediate between nearby terras pretas and local soils. The latter are also known as terras mulatas (Sombroek 1966; Woods 2002; Arroyo-Kalin 2010a). Systematic taxonomic proposals to classify ADEs have been advanced by soil scientists (Kampf et al. 2003, 2009). Other students argue different types of ADE constitute a broad continuum (Fraser et al. 2011).

A number of models have been advanced to explain the formation of ADEs. The most widely cited is the kitchen midden model (Sombroek et al. 2002), which argues that terra preta soils – that is, dark earths associated with abundant settlement remains – are the result of the decomposition of food remains, human excrements, household rubbish and combustion residues accumulated in pre-Columbian refuse dumps. A second model is known as the house garden model. It suggests that some of the same inputs would have accumulated in middens (where, in some cases, they would have been

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/5f2a4a4cf5a12e34dcd83b3edbb660c022f253de656a5ad48efee96151e3ed45.jpg)  
Figure 33.1 An ADE profile exposed by archaeological excavations at the Hatahara site by the Central Amazon Project in the Brazilian state of Amazonas (see Neves 2013). Below roots immediately under the exposed surface, ADE deposits show highly fragmented pottery and rubified clay fragments. They overlie a thick, partly melanized, buried A horizon with large bedded fragments of pottery (middle left of section) that suggest a pit. Stratigraphic distinctions are rendered invisible by strong melanization. Krotovina-like faunal burrows and root canals in the B horizon are infilled with sediments that originate higher in the profile. Ranging rod is  $1\mathrm{m}$  wide.

burnt) before being used as amendments for different types of gardens and orchards (WinklerPrins 2002; Hecht 2003; Myers 2004; Schmidt et al. 2014). A third hypothesis is the house model, which suggests that large and long-lived longhouses would have acted as efficient sediment traps to concentrate ash, small-sized charcoal, small-sized food waste, organic matter and soot accumulated on the underside of organic roof thatch. In this scenario human trampling would have helped to embed inclusions into stable dry surfaces prior to their reworking by soil fauna after human abandonment (Arroyo-Kalin 2008). A final model – the outfields or intensive cultivation model – is specific to terras mulatas. It suggests that expanses of ADEs located around and away

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/50aa4ee605f04b9999bbc29eb24891b9ee265ded90abcc59a8754ffc1e511c64.jpg)  
Figure 33.2 A surviving monolith showing a buried sandy ADE deposit at the Nova Cidade site, Amazonas, Brazil (see Arroyo-Kalin 2008). The lighter coloured A horizon at the land surface buries an ADE deposit that, in turn, buries a former land surface characterized by an Ab horizon. Measuring tape is  $200\mathrm{cm}$  long.

from pre-Columbian settlements would have resulted from clearance and cultivation practices variously based on the intensive use of fire and and/or additions of organic amendments (Sombroek 1966; Denevan 2004; Arroyo-Kalin 2010a).

Detailed archaeological studies emphasize that individual ADE expanses display significant horizontal and vertical variability (Costa & Kern 1999; Rebellato et al. 2009; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Costa et al. 2013; Soderström et al. 2013), often reflecting sequential occupations by different groups of people in pre-Columbian times (Arroyo-Kalin 2010a). Hence geoarchaeological and, specifically, soil micromorphological studies of ADEs, need to take into consideration a variety of processes taking place between past human practices and the formation of these resiliently enriched soil expanses (Woods 1995; Vacher et al. 1998; Kern et al. 2004; Erickson 2003; Arroyo-Kalin 2014a). Included here is the fact that past peoples prepared places through clearance of vegetation and topsoil exposure; that they conducted spatially patterned practices that led to the deposition

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6f67d5b3cd7d51ca9a73bfd652f54eb8e731ba5b5f32bcb7be9651a8d8fc29a9.jpg)  
Figure 33.3 An ADE profile exposed by excavations through a mound at the Hatahara site (depth  $230\mathrm{cm}$ , width  $100\mathrm{cm}$ ). The position of undisturbed blocks highlights the rationale employed in the sampling for micromorphological study. A subangular blocky structure has developed on the artefact-rich, strongly melanized A horizon, which overlies a well-stratified archaeological deposit showing bedded fragments of pottery. A large archaeological pit feature (marked with dashed lines) with abundant fragments of pottery cannot be distinguished from sediments at the same depth using sediment characteristics. However, microscopic charcoal from the feature dates to the third and fourth centuries BC whilst charcoal from underlying buried A and AB horizons (Ab, ABb) dates from the eighth and tenth centuries AD (see Arroyo-Kalin 2012).

and / or in situ alteration of debris and waste (as suggested by the four models previously described) and that they removed and relocated sediments for different purposes (e.g. post holes, rubbish pits, garden composts, platform building, planting and others) and, in some cases, affected sediments enriched by previous occupations. The broad outcome of this earth altering dynamic needs to be considered in the context of soil forming and sedimentary processes, including the role of soil fauna (burying past occupation surfaces, reworking originally stratified deposits and helping to comminate and degrade debris and waste), phyto-stabilization afforded by vegetation regrowth following site abandonment (which affected the great majority of ADE sites), changes to soil chemistry that would enhance or reduce the archaeological preservation potential of different areas within a given anthropic

soil expanse (Kern et al. 2004) and the effects of surface runoff and tree uprooting, especially in reshaping land surfaces with originally higher anthropogenic relief into horizontally more spread out mantles of enriched sediments. Hence, geoarchaeological approaches employing micromorphological observations to interrogate the properties of specific ADE expanses need to keep in mind that these soils are an outcome of soil formation on palimpsests of past human occupations.

# 33.2 Sampling Considerations

In the field, the first salient characteristic of Amazonian dark earths is strong melanization of the topsoil (Figures 33.1, 33.2 and 33.3). For instance, vertical exposures show that artefact-rich terra preta soils found in the vicinity of Manaus, Brazil, are often expressed as a dark-coloured, organic-rich, surface mantle that is tens of centimetres thick. Usually the latter will grade diffusely, through a transitional horizon, to underlying lighter coloured, organic-poor sediments that may show faunal burrows and root canals infilled with strongly melanized sediments. This description holds irrespective of whether the prevailing soil mantle is sandy or clayey textured, which, however, will determine a soil structure ranging from massive to weakly subangular blocky (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009). When not buried by additional sedimentation (thus becoming a buried horizon), this alternation of distinct layers (used here in the sense of Phillips & Lorz 2008) maps onto an A-AB-B horizon sequence as described by soil scientists. However, despite being obscured by characteristic strong melanization, the 'A horizon' can show indications of bedding that include changes in compaction, the presence of archaeological features, archaeological artefacts marking former surfaces and - in floodplain contexts - alluvial sedimentary inputs (see also Nicosia et al. 2017, this book). The 'A horizon' moreover tends to thin out at the scale of  $10 - 100\mathrm{s}$  of metres. Differences in horizons and subhorizons recorded by soil scientists (Lima et al. 2002; Kampf et al. 2009; Souza 2011; Macedo et al. 2013), therefore, are sometimes differences in archaeological stratigraphy that can be ascertained through a combination of geoarchaeological observations and supplementary archaeological evidence (Arroyo-Kalin 2009, 2014b). This strongly encourages a 'systematic' (continuous) rather than a 'selective' sampling strategy (in the sense of Courty et al. 1989) for soil micromorphological research, with supplementary blocks potentially used to sample sharp boundaries between layers, pit fills and any suspect evidence of relict stratification (Figure 33.3).

# 33.3 Soil Micromorphology of ADEs

A small number of soil micromorphological studies have been conducted on pre-Columbian anthropic soil expanses from the Brazilian Amazon (Creutzberg 2002; Lima et al. 2002; Schaefer et al. 2004; Arroyo-Kalin 2009; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Souza 2011; Macedo et al. 2013), French Guiana (Brancier et al. 2014), the Ecuadorian Amazon and the lower Orinoco region (Arroyo-Kalin 2014b). Aside from one micromorphological study of terras mulatas (Arroyo-Kalin 2009, 2010a, 2012), the majority of these studies focus on dark, artefact-rich terras pretas. Most of these studies have also carried out physical and chemical analyses on bulk samples and/or relied on elemental maps of thin sections produced through electron microprobe and SEM/EDS (Lima 2001; Schaefer et al. 2004; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Schaan et al. 2009; Souza 2011).

Except where inputs of alluvial sediments directly affect the locale where an ADE expanse has formed (see below), soil micromorphological studies suggest that thick melanized ADE layers are not the result of additions of mineral sediments of a different geological origin. Microscopic observations using different light sources instead suggest that most ADE expanses mimic quite closely the texture, c/f related distribution and porosity patterns of their respective background soils, which – as noted previously – are diverse (Lima et al. 2002; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Souza 2011; Macedo et al. 2013). As regards texture, thin sections of 'background' Oxisols highlight a bimodal particle size distribution whilst anthropic soils formed on these substrates show abundant particulate inclusions of anthropic origin (discussed below) that bulk up the silt and sand-sized fractions (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009). Studies report c/f-related distribution patterns varying from a grain-supported or enaulic microstructure in coarse-textured soils to a matrix-supported or porphyric c/f-related distribution pattern in finer textured soils. Along this continuum (from sandy to clayey), the microscopic organization of sediments range from a single-grain microstructure with irregularly shaped intergrain clayey peds, to a complex arrangement of subangular blocky to irregularly shaped clayey peds with scarce quartz sand. In the latter case many aggregates appear to be coalescent excrements of enchymraeids, earthworms, ants and termites. Porosity generally ranges from packing voids between quartz grains to the characteristic voids resulting from faunal turbation (Kooistra & Pulleman 2010). At this scale, in short, the overall impression gained from micromorphological observation is that ADEs are subject to intense faunal reworking. At the mesoscale of a

magnified thin section view, the organization of constituents of some ADE deposits greatly resembles European dark earth deposits that are similarly textured (Cammas 2004; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2010; Nicosia et al. 2017, this book), with coarse sand-sized or larger debris randomly distributed between clay peds that, in turn, embed fine-sized particulate material of anthropic origin (Figure 33.4). In some cases (Tertiary substrates), inherited quartz grains show runiquartz characteristics (Eswaran et al. 1975; Schnutgen & Spath 1983) and are essentially analogous to those observed in the A horizon of background soils (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009). In other cases (Quaternary alluvial settings) profiles record the rhythmic deposition of waterborne sediments (Souza 2011; Brancier et al. 2014).

Amazonian dark earths and background soils show significant differences, reflecting disparities in terms of anthropic inputs, degrees of bioturbation and preservation. All studies coincide that melanization is a result of the combined effect of intense staining with organic matter of the fine mineral fraction, coupled with the ubiquitous presence of microscopic charcoal fragments. Colours of the fine mineral fraction generally range from dark brown to greyish dark brown (PPL/OIL), with an undifferentiated to very weakly speckled b-fabric (XPL). As regards microscopic charcoal, reported densities vary greatly: some studies show that the latter is preferentially present as finely comminuted, fine sand-sized to silts-sized fragments that are densely embedded in peds of both sandy and clayey ADEs (Figure 33.5). Against this abundance, organs, tissues and/or cell fragments are rare, underscoring the very efficient comminution activity of soil fauna (Topoliantz & Ponge 2005) and the possible deposition of soot-like (Masiello 2004) fine-textured black carbon. Microscopic charcoal is rarer in an anthropic brown earth from the lower Orinoco (Arroyo-Kalin 2014b) and, consequently, does not appear to influence overall melanization directly. Similar contrasts have also been observed within anthropic brown earths of French Guiana (Brancier et al. 2014).

A characteristic aspect of most ADEs studied using soil micromorphology is the presence of small fragments of pottery and rubified clay aggregates, aquatic sponge spicules and opal phytoliths (Figure 33.6). Pottery fragments and clayey aggregates evidence comminution of debris produced and discarded in occupied settlements (microscopic fragments of heat-treated clay are lacking in reported terra mulata samples). Pottery fragments comminate down to sand size and are subangular to rounded inclusions primarily tempered with aquatic sponge spicules and, less frequently, with charcoal fragments, grog and silt- to fine sand-sized quartz grains. Of the pottery tempering agents, the well-reported use of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/0ef5476b8808973024fa67fa407262f9cd4d88d1904304091ffaa80cf34a86a6.jpg)  
Figure 33.4 (a) Sandy-textured terra preta from the Açutuba site, Brazilian state of Amazonas (see Heckenberger et al. 1999). Upper half (note red arrows) samples the lower part of an A horizon that has formed on an occupation deposit (lower part of the slide). The latter incorporates intrusive B horizon clayey aggregates (Y) and Laterite fragments (L), as well as Pottery (P) and Charcoal (C). (b) Clay-textured terra preta from the Hatahara site, showing abundant pottery (P), rubified clay (R), charcoal (C), and bone (B).

silica sponge spicules (Linne 1957) is confirmed by their occasional scattered presence in the groundmass, pointing to the availability of raw materials for manufacture and, potentially, the decomposition of pottery fragments. Amazonian sponges are mostly documented from flooded marshlands and river banks (Barros et al. 2013), so only in ADE sites located at these settings and on floodplains (as opposed to nonflooding uplands, where the majority of ADEs are reported) can sponge spicules be argued to be inherited from the parent material. Rubified clay aggregates likewise comminate to sand sized, subrounded bright orange clay aggregates, their most likely origins being the decay of different types of cooking facilities, including ovens (Arroyo-Kalin 2010b) and fire rests (Ribeiro 2014). Overall, heat-treated clay microartefacts, including their tempering agents, appear to be quite resistant to degradation and, consequently, they affect measurable properties of bulk samples (Costa et al. 2004a, 2004b; Arroyo-Kalin 2008). Phytoliths have been reported in studies of ADEs and can sometimes be seen in thin section. Of considerable interest to soil micromorphologists are inferences that can be drawn from the compared study of the magnetic susceptibility of background soils, terras mulatas and terras pretas (Arroyo-Kalin 2010a). The latter suggest that high magnetic susceptibility measurements in ADEs reflect the combined effects of inclusions of heat-treated clay and

pre-Columbian surface burning acting on Fe-rich sediments (see Allen & Macphail 1987).

Microscopic fragments of bone are reported in some but not other studies of ADEs. Bone fragments generally comminate to fine sand-sized fragments (Figure 33.7) and are very common in thin sections from clayey terras pretas forming on Tertiary-Age land surfaces (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Macedo et al. 2013) as well as in anthropic dark earths formed on Quaternary-Age substrates along the Solimões-Amazon river (Souza 2011). Microscopic fish bone is also common in charcoal-poor, dark brown, sandy-textured anthropic soils forming on Quaternary sediments of the lower Orinoco region (Arroyo-Kalin 2014b). Bone is nearly entirely lacking in studied samples from sandy terras pretas formed on Tertiary-Age substrates along the Negro river, near Manaus (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009). Bones are completely absent from studied samples of dark occupation deposits associated with Tertiary- and Quaternary-Age substrates in French Guiana (Brancier et al. 2014), as well as from strongly melanized, clayey-textured, charcoal-rich Quaternary Age deposits from the Ecuadorian Amazon (Arroyo-Kalin 2014b). The varying proportion of bone fragments in different contexts, therefore, must partly reflect regional availability and intrasettlement variation in the intensity of pre-Columbian discard, as well as preservation factors that include soil texture, porosity

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/7ef1560df6fe762732c2f03303f20c444e53bbfeb42074a445d78e81d9b159ee.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/0faed38a596489d4f52f0a8348653d2f1c2866b3bccc368e0a44c62d65f5f7a8.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ecbfdb1a53750b577ce4046b6b937b2f76ac114f297f5a56995985f56be22468.jpg)  
Figure 33.5 A hallmark of strongly melanised Anthrosols in Amazonia is the presence of finely comminuted, fine sand- to silt-sized fragments of charcoal embedded in the clayey matrix. (a) Irregularly shaped intergrain clayey peds from a sandy-textured terra preta (Acutuba site). Note large charred plant tissue fragment (C). PPL. (b) Inset from (a). Comminuted fine sand- to silt-sized charcoal (C) are abundant in the clayey fraction. OIL. (c) Clayey ped from clayey-textured terra preta, Lago Grande site, Brazilian state of Amazonas (see Neves & Petersen 2006). OIL. (d) Clayey ped from a terra mulata, Lago Grande site, showing silt-sized charcoal fragments (C) deriving from the comminution of charred plant tissue (T). OIL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/722db8b9a91091bf981945221d53ef41bb551e0447c6909effa95d765ea887c1.jpg)

and pH. In thin section, bone can be characterized by very low order interference colours under XPL, pointing to overall loss of collagen (Jans 2005; see also Villagran et al. 2017, this book). However, it is generally extremely well preserved, in some case showing strong autofluorescence under UVL, which suggests high retention of phosphorus in bone apatite (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009). Elemental maps based on SEM of thin sections with bone fragments in terra preta samples collected near Manaus highlight that the latter are rich in Ca and P and reveal that little leaching and diffusion of these elements towards the clayey fraction of the soil appears to have taken place (Schaefer et al. 2004; Souza 2011).

A common observation of different micromorphological studies is that ADEs show a very high frequency and chaotic disposition of gravel- to fine-sand sized rounded to subangular aggregates with fabric characteristics that

point to an origin higher or lower in the profile (Figure 33.8). Similarly, other horizons show frequent aggregates with dissimilar fabric characteristics and embedded microscopic charcoal typical of ADEs. In some instances, gravel-sized aggregates can be interpreted as evidence of soil modification by past pre-Columbian peoples (Figure 33.8a and b). In such cases, consideration of other contextual evidence can assist inferences about past digging and/or cultivation practices. However, in the smaller size classes, most aggregates can be associated with the activity of soil fauna and thus linked to their role in the burial and preservation of soil layers (Johnson 1985; Vacher et al. 1998; Phillips & Lorz 2008) and to reworking of specific deposits immediately after deposition. This underscores that traces of stratification indicating past occupation surfaces – in some cases perhaps similar to the stratified dark-coloured sediments observed

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/c52291499e06803f7806f077a990301b643cd4e2ddc078a5894df274b1f89539.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/bb5dbb6246f376533421856584a6fe4cfbe06a9fff741380322263f6c9ec6b36.jpg)  
Figure 33.6 (a) Small fragments of sponge spicule-tempered pottery are commonly observed in terra preta soils (Hatahara site). Note also bone (B) fragment. PPL. (b) Rubified clay aggregate embedded in clayey groundmass with abundant comminuted charcoal. B= bone, P= Pottery, R= rubified clay, S= sponge spicule, C= Charcoal, V= Void space. OIL.

in human-made mounds in Amazonian flooding landscapes (Versteeg 1985; Schaan et al. 2009; Jaimes 2012) - are unlikely to be regularly found in ADEs (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2010). However, it is the exception that proves the rule: a zone with contrasting fabrics within a buried A horizon under a clayey ADE deposit, stratified lenses in the fill of storage pits and very rare matrix-supported clayey peds in sandy ADEs can be observed in terra pretas formed on Tertiary-Age substrates; also illuvial clays appear to be truncated at the contact between a buried terra mulata A horizon (2Ab) and the Bt horizon (see Arroyo-Kalin 2009, 2010a; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009). These contrasting fabrics evidence faint but persistent microstratigraphic evidence. Along similar lines, studies in French Guiana have successfully quantified changes in charcoal density along a profile to distinguish different microfacies (Brancier et al. 2014). Studies along the floodplain of the Amazon river and in French Guiana have also documented rhythmic alluvial sediment inputs (Souza 2011; Brancier et al. 2014). Thus, it is accurate to state that soil micromorphological analysis is a powerful tool to detect relict stratigraphic information in ADEs.

Some pedofeatures observed in terras pretas are worthy of specific attention. Despite very intense levels of faunal reworking, well-developed dusty clay coatings are reported by different studies, especially along channels, planar voids and vughs of artefact-rich ADEs (Figure 33.8c, d). These pedofeatures contrast sharply with limpid illuvial clay coatings, which are typical of oxic horizons (Arroyo-Kalin 2008; Marcelino et al. 2010). Their 'high position' in the profile, above the B horizon, is likely related to low microporosity within a thick ADE deposit (after Kuhn et al. 2010, see also Teixeira et al. 2009). Dusty clay coatings may record the dispersal of organic-rich clay induced by the

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/337e53bfcb0afffa1ab841ff6972332613d91093c576b117063cba09ed7ce7cc.jpg)  
Figure 33.7 (a) Microscopic fragments of bone (B) embedded intrapeddally and comminuted to fine sand-sized fragments within terras pretas (see Figure 33.4). PPL. (b) Note fragments of bone (B) and charcoal (C) embedded in faecal pellets (F) within terras pretas. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/0c512f2fc3335498b4c7643c7b3c92ba14414b4bb97e7a65eb9fc8525b2a19cf.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/313a1f4f59f14f7e4f58d70d9683276ad62c274be89cd3603ddcda84bbdffb72.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6081e26ac32f837bcca34c59c875fd34ab8a9b4f78268772d00cf263a9d35fac.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6242b10fe2eef3fda0fba1505ad8ae5327a4a2d1e910f5c4b25ac753e4fc1066.jpg)  
Figure 33.8 (a) Limpid yellow clay aggregates suggesting an origin lower in the profile in terra preta. PPL. (b) Intrusive limpid yellow clay aggregates (A) within terra mulata (possibly a result of digging associated with past cultivation or tree uprooting). Note abundant comminuted charcoal (C). OIL. (c) and (d) Dusty clay coatings (CC) in terra preta. Note quartz grain (Q) and abundant comminuted charcoal (C) in the groundmass (G). OIL, PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3e835f92004838acd94dce72a9306466b1c2e27d6fe4288ffade2eed01702bc3.jpg)

release of potassium from ash (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009, after Slager & van der Wetering 1977), the destabilizing effects of bioturbation and alternating cycles of wetting and drying on faunally produced aggregates (Macedo et al. 2013) and past clearance and tillage (Brancier et al. 2014; see also Deak et al. 2017, this book). Similar coatings are found in the lower part of the A horizon of terras mulatas (Arroyo-Kalin 2008), which supports scenarios involving clearance and ash deposition. However, ash-diagnostic calcium oxalate plant cell pseudomorphs are not apparent in ADEs (nor reported in other Amazonian anthropic soils and archaeosediments). Whilst some of the dusty clay coatings may overlap features observed in agricultural and flooded soils (i.e. the 'agricutans' of Brewer and the flooding coatings of Brammer, see Kuhn et al. 2010), much work remains to be done in order to isolate robust micromorphological indicators of these types of practices, as well as of other inputs and processes.

# 34.4 Conclusions

Amazonian dark earths are best understood as the outcome of pedogenetic processes conditioned by land surfaces that have been enriched and transformed by pre-Columbian settlement and/or cultivation practices. They include terras pretas, terras mulatas and other dark-coloured anthropic soils associated with archaeological remains (Arroyo-Kalin 2014a). ADEs form from a palimpsest of pre-Columbian activities. As a category, ADEs is fuzzy in the sense that it can readily include other types of anthropic soils and archaeosediments from the Neotropical humid lowlands. Included here are a wide array of cases, ranging from dark anthropically enriched sediments associated with early archaeological sites both within and beyond the immediate region (Oyuela-Caycedo & Bonzani 2005; Castillo & Aceituno 2006; Arroyo-Kalin 2010a) to thin, charcoal-rich

sediment deposits forming on human-made mounds in lowland flooding landscapes (Versteeg 1985; Schaan et al. 2009; Jaimez 2012). The study of these and other stratified occupation deposits is important because some most likely bear a genetic relationship to ADEs as primary substrates.

Amazonian dark earths are remarkably fertile compared to the surrounding soil mantle upon which they have developed. Properties that underpin this fertility can be partially studied with the help of soil micromorphological analysis. The latter shows that microscopic charcoal fragments, bone and/or heat-treated clay fragments (including pottery and its constituents) are variously preserved within strongly melanized anthropic dark earths and sediments. These microscopic inclusions can be partially related to high organic matter and black carbon contents, high P and Ca and significant enhancement of magnetic susceptibility in both dark anthropic soils and archaeosediments (Lima et al. 2002; Schaefer et al. 2004; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Souza 2011; see also Glaser & Birk 2011). However, fossil traces of other likely inputs such as shell (Kern et al. 2004), ash (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009) and human bodily waste (Birk et al. 2011) have so far not been observed in thin section, most likely due to diagenesis. This underscores that much work remains to be conducted in order to explain the higher pH observed in most ADE expanses, as well as to pinpoint specific contributors to high Mn, Ba, Cu, K, Mg, Na, Sr and Zn (Woods 2003).

Melanization of dark pre-Columbian anthropic soils and other archaeosediments can be linked directly to the ubiquitous presence of silt-size or larger microscopic charcoal as well as strong organic matter retention. In this connection, comparative microscopic observations show that a hallmark feature of sediments recognized as terras pretas is high density of very fine sand- to silt-sized charcoal within the matrix (Arroyo-Kalin 2014b). This not only implies that the confident identification of highly comminuted charcoal (using OIL) is useful to distinguish between terras pretas and other strongly melanized sediments, but also helps to fine-tune existing assessments about the variability of ADEs. At the spatial scale of the soil expanse, charcoal will have been deposited where it was more abundantly produced in the past, where it was discarded and/or where charred material accumulated (domestic fires, pottery making facilities, middens, interior of houses, smouldering 'cool' fires, etc.). It is these charcoal-rich areas within a locale that will have been reworked by postdepositionalprocesses-verticallyandhorizontally-- into charcoal-rich sediments. At the scale of a given Amazonian dark earth expanse, therefore, it is fore

seeable that intervening areas will show less melanized sediments with lower charcoal density, leading to less strongly melanized sediments that embed variable quantities of artefacts. Similar, if not necessarily spatially overlapping scenarios can be envisioned regarding the overall abundance and ubiquity of microscopic heat-treated clay artefacts (pottery, rubified clay) and microscopic bone fragments, the latter being preferentially found in middens. At the same time, some of these will be more readily subject to degradation under low pH or within coarse textured ADEs.

Soil micromorphology helps to assess some of the ethnographically-based hypotheses that have been advanced to explain the formation of ADEs. It also demonstrates that terras pretas signal 'intensity' of very specific practices in the past (Arroyo-Kalin 2010a, 2012). Significant challenges lie ahead. First, a better balance needs to be achieved between establishing and measuring variability at the scale of the soil expanse vis-à-vis the very spatially restricted observations afforded by microscopic analysis. Second, fundamental questions about the sources and transportation of sediments within ADEs remain, including ascertaining the extent to which soil fauna conveyor activity contributes to burial and preservation of these deposits. Third, recording material signatures of behaviour correlates derived from experimental and ethnoarchaeological situations is especially important to test whether some types of ADEs were used for cultivation in pre-Columbian times. The importance of this challenge cannot be overemphasized: the ethnographic evidence for cultivation-oriented soil management is strong (e.g., Hecht 2003; Schmidt et al. 2014) and arguments linking cultivation to terras mulatas have been advanced (Sombroek 1966; Woods 2002; Denevan 2004; Arroyo-Kalin 2010a). When it comes to reworked occupation deposits, however, much tighter links need to be established between cause and effect before dusty clay coatings (Brancier et al. 2014), fragmentation of surface pottery (Schmidt et al. 2014), soil fertility (Lehmann et al. 2004; Glaser & Birk 2011) and/or the presence of plant macro and microfossil (Herrera et al. 1992; Bozarth et al. 2009) can be marshalled as strong evidence for cultivation in pre-Columbian times. This is because settlement activities unrelated to cultivation may readily result in micromorphological signatures and microscopic fossils that are analogous to those employed to infer clearance, uprooting and cultivation (e.g. Macphail et al. 1990; Holliday 2004; Deak et al. 2017, this book). To judge by the similarities and contrasts recorded by the few micromorphological studies of ADEs so far undertaken, however, the overall outlook is promising, especially if comparable techniques and similar reporting standards are adopted by future students.

# 34

# Cave and Rock Shelter Sediments

Carolina Mallo1,2 and Paul Goldberg3,4,5

$^{1}$ Departamento de Geografia e Historia, Universidad de La Laguna, Campus de Guajara, Tenerife, Spain  
$^{2}$  Instituto Universitario de Bio-Organica Antonio González, La Laguna, Campus de Guajara, Tenerife, Spain  
$^{3}$  Department of Archaeology, Boston University, Boston USA  
$^{4}$  Institute for Archaeological Sciences, University of Tübingen, Germany  
$^{5}$  School of Earth and Environmental Sciences, University of Wollongong, New South Wales, Australia

# 34.1 Introduction

Humans have inhabited caves and rock shelters since the Palaeolithic for more than 2 million years. Remains from their repeated occupations result in stratified geological and archaeological deposits. These sediments furnish a wealth of information about environmental changes, site formation processes, and inroads to understanding past activities and behaviour. Although rock shelters can form in a number of ways (Mentzer, 2017), they are cavities that tend to be wider than deep and can be found along cliff walls as an overhang. They thus provide protection from wind and rain, while remaining open to the outside, where there is natural light, vegetation and a good view of the landscape. Caves are more enclosed, and can penetrate tens to hundreds of meters into the bedrock, which is often limestone. Although cave entrances are more prominent in the archaeological record, use of deep galleries for domestic and ritual activities has also been documented in sites from different chronologies. Interestingly, geoarchaeological investigations of cave deposits have not only provided archaeologists important information about site formation and human activities, but they have supplied invaluable palaeoenvironmental, chronological, and geomorphological data to the geoscience community, who rarely study such contexts.

Both caves and rock shelters can be distinguished from open-air sites in being laterally constrained by walls. This has repercussions regarding depositional and postdepositional processes (Goldberg & Mandel 2008). Deep caves are restricted in their sources of sedimentation, which mainly comprise clay derived from solution weathering, authigenic minerals, and detrital mineralogenic silt and sand infiltrated through joints and conduits (Ford & Williams 1989). Occasionally, animals and humans

contribute faecal and bone remains. Humans might also introduce stone tools, pottery, or other artefacts. In contrast, rock shelter and cave mouth sedimentary deposits receive a relatively higher proportion of inputs from plants and animals (including humans), as well as from a wide variety of geogenic sources including bedrock, slopes, beaches, dunes, floodplains, or whatever sedimentary source occurs nearby.

The earliest sedimentological studies can be traced back to classic Palaeolithic sites of Southwest and Southeast France, and Central Europe, with the works of several Quaternary geoscientists (Bonifay 1956; Schmid 1958; Bordes 1972; Laville 1964; Farrand 1975; Laville 1976; Laville et al. 1980; Butzer 1981). At that time, field geomorphology and bulk sedimentological analyses provided general information about geogenic depositional processes and by inference, paleoclimates and climatochronological sequences (Laville et al. 1980). Such methods were standard practice in Old World geoarchaeology until the 1980s when researchers began to realize the extent of human inputs in many cave deposits (Goldberg 1979, 1980). Since then, cave and rock shelter archaeological deposits have been commonly studied at a microstratigraphic scale using multidisciplinary approaches, among which soil micromorphology has played a key role (Karkanas & Goldberg 2013).

Micromorphological investigations of prehistoric caves and rock shelter deposits have been carried out in different regions of the world (Goldberg 1979; Cremaschi 1990; Goldberg & Sherwood 1994; Macphail et al. 1994; Boschian 1997; Goldberg & Bar-Yosef 1998; Goldberg & Arpin 1999; Vallverdu 1999, 2001; Braillard 2000; Goldberg 2000; Goldberg & Macphail 2000; Macphail & Goldberg 2000; Goldberg 2001; Sherwood 2001; Karkanas 2002; Angelucci 2003; Mallol & Carbonell

2008; Angelucci & Zilhao 2009; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a; Mentzer & Quade 2012; Miller et al. 2013). These, among other similar studies have provided a corpus of information and data on the most common basic microscopic components, textures and fabrics in such sedimentary deposits and furnished hypotheses about the different kinds of geogenic and biogenic (including anthropogenic) processes they represent. As such, these works have equipped us with comparative material to approach site formation processes in archaeological caves and rock shelters. In this entry, we illustrate with examples of our own work, examples of the main depositional and postdepositional processes occurring in cave and rock shelter contexts and some of their representative micromorphological features.

# 34.2 Common Microfabrics from Rock Shelter and Cave Sites

Although caves and rock shelter deposits are complex – being an intimate mixture of geogenic and anthropogenic components – individual microfabrics are visible and decipherable under the microscope, and hence can be used to infer geological and human agents, as well as syn- and postdepositional modifications.

We present depositional and physical syndepositional processes together because the main agents of cave sedimentation (wind, water and animals, including humans) can also strongly modify existing deposits penecontemporaneously; thus the micromorphological features resulting from both primary deposition and reworking are intricately related. For instance, at the entrance runoff may rework aeolian silt and sand resulting in finely laminated sand, silt and sandy clay, which are virtually 'instantaneous' from the geoarchaeological point of view.

# 34.2.1 Geogenic Deposition and Syndepositional Physical Reworking

The variety of natural depositional environments and processes near cave entrances and rock shelters is rather limited. Certain kinds of deposits, such as sandy and silty ones derived from aeolian sedimentation or silty clayey deposits from low energy runoff or sheet wash are common in cave mouths and rock shelters and resemble those from the open air.

# 34.2.1.1 Aeolian Sedimentation

Aeolian deposits vary in their lithology and grain size depending on their source, but in general, they are characterized by predominantly well-sorted sand or silt in a matrix that can vary from loose to compact depending on the particular postdepositional processes affecting it;

depending on the archaeological context, sandy deposits can also be mixed with bone, stone, charcoal, shell, and pottery. With the exception of in situ sand accumulations of dunes (generally rare, although see for example some South African coastal caves: Mossel Bay – Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a), windblown sediment is syndepositionally reworked (e.g., runoff, bioturbation, or trampling) and mixed with other sedimentary components before burial.

Aeolian quartz silt is also a component in caves but rarely as a distinct layer of loess. Rather, it arrives incorporated with other, nonaeolian sediments, as in the case of silt being first embedded into surface soils, which were then washed into the cavity (e.g., Kebara, Dust Cave; Sherwood 2001; Goldberg et al. 2007).

Different microfabrics comprised of sand and silt have been documented in several sites. At Die Kelders Cave, S. Africa, aeolian sand decalcified in place is overlain by brown silty clay that partially has percolated into it; both are overlain by aeolian sand composed of quartz and shell, which was the precursor to the bottommost sand before it was decalcified (Goldberg 2000; Figure 34.1).

The site of Mandrin, in southeastern France (Slimak 2008), is a shallow cave facing the Rhone Valley and exposed to the Mistral seasonal winds. Here, the main component of the Pleistocene deposit is windblown quartz sand derived from alluvium with minor proportions of glauconite sand; there is very little micromass either coating or bridging the sand grains. This loose sandy deposit contains common, moderately well sorted sand-sized flint, rounded bone, char and charcoal fragments derived from Palaeolithic human occupations (Figure 34.2). This case of embedding is the result of trampling of anthropogenic debris on a loose, sandy substrate.

# 34.2.1.2 Runoff and Sheet Wash

Low energy transport of silty clayey sediment is a common process in caves and rock shelters and has been widely documented in a number of prehistoric caves where sediments are derived from an overlying plateau (e.g., Dust Cave, Alabama or Kebara and Hayonim, Israel, Goldberg & Sherwood 1994; Goldberg & Bar-Yosef 1998; Sherwood et al. 2004). Some of the characteristic microfabrics consist of a clayey micromass with variable amounts of silt and or sand derived from nearby soils or bedrock (Figure 34.3). Depending on the energy of deposition, the thickness of a single runoff lamina can range from one to several cm, or even more in massive flood events. Under the microscope, these may exhibit horizontal bedding or diffuse bedding, and sometimes localized silt or sand 'stringers' or laminae interbedded within clayey sediment (Figure 34.3). However, such fine bedding can be easily obliterated by postdepositional

processes and result in a massive or crumbly silty / sandy clayey microfabric. Strong rainfall bringing a large quantity of water into the cave may also result in vertical translocation of particles by percolating water, such as reported at Die Kelders (Goldberg 2000).

# 34.2.1.3 Dripping and Groundwater

Dripping water is a common phenomenon beneath rock shelter or cave entrance overhangs and in humid cave interiors and its identification in an archaeological context can provide important clues to locate former driplines or delimit very humid areas, as in the case of Pech de l'Aze IV where calcitic hypocoatings formed beneath the former cave roof (Dibble et al. 2009; Figure 34.4).

Due to the localized nature of dripping water, secondarily derived calcite is commonly found in isolated samples, not continuously across space. This is an important condition to take into consideration because secondary calcium carbonate precipitation is a common postdepositional phenomenon and its occurrence can be linked to many different processes. In contrast, groundwater, which has a similar micromorphological expression, can be found laterally spread and cementing greater portions of a deposit, typically with sparite. The presence of interstitial sparite is representative of phreatic conditions (Figure 34.5a and b) whereas micrite or microsparite may indicate vadose conditions (Figure 34.5c and d) (Durand et al. 2010). An example of the latter is the typical 'breccia' reported in numerous archaeological cave contexts (Figure 34.6). This type of sediment usually comprises clasts of archaeological and natural material cemented by secondary calcium carbonate of variable crystal size (Figure 34.7).

# 34.2.1.4 Roofspall

Bedrock debris from the walls and ceilings of caves and rock shelters, often known as 'roofspall', is a common element in most archaeological deposits of this kind. Overall, it is characterized by clasts matching the lithology of the bedrock, but clast shape and degree of sorting may vary. The presence of angular to subrounded, poorly sorted clastic accumulations point to the vicinity of a source of roofspall, while rounded, better sorted accumulations may be derived from transported roofspall (Figure 34.8), chemical attack (diagenesis), and more rarely, cryoturbation, which may round roofspall considerably.

In many cases, the bedrock is limestone or dolomite that is susceptible to subaerial weathering and can be affected by seasonal or larger scale climatic shifts in humidity and temperature. Therefore, variations in the proportion of roofspall throughout a stratified sequence can provide clues about environmental changes. However, in many sites (e.g., Kebara and Hayonim, Israel or Roc de Marsal, France) roofspall may be phosphatized or be totally dissolved, so one cannot take clast abundance as an absolute indicator of climatic change. In many caves in South Africa, quartzite bedrock is fractured and easily spalled, producing tabular, angular pieces (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a).

Whilst still in place, overhangs, walls and ceilings of caves and rock shelters are also exposed to biogenic agents (plant, animal and microbial activity), which may leave a micromorphological signature as weathering rinds or laminations on the surface of clasts (Campy 1989; Braillard 2000; Marks et al. 2002; Braillard et al. 2004) often a product of bat/bird guano diagenesis (see Figure 34.8d and e). In these cases, the rinds are yellow in PPL, isotropic in XPL, and carefully conform to the outer

surface of the clast; the colour and isotropy are indicative of a phosphatic composition, likely dahlite (Weiner et al. 2007 - see Figure 34.9a). In other cases, they are dark brown or black in PPL (Figure 34.9b, c and d), suggesting an organic composition that may originate from biogenic or pedogenic substances, or even from anthropogenic soot. In these cases, it is not possible to determine the source using micromorphology.

Nevertheless, rinds on clasts may also form diagenetically, while on the surface or buried beneath it. Postdepositionally, clasts of limestone roof spall are susceptible to diagenetic transformations (e.g., dissolution - Figure 34.10), and just as for bone, their degree of alteration is a measure of the intensity and extension of diagenetic processes, which can be useful to assess the integrity of the associated archaeological record. Factors influencing the degree of diagenesis include for example, biological activity, water content,

and position in the cave. At Kebara and Hayonim Caves, for example, extensive diagenesis was recorded in deposits from the central part of the cave, and not near the walls where the buffering effects of the limestone bedrock were evident.

A different kind of roofspall may occur in cases where the bedrock is composed of loose or friable silty marl or sandy limestone, dolomite, or a substrate liable to disaggregation into sand. In such cases, normal sedimentation consists of a continuous 'rain' of silt or sand, which will be syndepositionally mixed and incorporated into the substrate. Such grains can be identified as roof spall because their shape, size, and inclusions are related to the bedrock lithology (Figure 34.11). Many caves and shelters in the Dordogne, France are characterized by this type of sedimentation, as well as at Meadowcroft Rock shelter, USA (Donahue & Adovasio 1990; Goldberg & Arpin 1999).

# 34.2.1.5 Cold-Climate Features

Humid cave entrances and rock shelters subject to seasonally cold climates undergo cryogenesis. This is a process involving freezing and thawing of water trapped in bedrock cracks and subsurface sediments, generating roof spall (i.e., cryoclasts) and causing microstructural transformation of subsurface sediments, respectively.

Cryoclasts, which are characterized by their angular shapes, are common in Pleistocene archaeological cave deposits and have been traditionally used as markers of cold climates (e.g., Laville et al. 1980), although there proved to be ambiguity in this approach (Texier 2000). Identification of cryoturbation microfabrics, derived from in situ freeze thaw beneath the sedimentary surface,

provides more robust, contextualized evidence of cryogenesis (Van Vliet-Lanoë 1985, 1998, 2005, 2010). These microfabrics may include sand-sized cryoclasts – but with a fine-grained matrix – normally exhibit parallel horizontal fissures and weakly to well-developed lenticular aggregates in what is known as 'frost' or ice lensing. Other characteristic features are silt cappings on clasts (which may be cryoclasts – Figure 34.12) and vesicles from water saturation. Microlaminated calcitic pendentions on clasts have also been documented in arctic open-air contexts (Courty et al. 1994). Recurrent freezing and thawing may lead to stronger cryoturbation in which bedding is obliterated and aggregates become granular due to rotation (Figure 34.12E).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6d9e73a75264f6855bcd57149d0c3af2e52265f83fef7bf26018274f2a0df709.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/19cd64307d6a364fce31e07638f7bcdfd8b95ac983634fbc57b88a3523c98158.jpg)  
Figure 34.9 (a) Scan view of clastic sediment showing roofspall, coated with black matter, possibly soot (arrows). (b) and (c) Detail of clast edge. (b) PPL; (c) XPL. Mandrin Cave, France. (d) Scan view of a laminated phosphatic rind on roofspall clast from Hayonim Cave, Israel.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a156579674a64c66b50d58a951b59ad3bc528bc05cf80b4b3a44c9befde35b2b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ebee368602c4a2d58de6848f10b93584c95a49ddd813ef69d09fc955a4fe6720.jpg)

# 34.2.2 Biogenic Depositional Processes

Besides humans, cave and rock shelters are occupied by a variety of animals seeking shelter and warmth. These include hibernating bears, smaller carnivores (e.g., hyenas, fox) that use caves for denning, rodents (including bats) and birds. They not only produce their own waste and deposits (e.g., guano and mud from nest breakdown, respectively), but also physically transform the substrate, as is the case with bears (Stiner et al. 1996). Trampling, rummaging, burrowing, and digging by such animals can obliterate intact combustion structures and human occupation surfaces and strongly disturb the original spatial distribution of archaeological assemblages and their chronology by mixing anthropogenic remains from different time periods. Therefore, identifying the presence of particular animals can be highly informative of the

integrity of the archaeological record. This can be achieved using micromorphology.

# 34.2.2.1 Hyenas and Bears

Archaeological deposits in caves can be heavily disturbed by hibernating or denning animals, as these rework and compress them, as well as litter them with chemically reactive faeces. Identifying the presence of these animals in archaeological context is important because their occurrence indicates an alternation of human and animal occupation and the likelihood that the original makeup and spatial distribution of the context of human activity might not be intact.

Carnivore coprolites – such as those of hyenas – exhibit a massive groundmass with pale grey or yellowish brown hues in PPL, and are bluish grey to isotropic in XPL due to their phosphatic composition (Figure 34.13 – see

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/636c32db63f56e27b372705b5e64981432de7713e0d98ce71674eea80cc67541.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d5758a318319017173c4c7e065836e2b254dadd67223acd696ff2e7410463318.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/736600fa01b9086b9cf716aec1e1affd821c6354403bdd256ae3c3166f507351.jpg)  
Figure 34.13 (a) Hyena coprolites from Roc de Marsal. Note their massive, pale grey groundmass and silt cappings and coatings. PPL. (b) Angular carnivore coprolite from Mandrin Cave, France. Note the presence of vesicles. PPL. (c) Carnivore coprolite (pale brown grain at bottom right) from Lusakert Cave, Armenia. PPL. (d) Same as (b), XPL. Note isotropic appearance due to the phosphatic composition.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/f12b4f6dbba913206089ea3db5ce6be601fead35d56e42363559fa36b5932bb2.jpg)

Brönniman et al. 2017a, this book). Some specimens might also contain occasional bone splinters. Carnivore coprolite-rich microfacies have been identified at a number of sites (Zhoukoudian - Goldberg et al. 2001; Roc de Marsal - Goldberg et al. 2012; Hohe Fels - Goldberg et al. 2003; Cova del Gegant - Luján & Borras 2011). These commonly exhibit loose, often microaggregate or granular microstructures derived from recurrent surface rummaging and may contain scattered to abundant coprolite fragments (Figure 34.13c).

Bear hibernation has been macroscopically inferred sedimentologically by the reworking of the constituents, as well as the deformed and erosional upper contacts of individual layers. Microscopically, these sediments are strongly phosphatized, with clasts exhibiting weathering rinds. These features have been interpreted as resulting from bear excrements, urine and carcass decomposition (Braillard et al. 2004).

# 34.2.2.2 Bats and Birds

Caves are the natural habitat of bats and because they live in colonies, their droppings accumulate as significant layers of guano (see Karkanas 2017, this book). Identification of such layers in archaeological contexts is important, as they mark periods of human absence in the cave. In prehistoric instances, the guano is typically not preserved, although microfacies rich in chitin remains of insectivorous bat guano including chitin fragments are described in a study of Cendres Cave (Bergadà et al. 2013). However, more commonly, the guano is degraded and leaves behind only proxies, such as phosphatic crusts or nodules representing former surfaces exposed in antiquity (e.g., Mallol et al. 2010). The presence of reactive elements in the chemical composition of guano may also trigger diagenetic changes that affect the preservation of the archaeological record, particularly bone (Karkanas et al. 2000, 2002; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004a; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010b).

Bird guano is a common occurrence in cave entrances and rock shelters near the overhang zone, although in coastal caves, guano can be observed at the rear of the cave (e.g., Cave 13b, S. Africa, Karkanas & Goldberg 2010a). It can be found as sand-sized, massive, nodules reworked into the sediment or amorphous yellowish infillings. These features may contain seed coats, small bone or other undigested residues of the bird's food (Figure 34.14).

Due to the phosphate-rich composition of bird guano, it normally exhibits a pale yellow colour in PPL and is isotropic in XPL. Finally, we note the effects of bird nests, particularly of those made of mud by swallows, for example. While functioning they are attached to the roof/walls, but upon abandonment, they fall and become incorporated into the sediment often leading to puzzling interpretations of mud derived from a source not within the confines of the cave.

# 34.2.2.3 Herbivores

Domestic sheep and goats have been penned in Eurasian rock shelters since the Neolithic. Thus, herbivore faecal input has been widely documented in fumier installations from sites dating from the Neolithic, Bronze Age, up to the Middle Ages (see Shahack-Gross 2017, this book; Canti & Brochier 2017, this book; Bronniman et al. 2017b, this book). Although rare in older contexts, evidence of herbivore coprolites has also been described at some Palaeolithic rock shelter sites. For instance, the Middle Palaeolithic deposits from Lusakert, Armenia, contain low amounts of microscopic dung spherulites. They are contained in subrounded nodules of phosphatic appearance (Figure 34.15a, 15b). Further geochemical analyses are needed to corroborate its molecular composition. At El Salt, Spain, there is common herbivore dung in the form of spongy, fibrous coprolites comparable to those reported in Holocene stabling contexts (e.g., Matthews et al. 1997; Macphail et al. 1998; Macphail & Goldberg 2010 – Figure 34.15c and d).

# 34.2.2.4 Plants

Although plants do not usually grow deep within cave settings, they do in cave entrance areas or zones of rock shelters exposed to sunlight. Micromorphological evidence of plant growth includes calcitic root hypocoatings on clayey cave entrance sediment and less commonly pieces of primary tufa derived from breakdown of the ceiling/walls (Goldberg & Macphail 2012). At Gran Dolina, Spain, the presence of root hypocoatings indicated an advanced phase in degradation of the cave structure (Malol & Carbonell 2008). In addition, at Kebara and Hayonim, Israel, abundant accumulations of siliceous seed coatings of possibly Podonosma orientalis can be seen (E. Weiss, personal communication) (Figure 34.16); this species occupies rocky cliffs and is found on cave walls at entrance to cave today.

# 34.2.3 Anthropogenic Depositional Processes

Anthropogenic deposits are a considerable component of archaeological cave and rock shelter sites, and in some cases they constitute the majority of the cave infilling (e.g., Kebara: Goldberg et al. 2007). Processes and associated micromorphological features and fabrics include combustion features, bedding, and trampled sediment, both geogenic and anthropogenic, which have been studied mostly in Palaeolithic sites. These features are not archaeologically different from their open-air counterparts, except that they risk having been altered by postdepositional processes, described here. We do not include combustion structures and herbivore penning deposits, as these are thoroughly described by Mallol et al. 2017, this book, and Shahack-Gross 2017, this book.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/9a017186df8d115da36c9a473171758b54be8eb294fb4c52d06ef934fba70abb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6e27154e33acae74aeba2a03a430640d64bd4201beb84e823f4abc9e315d4a8c.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/2e099b2ac54f0e4f94015003288d17fd79c6ee6eb2301e165313eaef1535a69e.jpg)  
Figure 34.14 (a) and (b) Bone-containing bird guano infilling from El Salt rock shelter, Spain: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. (c) and (d) Seed-containing bird guano from the same site. (c) PPL; (d) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/727e9b84ed1d18413b63a7ccd9ce74f6d5cf24ea2ab344bfe1d37bcc0efe29e5.jpg)

# 34.2.3.1 Bedding

Ethnographic and ethnoarchaeological research in New Guinea has provided examples of human occupation of rock shelters in which living surfaces were covered with plants (Pétrequin & Pétrequin 1988). In archaeological contexts, plant bedding is rarely preserved except for sites in arid settings. Nevertheless, micromorphological evidence of bedding has been reported from the dry Middle Stone Age rock shelter site of Sibudu (S. Africa; Goldberg et al. 2009), where it is expressed as mm- tocm-thick layers of phytoliths, or charred plant material – or combinations thereof. Similar microfabrics have been reported from the Spanish Palaeolithic sites of Esquilleu (Cabanes et al. 2010) and Cendres (Bergadà et al. 2013). At Sibudu, experimental work has shown that the bedding installations were possibly burned for maintenance purposes (Miller & Sievers 2012). For detailed descriptions and images of burnt bedding microfabrics see Mallol et al. 2017, this book.

# 34.2.3.2 Trampled Occupation Debris

Microscopic features indicative of human or animal trampling are frequently identified in prehistoric cave sites (e.g., Gé et al. 1993; Dibble et al. 2009; Goldberg et al. 2009; Miller et al. 2013) and include compaction, fissuring, microaggregation and in situ crushing and scissor-fracturing of broken bone and charcoal (Courty et al. 1989; Goldberg et al. 2012; Mallol & Mentzer 2015; Rentzel et al. 2017, this book - Figure 34.17).

Commonly, trampling in caves takes place on dense, often clay-rich substrates. A few cases of archaeological occupation surfaces on loose, sandy substrates have been reported (Goldberg 2000; Macphail & Goldberg 2000). At Mandrin, France, archaeological Unit E - a massive sandy layer interpreted as a well preserved human occupation surface - yielded a microfabric of possible trampling on a loose, sandy surface. It consists of frequent well-sorted, sand-sized subrounded bone fragments (a few of which are vertically oriented), sand-sized flint

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/86e97058f6bde473e09ce3b64ebd4c568aaef64a635c2e3f2375666b7bd02fdb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ca36a3288b4af13cde10ea37bcafecfeff28166e364b46a2e1ff7f8077bb3366.jpg)
