flakes and fissured larger bone fragments. These elements are concentrated at the top mm of the sedimentary layer on which the archaeological assemblage was found (Figure 34.18). Trampling experiments on clayey and sandy cave and rock shelter sedimentary surfaces are needed to understand the formation of trampled occupation deposits.

# 34.2.4 Physical Postdepositional Processes

Besides providing sedimentary material, the different agents of cave sedimentation described above (wind, water and animals, including humans) can also strongly modify existing deposits. Wind and water may erode, and syndepositionally sort and translocate light material lying on the surface. This material can be identified microscopically.

In addition, biological activity - including both plants and animals - can have a profound effect on deposits.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/facc48d6dc5350123132a55b811febaeb1d39740bbc25f594a1bd2cd0f245086.jpg)  
Figure 34.17 (a) Trampled bone. Lusakert Cave, Armenia. PPL. (b) Trampled burnt bone. Roc de Marsal, France. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/12447f872bf2f5faa06db28b8454708fe9473ce24222536b3fcc9559d376a93b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/44dda8679a0ca158db0825116e11e055aed6be1794f0a3ea68aef260f29e0645.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d687398b31f8e9f25a80d3e63a2754b9c1b445296beac24e6c93fdc6c5c2f720.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3300f725babdb4beeec1a660feedc12fcb9a5917442b238248a721bffacc81ac.jpg)  
Figure 34.18 Subrounded sand-sized bone fragments ((a) and (b)) and fissured bone fragment ((c) and (d)) contained in a loose sandy sediment beneath a rich Neanderthal occupation surface from Layer E of Mandrin Cave, France. (a) and (c) PPL; (b) and (d) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/5791a481a54d648c9835d701adb9d76b5c757c4984a800b9a0df0c87b287da25.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/31ad9c1f96be240de3388123c3292269aac8ee3a22e6fda94cf3d3e03170d849.jpg)  
Figure 34.19 Examples of bioturbation in cave deposits. (a) Scan view of wasp burrow from Hayonim Cave, Israel. (b) Scan view of granular fill from a rodent burrow at Kebara Cave, Israel.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d251d4319cc914863e0ee01b299b4700cdcf10f28b6c1def05789214bdd66289.jpg)

As discussed above, roots both displace sediments but serve as a locus of secondary minerals such as calcite hypocoatings. Soil mesofauna – including worms, various insects (mites, ants, termites and wasps) – can increase porosity and create crumb, microaggregate or granular microstructures, and at the same time allow for artefact movement (Kretzschmar 1987; Van Nest 2002; Canti 2003; Reatto et al. 2009; Sarcinelli et al. 2009 – see for example Figure 34.19a). On a larger scale, repeated burrowing by rodents can completely obliterate depositional structures and replace them with characteristic loose, aggregate microstructures (Figure 34.19b).

# 34.2.5 Chemical Postdepositional Processes

The type of textural pedofeatures found in cave sediments is strongly dependent upon fluctuations in their water content. Cave and rock shelter environments are normally subject to precipitation and dissolution of different minerals (particularly calcite, phosphates and gypsum) according to a variable, complex combination of factors including temperature, water content, pH, oxidation state, and ion concentration. Although the pathways of chemical reactions are specific to each context, it is safe to say that water is necessary to affect dissolution and precipitation. Therefore, identification of particular pedofeatures derived from precipitation or dissolution may be informative of the local conditions (overall wet climate, dripline position, localized puddles or karstic springs, as described in 2.1.3). In addition, they can provide information about pre-existing components of the deposits that might have dissolved under humid

conditions (e.g., bone, excrements and other sources of organic matter).

The first detailed multidisciplinary studies of postdepositional dissolution and precipitation of different minerals in archaeological contexts were carried out in the 1990s and 2000s in the Near Eastern Palaeolithic cave sites of Tabun, Kebara and Hayonim (Weiner et al. 1995; Schiegl et al. 1996, 2003; Karkanas et al. 2000). These works showed how the common presence of bat and bird guano in damp, enclosed caves and cave entrances in combination with other sources of decaying organic matter may strongly alter the chemical composition of bone and calcitic wood ash. In some cases, these elements may disappear altogether (references above). Thus, they showed how micromorphological and mineralogical characterization of diagenetic processes allows us to assess the degree of integrity of the archaeological record; a crucial task prior to interpreting the composition and spatial distribution of the remains. Since then, further studies focusing on this topic have contributed experimental and ethnoarchaeological data (e.g., Shahack-Gross 2004b).

# 34.2.5.1 Precipitation

Calcite As previously noted, secondary calcium carbonate precipitation is a common postdepositional phenomenon in caves and its occurrence can be linked to many different processes. Besides the common carbonate pedofeatures related to dripping and groundwater (see 1.1.3), others, such as primary travertine or flowstone, indicate highly saturated conditions (Figure 34.20a), or

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/bc29391d82dbe7d4522cd76d130662e0b7c9fab766280a624f557e9ca0aa9357.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d41adf90c9464532ddae38b924ff809d177fcca22cafa838e0a694aba3e9a75d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/c78342559f0d68578b6009bd96736eac66146e140f87a3213c6fdab6aadce418.jpg)  
Figure 34.20 (a) Travertine fragment from Obi-Rahkmat rock shelter, Uzbekistan. PPL. (b); (c); (d) Reworked tufa fragments from El Salt Palaeolithic site, Spain. Note their distinctive spongy microstructure reminiscent of plant structures. (b), (c) PPL; (d) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/f2a0f9d9850e5d2c8b4501f764dd2e32545ebf450b171db57125d835ae1f5dea.jpg)

primary tufa from calcium carbonate precipitation on plants growing on wet substrates (Garralda et al. 2014) or on the cave ceiling and walls. Travertine/flowstone and tufa can also be found in secondary position as roof spall after having been reworked syndepositionally from their source (e.g., in Obi-Rakhmat; Mallol et al. 2009). In such cases, they can be identified as clasts of variable shape (angular to rounded) depending on the degree of transport (Figure 34.20b, c and d). Their presence as stalactite clasts in deposits, for example, can signal the presence of a former cave roof, which in turn points to a more enclosed space.

Phosphates In archaeological cave sediments, phosphate mineralization is principally related to the presence of bird or bat guano, and is commonly associated with the dissolution of bone, ash, limestone, and other calcitic

elements. In cases of extreme diagenesis (e.g., Tabun and Hayonim Caves, Israel) phosphate diagenesis is associated with the breakdown of clays and the formation of opal (Goldberg & Nathan 1975; Karkanas et al. 1999; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010b; Weiner et al. 2002, 2007). Among postdepositional processes, phosphate diagenesis has been prominently studied and is increasingly better understood as micromorphology is coupled with geochemical analyses. In thin section, dahlite is the most abundant phosphate, and can occur, for example, as massive optically isotropic nodules, weathering rinds on limestone (see Figure 34.8), or as acicular and botryoidal crystals lining veins (Goldberg 1973; Rink et al. 2003). Other relatively common secondary phosphate minerals in prehistoric caves, such as crandellite, taranakite, leucophosphite and montgoryite, are much more difficult to identify

unequivocally in thin section because of the crystal size; variscite, however, is rare and vivianite with its blue and brown colours does not appear to occur in caves (for details, see Weiner et al. 1995, 2002; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010b).

Gypsum Gypsum is common in cave deposits although its mode of formation is not always clear. In some cases it occurs in association with degradation of bat guano (Shahack-Gross et al. 2004a), where it occurs as lozenge-shaped crystals; in alterations of recent surfaces at Tabun Cave, recent gypsum can be found as interlocking mosaics of silt-sized crystals. On the other hand, several prehistoric sites in South Africa (e.g., Diepkloof and Sibudu, South Africa) contain secondary gypsum, but the origin remains unclear. At Sibudu for example, gypsum occurs as lozenge shapes, needles and fine mosaic nodules, and in some cases is found within layers of phosphate-rich guano, which again points to association with guano alteration.

Other Minerals In addition to phosphates and gypsum, cave environments contain a wealth of secondary minerals (Hill & Forti 1997). We point out here just a couple more minerals that we have encountered or appear to be significant. One of the most striking features found in some of the most prominent prehistoric sites (e.g., Grotte XVI, Karkanas et al. 2002; Zhoukoudian, Goldberg et al. 2001; Theopetra, Karkanas et al. 1999; Pech de l'Aze IV and Roc de Marsal, Goldberg et al. 2012) is the presence of bright red layers or zones, which have commonly been taken to represent the reddening of the deposits by fire. Zhoukoudian is perhaps the foremost example of this phenomenon, where the red colour is still cited as representing among the earliest evidence of early use and control of fire. However, there, as well as Pech de l'Aze IV and Roc de Marsal, for example, the red colour can be shown to represent the precipitation of very fine silt-sized crystals of hematite that overprint the existing matrix and carbonate clasts (Figure 34.21a). Rather than being produced by fire, the bright red colour associated with the hematite appears to be related to the presence of organic matter, which is visible in the field at both Roc de Marsal and Pech de l'Aze IV.

Moreover, in association with the reddening of the deposits from the middle part of Layer 4 at Zhoukoudian (Goldberg et al. 2001), we find vugs with neoformed mosaic quartz crystals (Figure 34.21b and c). This precipitation appears to be related to the formation of secondary hematite after the cavity was open to the sky. In this case, leaching associated with monsoonal climates resulted in the breakdown of biotite and other iron-rich minerals, followed by iron and quartz precipitation.

# 34.2.5.2 Dissolution Features

Mineral dissolution is a common process in moist or wet cave and rock shelter contexts and has been documented by micromorphology at different sites (e.g., Goldberg et al. 2001; Karkanas et al. 2000, 2002; Weiner et al. 2002; Mallol et al. 2010, Miller et al. 2013). It commonly involves the same suite of minerals described in the previous section, and includes primary as well as secondarily precipitated minerals (e.g., dissolution of gypsum derived from organic matter degradation). Some minerals, such as gypsum and anhydrite, are more water-soluble than others and are likely to be readily dissolved. Thus, their presence in archaeological cave sediment indicates dry sedimentary environments. Often, their pseudomorphs are preserved within the sediment and can be identified microscopically (Figure 34.22).

Acidity (pH) is another important factor of mineral dissolution, and overall, carbonate minerals are susceptible to dissolution in acidic solutions. Among the most common dissolution features in karstic settings are decalcification zones, resulting from fluctuating pH and water content in calcitic sediments. In such cases, localized decalcified areas with sharp boundaries can be recognized (see Figure 34.1). Often, individual calcite crystals at the decalcification front exhibit serrated edges. Decalcification may also be observed in roofspall clasts, which might show internal dissolution vugs of variable size (see Figure 34.10).

In sum, caves – but to a lesser extent rock shelters that are more open – are vast sinks for biogenic (including anthropogenic) and geogenic materials. But, unlike open-air sites, their closed spaces foster certain conditions that can extensively modify depositional materials, both at the time of deposition but also after it. In fact, the whole suite of diagenetic changes that are virtually unique to the cave environment can be so extensive as to obliterate original depositional signatures and ultimately their interpretations.

# 34.3 Conclusion

Considering the role and importance of caves and rock shelters in human history – as well as their worldwide abundance – it is remarkable how few micromorphological studies have been undertaken. It is only in the past three or four decades that micromorphology has been used in new projects, and in the restudy of deposits from previously excavated sites. Although there are numerous reasons for this that cannot be discussed here, we simply mention that the extant ones have furnished remarkable evidence for past human environments and activities. In the future, in addition to carrying out more

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/35e0bd59bebb5f90098968bc069860af9b0303d0d4c5c84b12ce0d717e3f2c96.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/0eb1176a0b5ff3210f67202d139764effa38f166564dfc09a0f8ee4ea9b80356.jpg)  
Figure 34.21 Examples of hematite precipitation. (a) Precipitation of very fine silt-sized crystals of hematite overprinting the existing micromass materials. Roc de Marsal, France. OIL. (b) and (c) Vughs with neoformed mosaic quartz crystals. Zhoukoudian, China. (b) PPL; (c) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/34a3535ce450469e1565a94f573426af31136f90b5a9afdeb1dd148b5a2df6c8.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/1ac0eb28c8fd1ced7e30495c6bc7576b804a0c234391f5cb0000e92c397e914b.jpg)  
Figure 34.22 Moldic voids after lenticular gypsum in archaeological sediment from Lusakert Cave, Armenia. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/6f1ce9a4986e98ba36b0d850c5ed2ab919f804d58513fad2e79ff6f843cd2857.jpg)

micromorphological studies of specific caves, we recommend the following avenues for future research:

- More experimental studies are needed that involve specific human activities and deposits, as for example, in trampling and fire (Miller et al. 2009; Mallol et al., 2013a, 2013b) and the construction of bedding (Miller & Sievers 2012), all important human activities in the past.  
- More attempts to incorporate ethnoarchaeology to serve as essentially 'reference collections' and analogues to evaluate what has gone on in the past; in the past few years, however, researchers have realized the effectiveness of this strategy (Shahack-Gross 2004a; Mallol et al. 2007; Friesem et al. 2011, 2014). Moreover, this approach provides information on diagenesis and other processes that take too long to evaluate with experimentation.

- Greater integration of micromorphological observations with microstratigraphic analyses, such as microFTIR (see Berna 2017, this book), XRF (see Mentzer 2017, this book), XRD (see Berthold & Mentzer 2017, this book) and GC-MS (Sistiaga et al. 2014; see also Shillito 2017, this book). This multi-analytical approach provides for robust geoarchaeological information by linking contextualized analytical data with specific micromorphological signatures (Goldberg & Berna 2010).

Finally, we note that the promotion of books like this one provides visual micromorphological data to an ever increasing audience of archaeologists and natural scientists. After all, micromorphology is a visually intensive approach, and the more comprehensive the data set, the more accurate are our interpretations.

Part III

Additional Techniques

# 35

# Sampling for Soil Micromorphology

Georges Stoops<sup>1</sup> and Cristiano Nicosia<sup>2</sup>

Vakgroep Geologie, Faculteit Wetenschappen, Universiteit Gent, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Centre de Recherches en Archeologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Belgium

# 35.1 Introduction

By definition, micromorphology is the study of undisturbed soil materials with a microscope. The utmost care must therefore be taken to produce undisturbed samples. Three principal steps have to be considered: (i) sampling strategy (which samples to take and where to take them); (ii) sampling technique and instruments; (iii) transport and preservation of samples. Part of the following text is based on Stoops (1978). Other accounts on how to collect samples for micromorphology in archaeological contexts can be found in Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Cruise 2000; Goldberg & Macphail 2003.

# 35.2 Sampling Strategy

For physical and chemical routine analyses, homogeneous bulk samples are necessary that represent, as closely as possible, the mean horizon characteristics. This, however, is not always true for micromorphology in general, and for archaeological soil micromorphology in particular.

In pedology, in order to determine the average characteristics of a horizon, a homogeneous sample is required, best taken in the middle of the horizon itself (Figure 35.1a). On the other hand, when sharp boundaries occur between horizons, sampling across boundaries, might be more informative (Figure 35.1b). A third possibility is collecting samples from specific features of interest, such as a certain nodule type, a tongue, and so forth (Figure 35.1c).

In archaeological contexts, the sampling strategy depends greatly on the specific feature(s) intended for analysis but even more on the question(s) asked of the soil micromorphologist. Proper questions are the necessary starting point for any meaningful micromorphological analysis carried out in an archaeological context.

This is especially true if one considers that archaeological stratigraphy does not rely exclusively on 'natural' - hence to some degree 'predictable' or 'replicable' - processes but also reflects 'the historical and social particularities of the people who created it' (Lucas 2012). Ideally, the sampling should be carried out by the micromorphologist who will study the thin sections, so that he/she can get an idea of the characteristics of the site. Micromorphology should not be considered as a stand-alone technique but rather as one of the different scales of observation of, or one of the available tools in, a multiscale geoarchaeological study. The questions for the soil micromorphologist should derive from discussion with the archaeologists and the other specialists involved in excavation (see for example Courty 2001; Macphail & Cruise 2001). The micromorphologist must be in charge of deciding where to take the samples, how to collect them, and how large they should be. The question of how many samples to collect is answered easily: the more the better, given time limits (especially in rescue archaeological contexts). It can be decided later which samples will be transformed in thin sections, based on the available budget and the research questions at the end of an excavation campaign. If properly stored (see section 35.5), samples can last a very long time and can be used to answer questions arising many years after the end of an archaeological project (see for example the study by Cremaschi & Ottomano 1996 on a stratigraphic column conserved for around a century in a museum). Moreover, undisturbed monoliths can also be used for analyses other than soil micromorphology.

Where samples should be taken is a difficult question that can only be answered case by case. All spots of interest should be sampled, including adequate coverage of the vertical stratigraphy and sometimes the lateral changes. In some cases, samples can be collected along the whole thickness of a layer or along a complete

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/422033384ddfe636c9e4ff813c8b3bf7a0d147509127a0ad745742c4ed87fe64.jpg)  
Figure 35.1 Ideal position of samples for pedology studies (modified from Stoops 1978).

succession of layers. This is the often the case when the aim is a detailed reconstruction of many superimposed events, represented by microstratified or laminated deposits (e.g., some caves sequences, combustion structures, superimposed floors, or certain waterlogged contexts). The whole thickness of dark earth units is normally sampled for micromorphology, as internal differentiations are most often invisible to the naked eye (see Nicosia et al. 2017, this book). In other cases, when for example questions concern only a general assessment of the composition of a layer, an 'average' sample in the middle, similarly to sampling in pedology (see above), will suffice. In other cases samples might need to be collected not only in the layer of interest but also from underlying ones, as for example in tilled horizons (where textural pedofeatures might form at depth below an ancient Ap horizon – see Deak et al. 2017, this book), or when assessing processes such as decarbonation, leaching or secondary mineral formation. The choice of the position of samples is therefore a very delicate step in an archaeological micromorphology study and should be dealt with by a trained specialist.

The size of the samples depends upon the aim of the study, the possibilities of the thin section workshop and budget considerations, taking into account that larger thin sections are much more expensive than small ones. When large blocks are sampled, it is possible to use only part of them for the thin section preparation and the remaining part for other analyses. For the study of structure and stratigraphy large thin sections are needed, whereas for materials (e.g., mortars, pottery, bones) petrographic sections  $(48 \times 28\mathrm{mm})$  are sufficient and, moreover, are easier to handle on a petrographic microscope.

For quantitative research, replicates may be necessary; either several thin sections of the same impregnated block, or one thin section of several samples taken in the same horizon or layer. Murphy & Banfield (1978) concluded that at least six samples with one thin section of  $7.5 \times 6\mathrm{cm}$  per sample are needed in quantitative porosity studies. In a similar study, Millfred et al. (Milfred et al. 1967) concluded that 21 cores of  $100\mathrm{cm}^3$  with two thin sections per core are needed for a statistically acceptable analysis of the six main components by point counting.

In the case of budgetary constraints, the best choice has to be made, trying to take samples illustrating more than one feature or point of interest. Care should ideally be taken to have an overlap of at least  $1\mathrm{cm}$  between successive samples, in order to obtain a complete profile section. A photograph of the sampling site, if possible with the sample boxes still in situ, or with an outline marked with the trowel on the profile is always very useful to reconstruct the original context. Samples should also be indicated on profile drawings, positioned by means of total station and recorded on stratigraphic unit forms, if these are compiled. This not only allows any researchers to know the exact position of each sample but, very importantly, facilitates communication with the others involved in an excavation.

Bulk samples for additional analysis (e.g., chemical, mineralogical and palynological) have to be taken in close association with micromorphological ones, so that they can be correlated with the thin section analysis.

# 35.3 Taking Samples in the Field

Most archaeological soils, sediments and associated earth materials are soft and not very coherent. Taking undisturbed samples therefore requires special techniques. A standard one is the use of so-called 'Kubiëna' boxes or tins, named after W. L. Kubiëna, the spiritual father of soil micromorphology, who invented this sampling method (Kubiëna 1953, not 1938 as sometimes mentioned). The original Kubiëna box is made of bladed aluminium, later also stainless steel, brass or zinc were used (Figures 35.2, 35.3 and 35.4). Its standard size is  $80\mathrm{mm}$  long,  $65\mathrm{mm}$  wide and  $40\mathrm{mm}$  thick, but measures vary according to the dimensions of the thin sections expected to be made. Both the upper and the lower cover can be removed and the sides are closed with a hinge that can be opened to allow an easy removal of the sample without pushing. Larger boxes of  $150\times 80\times 50\mathrm{mm}$  were used by several laboratories preparing so-called mammoth thin sections (Jongerius & Heintzberger 1975).

To take a sample, one cover is removed and the open box pressed into the soil. One should never try to force the box in the soil with a hammer as this may both

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/119f5397c180de3312f59a86dec92fdfc91cb2ccb8229a1d4c1ec8bae2d4d774.jpg)  
Figure 35.2 Kubiëna boxes about to be gently pressed into a profile cutting through Iron Age plough marks.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/270d7137fb5e97d1d594c2ce674418445bd7ee4142b7d61ed3c89091b03a77e7.jpg)  
Figure 35.3 A Kubiëna box pressed into soft, laminated archaeological sediments and ready to be extracted after lid is placed.

damage the box and the microfabric of the soil by vibration. In a soil pit, a car jack, placed between the box and the opposite wall, can be used to force the box in the profile wall. When the material is too hard or compact to introduce the box just by hand pressure, a deep groove needs to be cut along the sides of the box. A pair of scissors is useful in the case of surface horizons with roots. During the introduction of the box into the soil,

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3909cfb63c3ee032848759bfbbe18185e27bad6f4e37f6d10cdbcfe39344aa40.jpg)  
Figure 35.4 Kubiëna boxes pressed into soft organic sediments and with their lids on. Samples are collected in close conjunction with continuous columns for archaeobotanical and chemical analyses. Note that label and orientation are marked directly on the lid of the boxes. They should also be noted on the frame, as covers are often removed to dry the sample.

fragments may separate and obstruct the lower part of the box. They should be removed before continuing pressing the box in the soil, by opening the second cover. Once the box is completely filled, it is removed from the profile wall by means of a knife or a spade put behind it. The excess of soil should not be pushed in the box, as this will certainly alter the microstructure, but it should be cut off carefully with a knife. When the box cannot be introduced deeply enough so as to be completely filled, the empty space should be filled with cotton wool (or in the field provisionally with dried grass, moss, or crumpled paper).

Samples can also be collected by simply digging out or 'carving' a block of the desired size from the profile (Figures 35.5 and 35.6). The block should be infolded first in paper (either toilet paper, Scottex, or newspaper) and then wrapped tight with tape until it feels very firm. Paper is preferable to aluminium or plastic film as it absorbs part of the water present in the sample. Another option is to place the carved-out monolith in a

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b65d8a1b03497c54bb57b99ea8dc8ba02e84b752b651423f5dab3cf6014cd178.jpg)  
Figure 35.5 Blocks of coherent archaeological sediments delimited by grooves and ready to be carved out of a profile. The samples can be then wrapped in paper and scotch tape. Once wrapped, the label and the orientation should be marked on the sample itself. Note the close conjunction with samples for pollen and geochemistry, marked with green and red labels respectively.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b658ce708d48bf81b145204427ceea61052dad8ce02f1e306441d9fb40339429.jpg)  
Figure 35.6 If archaeological sediments are coherent, a block can be easily carved out of the profile. The block can then be placed in a plastic box or wrapped with paper and scotch tape and labelled.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/7b2495c0d2f550252b4a437a05b317c4c4cde771482923f89822f73cec29b2fc.jpg)  
Figure 35.7 Blocks of different sizes carved out of a profile and placed in plastic boxes. Note that labels (sample name and orientation) are written on the boxes themselves. Image courtesy of Y. Devos and B. Wouters.

plastic box. Care should be always taken to mark the 'way up' of the sample and its name on the box (Figure 35.7). The boxes can be then eventually wrapped in paper and tape. Carving is an ideal technique when materials are rich in gravel or in artefacts (as is often the case in archaeology), are particularly firm, or have a strongly separated soil structure. Josephs & Bettis (2003) describe, as an alternative to the classical Kubiëna boxes, the use of nonmetallic outlet and switch boxes (after removal of nail hooks). These boxes are pushed or hammered in the soil. Note that vibrations can be harmful for the

microstructure. As samples are difficult to remove from these boxes, they are generally impregnated within the boxes. Another alternative is proposed by Goldberg & Macphail (2003). For obtaining undisturbed monolith type samples they use square section PVC downpipe. The pipe is cut in convenient lengths, easy to transport (e.g., 10 to  $40\mathrm{cm}$ ). The complete pipe can be pushed or hammered vertically in the soil. Cutting one side yields a pipe with a U profile that can be cut into the wall with a knife, and is especially useful when large soil columns are collected (Figure 35.8).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ca562976b22f207d146850b7514891c5d976cf6df362839f7015728b435c7ba9.jpg)  
Figure 35.8 A large monolith delimited by grooves and ready to be carved out of the profile. To avoid breakage, a plastic wireway for electrical cables was used to hold the sample and it was wrapped with paper and scotch tape. Large monoliths can be then be subsampled for other analyses.

Noncoherent samples (such as loose sands or ash) are difficult to sample when dry, even with boxes. It can sometimes be helpful to moisten them. Several methods have been proposed to stabilize the material prior to sampling. Goldberg (1976) proposed stabilizing clods of fragile materials by applying drops of water glass (sodium silicate solution) from an eye dropper over the entire surface. After drying in the sun (for about  $1\mathrm{h}$ ) the sample is sufficiently hard to be packed. Only the outer rim of the clad will be hardened in this way, leaving the entire interior for impregnation in the laboratory. Another consolidation of samples in situ can be achieved with a  $0.5\%$  cellulose acetate solution in acetone, applied several times to the profile wall. The solution is prepared by dissolving small pieces of cellulose in acetone (Murphy 1986). It can be atomized on a vertical wall of the profile, or dripped on a horizontal platform. After evaporation of the acetone, the surface of the sample will be stable.

In very dry soils, stabilization in the field may be possible by impregnating the material in situ with diluted polystyrene (as used for impregnation of samples in the laboratory, but a shorter hardening time), as proposed by Bisdom (1967). This provisional impregnation may, however, later hinder the final impregnation in the laboratory. For the same purpose, Prone (1976) used a modified resin and propylene oxide, both mixable with water. Obtaining these products is however not easy.

Blevins et al. (1968) described a method for fixing the samples in the field by freezing with liquid nitrogen  $(-78^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  that is poured into small holes surrounding the sample location. The sample needs to be transported in frozen condition to the laboratory. Apart from the technical problems, disruption of the sample by ice crystallization cannot be excluded.

For the study of soil surfaces (e.g., a ploughed surface) FitzPatrick et al. (1985) recommend the use plaster of Paris: a quarter mixture of water and plaster of Paris is slowly poured in a metallic frame partly inserted into the soil surface. After hardening, the frame containing the soil and the plaster crust can be excavated and packed.

Another possibility for particularly loose and/or delicate materials is to wrap the sample in gypsum bandages, which are made of cotton embedded in plaster of Paris, and are easily available in pharmacies (Figures 35.9 and 35.10). They need to be wetted, wrapped around the sample and left to dry. In very wet environmental conditions the hardening could be problematic. If pre-embedded bandages are not available, rough textile dripped in a plaster of Paris solution can be used for the same purpose. Small amounts of acrylic polymer (e.g., Polyfilla) can be added to increase hardness (Goldberg & Macphail 2003).

# 35.4 Labelling

Correct labelling of each sample is of primary importance. Two pieces of information are important:

- The number of the sample, corresponding to the number(s) given in the field description, drawings or photographs. In order to facilitate communication with the excavation team and with other specialist, the stratigraphic unit number of the sampled layer can be indicated on the sample. Unnecessary long or complicated sample labels should be avoided, as they make transcription mistakes during or after processing more likely.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/597bb3d5ebd20e65d57c83c848a297f993608f812de6051430d9308500e7adbb.jpg)  
Figure 35.9 Samples delimited by grooves and wrapped in gypsum bandages. Middle Paleolithic site of El Salt (Spain). Image courtesy of C. Mallol.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/7325c20615c0fd125e5ae66b2c74477a564ea17cae966936a6c9f5ce2356fad9.jpg)  
Figure 35.10 A large monolith wrapped in gypsum bandages and ready to be extracted. Note that sample name and orientation are marked directly on the sample. Olduvai Gorge, Tanzania.

- The exact orientation. The latter can be indicated by the words 'top' and 'bottom', or by an arrow pointing to the top written directly on the sample. An arrow can be scratched on blocks made of coherent but soft material. It is important to use a water-resistant marker and to mark label and orientation on more than one face of

the sample, as vapours during processing can cause the writing to disappear. Kubiëna boxes are best labelled on the side, not on one of the covers, as the latter often will be removed in the laboratory for drying.

# 35.5 Transport and Preservation

The influence of transport on sample quality is frequently underestimated especially when samples were taken in remote areas.

# 35.5.1 Preparing Samples for Transport

# 35.5.1.1 Monitoring the Moisture Content

For specific types of samples, preservation or regulation of the moisture content is very important. When relatively loose samples (e.g., sandy materials) become dry before or during transport, the cohesion between the particles may be lost, in which case only a loose stuctureless material will be left. Samples containing hygroscopic salts have to be kept at the original moisture content to avoid mineralogical changes by absorption or a loss of crystal water. For instance, mirabilite  $\mathrm{(Na_2SO_4.10H_2O)}$  can be transformed to thenardite  $\mathrm{(Na_2SO_4)}$  or vice versa.

Transporting oversaturated soil material should be avoided, as vibrations may destroy the fabric, especially in the case of thixotropic material. Controlled drying to acceptable moisture content to enhance cohesion will be necessary but care should be taken that the sample does not become completely dry. Complete drying of clayey samples will cause artificial cracks and often a considerable shrinkage of the material. In the case of peat, fragments of leather etc., drying may result not only in a change of the fabric, but also in changes of the optical characteristics as the material becomes almost opaque after air or oven drying.

To keep the desired moisture content, sampled blocks and Kubiëna boxes have to be wrapped in plastic sheets

or long plastic bags and closed hermetically. This will also avoid oxidation (e.g., in the case of hydromorphic soils).

# 35.5.1.2 Precautions against Biological

# Degradation

The presence of mesofauna in a sample may result after some days in the total loss of microstructure. This is especially the case for samples rich in organic material. Fungi may also cause problems. It might therefore be useful to add a few drops of fungicide or formalin to the samples before wrapping them.

# 35.5.2 Packing and Shipping

When samples have to be shipped (or transported by car on a bumpy road) it is important that they are firmly packed in strong (by preference wooden or metal) boxes. To increase stability, crumpled paper can be placed between the individual samples. Care should be taken to put the most stable samples (e.g., rocks, indurated horizons, Kubiëna boxes) at the bottom, the weakest at the top. Do not make the boxes too heavy to handle.

collection of samples for micromorphological analysis. Geoarchaeology 18, 567-570.  
Kubiëna, W. L. (1953) The Soils of Europe. Thomas Murby & Co., London.

# Fluorescence Microscopy

Georges Stoops

Vakgroep Geologie, Faculteit Wetenschappen, Universiteit Gent, Belgium

# 36.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

The absorption of radiation and subsequent emission of light with a longer ('warmer') wavelength is called fluorescence or phosphorescence. In the case of fluorescence the reradiation is almost simultaneous; in the case of phosphorescence the emission persists longer after the excitation. In earth sciences, primary fluorescence or autofluorescence is mostly used – the fluorescence emitted by the material as such. In biological and medical sciences secondary fluorescence is more commonly used: fluorescence emitting substances, called fluochromes, are selectively absorbed on specific substances making their detection easier using UV or blue light. For porosity studies in soils and rocks a fluochrome is mixed to the impregnating resin, making distinction between solid material and voids clearly visible for image analysis.

# 36.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedure

In material sciences only (oblique) incident radiation (called epifluorescence) of short wavelengths (ultraviolet or UV, violet, blue) is used, whereas in medical and biological sciences transmitted blue or UV light is more commonly used. To obtain a sufficient strong irradiation a mercury burner (HBO) of  $50 - 200\mathrm{W}$ , or a xenon burner (XBO) of  $75 - 100\mathrm{W}$  is needed. The required wavelength is isolated with the help of an excitation filter, blocking all but the selected exciting wavelength, or, rarely a prism monochromator. In order to observe the fluorescence a suppression or barrier filter is used, blocking the exciting wavelength and thus separating the object emission from the reflected excitation light. If the white light of

the mercury or xenon burner is used to study the thin section in OIL, it is most important to insert a sufficiently thick colourless UV stop filter to protect the eyes.

Epifluorescence microscopes are equipped with the necessary light source and a set of excitation and suppression filters. Most research microscopes can be transformed for fluorescence studies by adding accessories, as far as the microscope has the possibilities for (oblique) incident light illumination. Most HBO and XBO lamp houses are equipped with filter holders to contain excitation filters. The suppression filters fit in a filter turret or filter holder in the microscope tube between the sample and the ocular system. Special accessory units containing both types of filters in revolving fittings are commercially available for some microscopes. For more technical details the reader is referred to technical manuals such as Ploem & Tanke (1987) and Nikon (1990).

To obtain the best results, the right match between excitation and suppression filters is of primary importance (Table 36.1).

Although epifluorescence microscopy can be applied to fresh fracture surfaces, better results are obtained on polished impregnated blocks, uncovered thin sections and even glass-covered thin sections. If, in the case of uncovered thin sections, oil is applied to the surface to enhance contrast and colour, care should be taken to select a nonfluorescent oil. Fluochromes added to the impregnating resin (e.g., to study porosity) or autofluorescence of the resin (e.g., in the case of some polystyrene resins) may interfere with the autofluorescence of solid components.

It is recommended that work should be carried out in a relative dark room to avoid the interference of light reflections on the thin section and for a better observation of the often weak emissions. Not all digital microscope cameras are sufficiently sensitive to make pictures of weak autofluorescence.

A special case of fluorescence is so-called cathodoluminescence (CL). Instead of incident UV or blue light, a beam of electrons is used to excite the sample. This requires special equipment to be mounted on the microscope table. A metal vacuum chamber containing the electron gun and sample holder is equipped with two glass windows, one at the bottom to allow light to enter for observations in transmitted light and

Table 36.1 Combination of excitation and suppression filters.  

<table><tr><td>Exciting radiation</td><td>Excitation filter (nm)</td><td>Suppression filter (nm)</td></tr><tr><td>Ultraviolet</td><td>330–380</td><td>420</td></tr><tr><td>Blue violet</td><td>400–440</td><td>480</td></tr><tr><td>Blue</td><td>420–490</td><td>520</td></tr></table>

one at the top for observation. The chamber is connected to a vacuum pump and a high voltage source furnishing the power for the electron gun. Two types of guns are available, the relatively weak 'cold' gun, sufficient however for the study of carbonates, phosphates, and so forth, and the 'hot' gun necessary for the study of silicates (mainly quartz) grains. Some SEM systems or microprobes are equipped with an optical CL device.

Thin sections should be uncovered and of small size (generally petrographic size, i.e.  $48 \times 28\mathrm{mm}$ ), as the sample holder does not allow larger sections to be introduced.

Cathodoluminescence intensity is a function of the concentration of the activator 'impurity' in the crystal lattice but other elements, called quenchers (e.g., Fe) have an opposite role and reduce or inhibit the luminescence (Marchal 1988; Barbin & Schvoerer 1997).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b6ced1a206e025ed6381fc650dce9ce52e799bed6f34de3590a2d36eda66b1d3.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a4be26e8863b633b0c32ccbeeb114319f7da64f181b1d35ca6cebd27866abb3b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ce64e86c47ce1e9756ec10050cdaddbca9a3876825c5ee2a7e4a6f6b7970eaab.jpg)  
Figure 36.1 Bone (left) and shell (right) fragments. (a) PPL; (b) XPL; (c) UV light autofluorescence; (d) blue light autofluorescence. Medieval dark earth, Ghent, Belgium.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/561b81d777d8012ac8d61bae7f67e50fee1db50f2e6ad3a7307f14708950110c.jpg)

# 36.3 Applications in Archaeology

The use of fluorescence microscopy in soil and regolith micromorphology is summarized in Stoops (2003). More details are given in Van Vliet-Lanoë (1980). Application to archaeological materials is very promising and is discussed in general by Macphail et al. (2004) and Macphail & Goldberg (2010). For illustrations, the reader is referred to the cited chapters in this book.

- Many phosphate minerals show autofluorescence. The technique is very useful in the detection of bone fragments dispersed in the deposits (Villagran et al. 2017, this book), of excrements of carnivores or omnivores (Nicosia 2008; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010; Brönniman et al. 2017a, this book; Karkanas 2017, this book). Bones give a greenish yellow fluorescence when excited with blue light and a light-blue fluorescence with UV light (Figure 36.1), influenced by degree and type of preservation.  
Calcium carbonates (calcite, aragonite) are frequently fluorescent, the colour and intensity changing in function of the activators present (e.g., Mn). The technique therefore can help to distinguish different layers in nodules or coatings, for instance calcite coatings on pottery or rock fragments (Dravis & Yurewiz 1985; Khormali et al. 2006 - Figure 36.2). Calcite phytoliths are also often fluorescent (Altemüller & Van Vliet-Lanoë 1990). According to the experience of the author, fluorescence of shells can change after heating. This topic needs however further detailed study.  
- The use of fluorescence for the study of organic materials in soil thin sections was discussed by Babel (1972) and Altemüller & Van Vliet-Lanoë (1990). Lignified tissue has a strong brown colour with UV excitation; cutinized and suberized cells and also hyphen have a whitish colour with UV; parachymatic tissues emit a brownish or reddish colour when excited with blue light. Young cells in root tips and root hairs display a blue colour in UV. Humification changes fluorescence colours and intensities of organic tissues (e.g., in lignified tissues fluorescence colour changes gradually to whitish and will finally disappear). Moreover, humic compounds mask autofluorescence. This can be overcome partly by treating the uncovered thin section with sodium hypochlorite to bleach the humic components (Babel 1964; Stoops 2003). In archaeology, it is used especially for the study of plant remains (Ismail-Meyer 2017, this book) and herbivore excrements (Bronniman et al. 2017b, this book).  
- Phytoliths can be fluorescent under specific conditions, for instance in Podzols and volcanic ash soils (Van Vliet-Lanoë 1980).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/eba7a90e2547ab728cb69a6db1fdcb00d2037783dc27b0e9fdf8ba4f59e003a1.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a84955852b0e5924777cff19ff5768e4c7d2cca1b00d40f2e58e688b84018861.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/36166d93975f00e34c15c7587c112fe435449fe9f19b58a6d558525bf94e30e1.jpg)  
Figure 36.2 Fragments of marble (m), limestone (l) and calcareous cement (c) in stucco. (a) PPL; (b) XPL; (c) blue light autofluorescence. Note the almost absence of fluorescence in limestone and marble fragments, and the strong fluorescence in the calcareous cement. The air bubble (on the lower part of the limestone fragment) shows strong internal reflection of the fluorescence light. Romanagna, Pessinus (Turkey - see Stoops 1984).

Cathodoluminescence has been used in soil micromorphology mainly for the study of carbonates, as it often allows to distinguish lithogenic from pedogenic calcite features (e.g., Khormali et al. 2006; Wright & Peeters 1989), or different gentic types of nodules. In geology a much wider use has been made, as for example to distinguish between igneous and metamorphic quartz. In archaeology the use of cathodoluminescence is still limited (Schvoerer et al. 1986), having been employed in the study of building materials (Barbin et al. 1992), mortars, coprolites (Le Bailly et al. 2006). As also shells show a different CL signal depending upon the environment of growth (Barbin 1997), the method could be useful for detection of shells of different origins.

# 36.4 Concluding Remarks

Fluorescence microscopy is a simple method, requiring only some accessories to adapt a research microscope. It is therefore quite frequently used in archaeological micromorphology, mainly to detect vegetal residues and phosphatic remains. More research is needed for instance on its use to identify fibres (e.g. from textiles), and the influence of fire and burning on bones, shells and other materials. Its use in research on bricks, tiles, pottery and mortars and plasters is almost nonexistent.

Although cathodoluminescence is a relatively common technique in geoarchaeology, its use in micromorphology is still restricted. It might be a powerful tool in the study of carbonates, phosphates and so forth but has been strongly underused up to now.

# Gas Chromatography – Mass Spectrometry (GC/MS)

Lisa-Marie Shillito

School of History, Classics and Archaeology, Faculty of Humanities and Social Sciences, Newcastle University, United Kingdom

# 37.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

Gas chromatography – mass spectrometry (GC/MS) is a technique that combines gas chromatography (GC), which separates the components of a mixture, and mass spectrometry (MS), which identifies specific individual components of that mixture. The gas chromatograph consists of a thin capillary column selected for its specific dimensions (length, diameter, film thickness) and phase properties. Molecules in the GC are retained by the column and come off at different times, known as retention times. The resulting chromatograph is a plot of time against relative intensity of the different molecules, with each peak representing a different molecule. When used by itself, identification by GC is based entirely on the retention time of compounds; however, it is not as powerful for complex samples as multiple compounds may have similar retention times. The retention time depends largely on the difference in chemical properties between substances in a sample and their affinity for the column. Substances with a greater affinity stay on the column for longer. When combined with the MS, each individual analyte is broken into ionized fragments, which are detected using their mass-to-charge ratio,  $\mathrm{m/z}$ . This process enables identification of compounds based on their total mass and unique fragmentation pattern, or mass spectrum (Figure 37.1). For further reading on GC see Fowlis (1995), which deals with the theory of gas chromatography and application of the methods, and McMaster (2008) which discusses GC/MS, including equipment maintenance, fundamentals of the technique and a wide range of applications. More basic overviews for archaeologists can be found in Pollard et al. (2007) and Pollard & Heron (2008). For a review of organic biomarker analysis in archaeology, see Evershed (2008).

# 37.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedure

The method for extracting organic residues varies depending on the type of molecules expected. The most basic procedure is a straightforward solvent extraction, either by sonication, Soxhlet extraction or accelerated solvent extraction (ASE). These are different methods for removing the organic residue from the soil/sediment matrix. Further procedures can be conducted to split this total extract into separate chemical groups. The extract may also need to be chemically altered to make it more volatile and suitable for analysis by GC/MS. Insoluble fractions may be investigated using pyrolysis GC/MS (Py/GC/MS), where the soil/sediment is directly combusted without prior extraction.

The advantage of GC/MS is its specificity. Unlike other geochemical techniques, GC/MS results provide an absolute value in that the specific organic molecule present is identified. This is based on the unique mass spectrum of the molecule. Identification is made by comparing the spectra with a mass spectral library, though the identification can be achieved by hand with knowledge of fragmentation patterns.

Analysis is carried out on loose sediment, which should be collected from the micromorphology block prior to resin impregnation, or directly adjacent, to enable direct comparison between microscopic observation and GC/MS data. Alternatively, the block could be split and one part stored for future subsampling. Normally the thin section should be studied first, to target layers that would benefit from further analysis by GC/MS. The larger the sample, the easier the analysis, as a greater quantity of residue can be extracted. Smaller sample sizes may produce insufficient residue to quantify. A minimum of  $1\mathrm{g}$  is recommended for materials such

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/eba8a7bca122cae6a036ce96b8e5c24867680e2bd49bd8d4835591f9c59156e4.jpg)  
(a)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a362f6cc41814c059252f6febed52484b1d45117c19ddbffce5aa14b6a3b6156.jpg)  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a943a8dc0476cbd140ff704a95341e9ed2d6bed87c9b74a31267e187652b1874.jpg)  
Figure 37.1 (a) GC trace showing sterols extracted from lenses of animal dung deposits in Building 1 at the Neolithic site of Sheik-e Abad (adapted from Matthews et al. 2014). The sterol distribution indicates that this is ruminant faeces, supported by the lack of lithocholic acid and small quantities of deoxycholic bile acids. 1. coprostanol 2. epi-coprostanol 3. cholesterol 4.  $5\alpha$ -cholestanol 5.  $5\beta$ -stigmastanol 6. sitoserol 7.  $5\alpha$ -stigmastanol. (b) Micrograph associated with the animal dung deposits, showing amorphous organic matrix containing phytoliths. PPL. (c) Same as (b), XPL showing calcareous spherulites. Image courtesy of S. Elliot.  
(c)

as coprolites or humic soils where residues are more concentrated. For mineral soils and sediment samples  $10\mathrm{g}$  is recommended. For Py/GC/MS  $1 - 3\mathrm{mg}$  is sufficient. A good example discussing different methods for residue extraction from soils is the organic residue study of the Rothamsted Classical Experiments (van Bergen et al. 1997; Bull et al. 1998). Careful sampling of microlayers can provide extremely high resolution results; for example, microsampling of alternate layers of trampled sediments, identified in thin sections of early Neolithic deposits in the Central Zagros (Figure 37.1), revealed alternating deposits of human and animal faeces (Shillito et al. 2013b). In this sense the method is destructive but sampling can be carried out directly from blocks before impregnation, so does not add any further to the destruction from production of a thin section.

Results are semiquantitative. Analysis should be carried out using a reference standard, a known amount of which is added during residue extraction. Recovered values can then be compared to this reference to provide

an estimate of relative concentrations. The reference standard should have a similar composition to the molecules under investigation.

The major limitation of the technique is that it is time consuming and expensive and requires a relatively specialized laboratory setup. Interpretation of results requires an understanding of organic geochemistry. Although specific molecules are identified, this does not always mean a straightforward identification, as some organic compounds can occur in many different materials, for example cholesterol. GC/MS is also limited to volatile compounds, though a range of other chromatographic techniques, such as liquid chromatography (LC/MS), are available for other types of compounds.

When sampling, precautions should be taken to avoid contamination, for example with organic residues present on the hands but also from the sampling kit. Plastics are particularly prone to contaminating GC traces, and samples should preferably be stored in glass jars, paper bags and/or wrapped in foil. Although peaks from

plastics are easily identifiable, they can potentially overlap with compounds of interest and 'swamp' the spectra. In the laboratory, all equipment should be furnaced and solvent rinsed before use.

# 37.3 Archaeological Applications

Gas chromatography - mass spectrometry can be used whenever it is necessary to identify specific organic components within soil or sediment, which are ambiguous under the microscope. These may include human and animal faeces, waxes and resins, combustion products and the composition of decayed plant remains for example. The combination of organic geochemical identification with microcontextual information in thin section is a powerful means of investigating formation processes of

The identification of absolute biomarkers is especially important in cases where ambiguities arise in visual identification, for example species of coprolite. # Isotope Mass Spectrometry (On Microdrilled Powders)

Susan M. Mentzer<sup>1,2,3</sup>

$^{1}$  Institute for Archaeological Sciences, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany  
$^{2}$  School of Anthropology, University of Arizona, Tucson, USA  
3 Senckenberg Centre for Human Evolution and Paleoenvironment, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany

# 38.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

Many analytical techniques – beyond optical petrography – can be applied to micromorphological samples, which include thin sections and resin-impregnated sediment blocks. A relatively recent development, the use of stable isotope mass spectrometry on powders drilled from archaeological sediment blocks was first reported in Mentzer & Quade (2013).

Analyses of the stable isotopes of carbon and oxygen in carbonate minerals have many applications in archaeological science. Isotope ratios, normalized to an international standard, have been measured in cave speleothems, and lacustrine, soil and biogenic carbonates for the purposes of paleoenvironmental reconstruction in or near archaeological sites (Cerling & Quade 1993; Sikes 1994; Frumkin et al. 1999; Leng & Marshall 2004; McDermott 2004; Hodell et al. 2005; Pustovoytov et al. 2010; Quade et al. 2014); in wood ashes for the purposes of identification as well as understanding fuel sources and combustion temperatures (Shahack-Gross et al. 2008; Shahack-Gross & Ayalon 2013); in lime plasters to understand production sequences (Kosednar-Legenstein et al. 2008); in marble and limestone for sourcing studies (Herz 1992; Wenner & Herz 1992; Attanasio et al. 2006) and in enamel carbonates for reconstructing hominin subsistence behaviours (Sponheimer & Lee-Thorp 1999). Each of these types of studies takes advantage of the fact that the relative abundances of isotopes of carbon and oxygen vary in predicable ways due to equilibrium and kinetic fractionation (Sharp 2007).

Typically, geoarchaeologists wishing to conduct both micromorphological analyses and stable isotopic measurements collect separate samples. Loose samples of carbonates or other materials can be collected in the vicinity

of an oriented sediment block or can be removed from its exposed face prior to resin impregnation. Micromorphological observations then provide general information about the sample context. This approach has limitations when the target material is heterogeneous in source, distribution, composition or state of preservation. For example, loose samples collected from a laminated calcareous deposit, such as the ash layer of a multiphase hearth, will likely span several microscopic depositional units. Geochemists working with similar materials, such as speleothems and corals, have developed several microsampling strategies. Microdrilling, which is the targeted removal of several micrograms of solid sample using a very thin drill bit, can be conducted on micromorphology samples. Isotopic analysis of the resulting powder proceeds via a mass spectrometer.

At present, the microdrilling technique described here has not been used to obtain samples for measuring the isotopes of carbon and oxygen in carbonate minerals besides calcite and aragonite, other materials or compounds (e.g., carbon in charcoal, or carbon bound to phosphate groups within enamel), or other isotopic systems (e.g., H, S, N) in an archaeological setting. Whether the microdrilling technique can be used for other systems is dependent on the target material, the method of isotope extraction, and whether this method removes elements from only the target material, or from both the target material and resin. Use of other types of instrumentation for in situ isotopic measurements, such as laser ablation methods that generate gas (e.g., GC-IRMS; see Sharp & Cerling 1996; Spötl & Mattey 2006) may be possible; however the effects of resin on the evolved gas must first be tested. Methods that generate ions (e.g., laser ablation inductively coupled mass spectrometry and laser microprobe mass spectrometry) are not appropriate for silt- and clay-sized particles embedded

in resin, because the instrument will analyse both the sample and the resin. Laser ablation may be used on coarser materials that do not contain resin, such as fragments of limestone or shell.

# 38.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedure

# 38.2.1 Stable Isotopes of Carbon and Oxygen in Carbonates

The basic analytical procedure for measurements of stable isotopic ratios of carbon and oxygen in bulk samples of carbonates is as follows. A subsample of the target material is obtained and ground to a fine powder. The power may be roasted at a temperature of  $250^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  in order to remove organic material. The powder is then reacted with phosphoric acid to yield carbon dioxide  $\left(\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right)$  using either an automated system with a common acid bath, or individual acid baths and gas aliquots. The  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  is fed via an inlet into an isotope ratio gas mass spectrometer (IRMS), which measures, by mass ratio, the abundance of the different isotopic species of  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  distinguished by mass (e.g., 44, 45, 46) relative to those in  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  generated from an internal laboratory standard (measured at the same time if using a dual inlet system). Finally, the abundances of the isotopes are reported in parts per thousand  $(\%)$  using delta notation  $(\delta)$  relative to the external international standard, the Vienna Pee Dee Belemnite (VPDB) formation. This method is applicable, with modification to the reaction conditions, to several types of carbonate compounds and minerals in solid form (Al-Aasm et al.

1990) including calcium carbonate (calcite and aragonite) and calcium magnesium carbonate (dolomite).

# 38.2.2 Microdrilling

The microdrilling technique featured here differs very little from the standard analytical procedure on bulk samples described above. Microdrilling as a method of subsample extraction has been applied widely in studies of speleothems (e.g., Spötl & Mattey 2006). Stabilization of fragile samples in resin is a common step in the isotopic subsampling of corals (e.g., Leder et al. 1996; McConnaughey et al. 1997). In micromorphological samples, a carbonate subsample for isotopic analysis can be obtained by drilling target material directly from a sliced, resin-impregnated sediment block or, for better correlation with micromorphological observations, the tile or chip that was used to produce a thin section (Figure 38.1). The sampling workstation contains a handheld drill, stationary drill, or a digitally automated drilling system (Dettman & Lohman 1995) equipped with various sizes of dental drill bits, as well as an illuminated magnifier. Drilling produces powder that can be captured manually using a scalpel and weighing paper. The drill bit and scalpel should be cleaned chemically prior to collection of subsequent samples and all powder from previous sample holes should be evacuated using compressed air. The procedure is destructive to small areas of the resin-impregnated block or tile (see Figure 38.1a). For a detailed comparison of different sampling techniques, see Spötl & Mattey (2006).

The minimum sample size that can be analysed is dependent on the diameter of the drill bit (typically

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/1e474d134b652d2be00071b8a3176c4845f3864e5fcb74493d2f57229cd6fdfe.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/7c133013ef54657c0781bc364cdb42f93c472aecb8b6a0ebf609ca2eb358397e.jpg)  
Figure 38.1 Isotopic sampling in conjunction with micromorphological analyses. (a) A slice of resin-impregnated, oriented sediment from a cave site in the USA contains the same microstratigraphic units as the thin section in (b), and observations of their characteristics can be used to inform sampling. A series of samples were drilled from the left side of the tile for carbon and oxygen isotopic measurements. The diameters of the holes are approximately  $0.5 - 1\mathrm{mm}$ . (b) The petrographic thin section. Incident light scan.

0.3–0.8 mm), the percentage of carbonate in the resulting powder and the specifications of the instrumentation, including whether the production of the  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  is automated using a common acid bath. In some archaeological samples, drilling large quantities of powder may not be an option. For example, in microlaminated samples, the maximum sample size may be constrained by the individual layer thickness and its lateral extent within the sample. Finally, it should be noted that the target area should be homogeneous in composition to 1–2 mm depth from the drilling surface. Following drilling, the sample may be powdered further if needed, and analyses proceed as above on powders composed of a mixture of carbonate and resin. Mortensen et al. (1998) and Mentzer & Quade (2013) have evaluated the possible contributions of the resin to the evolved gas and have found none.

# 38.2.3 Mass Spectrometry

It is strongly recommended that analyses of microdrilled samples be conducted with an automated preparation system, such as a Kiel Carbonate Device (Thermo Scientific). This type of device is optimized for carbonates, and automates and conducts the acid reactions online (attached directly to the mass spectrometer), small samples (e.g.,  $< 25\mu \mathrm{g}$ ) can be analysed and higher analytical precisions may be attained. In the case of carbonates drilled from resin-impregnated archaeological sediments, the ability to analyse small samples is particularly important, especially when targeting anthropogenic carbonates. Geogenic and biogenic carbonates, such as speleothem fragments, soil carbonates and shells are relatively dense and pure. In contrast, anthropogenic carbonates, such as lime plaster and wood ashes, are typically mixed with other materials and therefore contain lower percentages of carbonate. Furthermore, these materials are porous and a greater proportion of the microdrilled sample will be composed of resin. The results of isotopic analyses of three different types of materials - both microdrilled powders and comparable loose sediment samples - are illustrated in Figure 38.2.

For comparative purposes, several types of carbonate from two archaeological sites were sampled and analysed using different techniques (Figure 38.2). First, calcareous materials from a cave in North America were collected as both loose samples and microdrilled powders. Geogenic carbonates (speleothem fragments and bedrock) contain 84.0 to  $98.4\%$  carbonate. When the same materials are embedded in resin and microdrilled using the procedures described above, the resulting powders contain similar percentages of carbonate due to the high crystal density of the materials. Loose samples of calcareous sediment from the same site contain 18.9 to  $55.6\%$

carbonate due to the presence of inclusions such as organic material and mineral silt. When the same calcareous sediment is embedded in resin and microdrilled as described above, the resulting powder contains 5.8 to  $29.4\%$  carbonate, owing to dilution of the material by resin. This difference in carbonate abundance resulted in low precision of some isotopic measurements on the microdrilled powders. In samples with low carbonate abundance, errors were as high as  $0.4\%$ , oxygen. In this case, measurements were conducted on  $100 - 400\mu \mathrm{g}$  of powder using a Finnigan MAT 252 IRMS equipped with a Thermo Scientific Gasbench II continuous flow device but without an autosampler. Resin-impregnated, microdrilled samples exhibit greater overall analytical error than loose samples analysed with the same instrument (see Figure 38.2). Second, a different set of resin-impregnated powder samples from a Neolithic site in Turkey (Mentzer & Quade 2013) were collected from microlaminated calcareous features and were processed using different techniques. The measurements were compared to the cave samples from above. The powders ranged in mass from  $10 - 100\mu \mathrm{g}$  depending on purity. These samples were also analysed using a Finnigan MAT 252 IRMS. However, this instrument was equipped with a Kiel-III device and the resulting measurements have precisions of  $0.1\%$  and  $0.08\%$  for carbon and oxygen. This comparison demonstrates that automated sampling systems provide better precision for resin-impregnated, microdrilled powders when carbonate abundance is low.

# 38.3 Archaeological Applications

Stable carbon and oxygen isotopic analyses of biogenic, geogenic and pedogenic carbonates using mass spectrometry is a well-established field of study. As described above, applications of isotopic analysis to calcareous archaeological materials are also numerous but are typically conducted on loose samples, which limits the ability of micromorphologists to provide information about the context or conditions of preservation. At present, microdrilling of micromorphological samples has been applied to only a few sites, with publication of results limited to Mentzer & Quade (2013). This sampling strategy shows great promise for projects that emphasize a microcontextual approach to the study of anthropogenic deposits (Matthews 2005; Goldberg & Berna 2010). For example, the analyses of samples drilled from plaster floors revealed that the carbon and oxygen isotopic values could readily distinguish between lime and ashes as the sources of carbonate (Mentzer & Quade 2013). In addition, there are many hypothetical situations in which microdrilling of carbonates from resin-impregnated blocks of archaeological sediment might be useful or preferred over other

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/8933ddea081be4de555bff75904068d204fa2f5b6ae396c47cb25265b23aff89.jpg)  
Figure 38.2 Isotopic measurements on both resin-impregnated and loose calcareous sediments from two archaeological sites indicate that the results are comparable. Nineteen microdrilled samples were obtained from Site 1, a limestone cave located in the USA (Sierra Diablo Cave, unpublished data produced by H. Martinez-Dyrzo in collaboration with J. Vasquez and V. T. Holliday). An example of a microdrilled thin section tile from Pleistocene calcareous deposits in this site is illustrated in Figure 38.1(b). Loose samples of the calcareous sediment contain 18.9 to  $55.6\%$  carbonate due to the presence of inclusions such as organic material and mineral silt. When the same calcareous sediment is embedded in resin and microdrilled, the resulting powder contains 5.8 to  $29.4\%$  carbonate, owing to dilution of the material by resin. This difference in carbonate abundance resulted in low precision of some isotopic measurements on the microdrilled powders. Error bars for these samples are larger than the data points. The measurements (light green squares), yielded oxygen and carbon isotopic values that range from  $-10.59$  to  $-0.46$  and  $-12.09$  to  $-5.30\%$  VPDB (with a maximum of  $0.4\%$  error), respectively. In comparison, analyses of five loose sediment samples from the same stratigraphic unit obtained in various locations within the cave yielded oxygen and carbon isotopic values that range from  $-20.09$  to 0.75 and  $-18.07$  to  $-2.95\%$  VPDB, respectively. Both types of samples show a clear and strong positive linear correlation between oxygen and carbon ( $R^2 = 0.9509$  for the microdrilled samples,  $R^2 = 0.9646$  for the loose samples). Two microdrilled samples from thin section tiles from Site 2, a Neolithic tell located in Turkey (Aşkı Lohyuk, data reproduced from Mentzer & Quade 2013) contain calcareous ash (light orange triangles) and exhibit oxygen and carbon isotopic ratios that are similar to those from seven loose sediment samples (dark orange triangles). Likewise, the single measurement on microdrilled lime from Site 2 (light blue diamond) is in good agreement with the results obtained on seven loose samples of lime. The samples from Site 2 were prepared with a Kiel device and ranged in mass from 10 to  $100\mu g$ . In comparison, the samples from Site 1 were prepared without a Kiel device and ranged in mass from 100 to  $400\mu g$ .

methods (Figure 38.3). These situations can arise from bulk sampling limitations, as well as from the presence of strongly laminated sediment, heterogeneous materials or postdepositional alteration.

First, the isotopic subsampling strategy may be constrained to microdrilling of resin-impregnated blocks due to the fact that loose samples were not collected or that the feature of interest is not present in the loose samples. This limitation might arise when conducting new analyses on old, archived samples. In addition, some types of samples are highly cemented by secondary carbonates or silica, which could prevent subsampling of calcareous components using other methods (Figure 38.3d).

Second, complicated microstratigraphy could hinder accurate sampling of individual calcareous layers or features

in the field. Examples of these types of archaeological sediments include plaster layers in a floor (Figure 38.3a), and thin yet discrete layers of calcareous ashes in a combustion feature (Figure 38.3b and c). In these situations, loose samples of the feature's sediment would represent an average of multiple discrete depositional events. Furthermore, vertical sampling for isotopic measurements within seemingly homogenous deposits may reveal chemical laminations that cannot be visually resolved (Figure 38.4).

Third, some types of sediments in archaeological sites are heterogeneous in composition. When dealing with samples that contain several types of calcareous materials, the analyst may choose to utilize the microdrilling technique in order to target or avoid certain materials. Examples of these types of deposits include sediment

Figure 38.3 Proposed archaeological situations in which microsampling guided by micromorphological analyses may be warranted. (a) Multiple discrete layers of calcareous plaster from a building floor (Aşkı Lühyük, Neolithic tell, Turkey). The individual plaster layers (PL) range from  $0.5 - 9\mathrm{mm}$  in thickness. Several layers show strong high-order interference colours in the micromass. The composition of these layers was explored further using micro-FTIR analyses (see Berna 2017, this book), confirming the presence of calcite as a fine fraction component in two of these layers (CalPL), while a third layer did not contain calcite  $(\mathsf{PL}^{*})$ . Additional thin and discontinuous plaster layers are present but not labelled. Using the microdrilling technique, samples of the two thick calcareous plaster layers may be obtained. Furthermore, other types of calcareous materials, such as occupation debris layers (OD) and fill (F) may be avoided. Composite image, XPL. (b) Multiple in situ layers of calcareous ashes (CA) interbedded with layers of mixed materials that include sand-sized fragments of bone in a sequence of Middle Paleolithic combustion features ( $Uççalık/Zl Cave II, Turkey$ ). These layers are not clearly distinguishable in the field. Using microdrilling, samples of each ash layer may be obtained. PPL. (c) Same as (b), XPL. The pure calcareous ash layers exhibit higher order interference colours than the mixed layers. (d) Aggregate of laminated, calcareous ashes (CA) in an ashy matrix impacted by precipitation of secondary carbonate in a brecciated Epipaleolithic deposit from a cave in Turkey ( $Uçalık/Zl Cave I$ ). The oriented block was removed from the site with a hammer and chisel, and loose sediments are therefore not available for isotopic subsampling, but using microdrilling, samples of the aggregate may be obtained. XPL. (e) Locally bioturbated and cemented deposit of Upper Paleolithic calcareous ashes from  $Uçalık/Zl$  Cave I. Areas with the more extensive alteration (ellipse) can be avoided during sampling using thin section studies. XPL.

The approximate location of the vertical sampling transect is indicated by the yellow rectangle over a composite image of the thin section (XPL). (b) Fluctuations in composition are apparent on the plot of isotopic ratios. containing reworked aggregates of ashes (Figure 38.3d), a nodular calcic horizon formed on a colluvial deposit containing older calcite nodules, and lime plaster containing unreacted or partially reacted lime or other inclusions.

Finally, postdepositional alteration can render certain portions of the deposit less desirable for analysis. For example, in some cases the calcareous material of interest may be locally impacted by secondary calcite formation or recrystallization (Figure 38.3e). In cases where these areas are not visible in the field or in hand sample, they may only be avoided using microdrilling. In addition, the analyst may use other types of analyses that provide evidence for alteration prior to isotopic sampling. For example, both cathodoluminescence microscopy and micro-FTIR analyses may be conducted on

# 38.4 Concluding Remarks

At the time of writing, the analysis of stable isotopes in resin-impregnated archaeological sediment samples is in its infancy and a broad range of applications may be imagined but has not yet been realized. In the upcoming years, it is expected that the variety of materials and isotopic systems that can be analysed will increase, as will the power of integration of in situ quantitative geochemical analyses and micromorphology.
