# FTIR Microscopy

Francesco Berna

Department of Archaeology, Simon Fraser University, Burnaby, Canada

# 39.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) is an instrumental technique used to identify the functional groups (e.g., amide, phosphate, carbonate and hydroxyl) present in organic and inorganic compounds by measuring their absorption of infrared radiation over a range of wavelengths (e.g., 50 to  $5000\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$  wavenumber). The method is based on first collecting an interferogram of a sample signal using an interferometer, and then performing a Fourier transform (a mathematical algorithm) on the interferogram to obtain the infrared spectrum. A modern FTIR spectrometer thus collects and digitizes the interferogram, performs the Fourier transform and displays the FTIR spectrum.

The coupling of a FTIR spectrophotometer to an optical microscope produces a system capable of doing FTIR microspectroscopy. This technological achievement has been possible since the early 1980s following the introduction of Fourier transform computer capabilities as well as advances in IR detector technology (Messerschmidt & Harthcock 1988). The success in application of IR microspectrophotometers and FTIR microscopes to many areas of research (semiconductors, polymers, and pharmaceuticals), as well as forensic investigation, has established this technique as a powerful tool in the analysis of small samples (e.g., particles with a diameters less than  $150\mu \mathrm{m}$ ). Researchers in conservation science quickly saw the applications of FTIR microspectroscopy to the analysis of tiny samples from works of art (Derrick et al. 1999). The first published application of IR microspectroscopy was for the analysis of painting materials, done with a dispersive spectrometer by van't Hul Ehrnreich in 1970 (Derrick et al. 1999). Later, the attachment of microscopes to FTIRs stimulated numerous applications for the analysis of paint layers, binding media, varnishes, waxes, dyes, and other

materials (Derrick et al. 1999). Archaeologists followed (Berna & Goldberg 2007; Berna et al. 2012; Prinsloo et al. 2014). To date FTIR spectrometers are fully integrated with optical microscopes into FTIR microscopes (e.g., Thermo-Nicolet Continuum and iN10®, Bruker Hyperion®, Agilent Cary®). Some of these instruments have been connected to synchrotron light sources for the analysis of particles with diameter as small as  $3\mu \mathrm{m}$  (Miller et al. 2003). For more information on the history, design, and operation of IR microspectrophotometers, see Messerschmidt & Harthcock (1988) and Katon et al. (1989-1990).

# 39.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedure

Commercial benchtop FTIR microscopes are built with fully integrated optical and FTIR microscopic capability. This capability is achieved by mounting optical objectives and IR light condensers on the same nosepiece. High-end models are also equipped with polarizers and analysers for transmitted light microscopy. Particles of interest embedded in uncovered petrographic thin sections are centred and focused with the optical microscope objective and then analysed through the FTIR objective (Figure 39.1). Infrared spectra of the particles as small as  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$  can be collected individually or mapped in larger areas with the use of automated stages and dedicated software. Samples can be analysed in transmission, total reflection, and attenuated total reflectance (ATR). The transmission analysis of samples processed in petrographic thin sections is limited to the use of IR spectral bands above  $2400\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ , as the glass slide absorbs the transmitted energy of the IR bands below  $2400\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . When analysed in reflection (total or attenuated), the particle is shined and the reflected and unreflected (absorbed) radiation is measured. In reflection the sample is shined through the

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/9e9cb74e18f0522be022ebbce07ef3cd51b83e2dfea4cbe47a43575b8523e8e1.jpg)  
Figure 39.1 (a) Scan of petrographic thin section (Excavation 1, Wonderwerk Cave, South Africa) being analysed. (b) Screenshot showing transmitted light image of the area being viewed, spot (red cross) being analysed on a specific particle and the FTIR spectra being collected showing the OH-stretching IR absorptions of kaolinite at 3695, 3650 and  $3620\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$  wavenumbers.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/84804caab09d12b3c4dae4aa38f9e21fc469aefa6e0ab454190a9e310db59a66.jpg)

air while in ATR the sample is placed in contact with specially designed high index crystals (e.g., diamond, germanium, zinc selenide) that direct portion of the IR beam (i.e., the evanescent wave) into the sample. Spectra acquired in reflection (total or attenuated) are subject to light scattering phenomena and thus for best results the surface of the thin section needs to be polished with  $1\mu \mathrm{m}$  grit abrading material. Commercial and custom built spectral library are used for the identification of the functional groups and the corresponding compounds (e.g., spectral libraries available at http://www.thermoscientific.com/, accessed 6 March 2017; Kimmel Center for Archaeological Science: http://www.weizmann.ac.il/kimmel-arch/infrared-spectra-library, accessed 6 March 2017; Simon Fraser University Geoarchaeology Lab).

# 39.3 Archaeological Applications

The application of FTIR analysis to archaeological investigation ranges widely from dating, to use of space, ancient pyrotechnologies, diagenesis and transformation of the archaeological record; all major aspects of site formation processes (Weiner 2010). By FTIR it is in fact possible to identify organic and inorganic compounds of archaeological interest. These include proteins (e.g., collagen, albumin), organic polymers (e.g., cellulose, lignin), fatty acids, resins (e.g., ambers), waxes, silicates (e.g., quartz, feldspars, clay minerals), carbonates (e.g., calcite, aragonite, vaterite, dolomite), phosphates (e.g., hydroxyl

apatite, variscite, taranakite, fluorite), sulphates (e.g., gypsum, anhydrite), and nitrates (e.g., nitratite, saltpetre). Fourier transform infrared microscopy enables the researcher to perform FTIR analysis directly on the thin sections of intact archaeological materials and sediments, thus permitting the integration in real time of micromorphological, petrographic, chemical and mineralogical information (Berna & Goldberg 2007; Berna et al. 2012; Poduska et al. 2012; Simms et al. 2012). Fourier transform infrared microscopy normally provides qualitative information, namely presence or absence of a particular mineral (e.g., aragonite) and/or organic compound (e.g., bone collagen) and their general qualitative state (e.g., degree of crystallinity and/or state of preservation and/or thermal alteration). Quantitative analysis is also possible provided that the system sample thin-section instrument is calibrated with standard materials processed in petrographic thin section.

Fourier transform infrared microscopy applied to soil micromorphology is particularly effective for the study of syn- and postdepositional diagenetic processes. In fact, important authigenic minerals such as calcium, aluminium and iron phosphates (e.g., apatite, variscite, taranakite, montgoryite, crandallite, leucophosphite) that are proxies of specific geochemical conditions of formation can be readily identified in thin sections by FTIR microscopy (Figure 39.2). Specifically, calcium phosphates (e.g., carbonate hydroxyl apatite) form in neutral conditions, aluminium and magnesium phosphates (e.g., montgoryite, taranakite, variscite) form

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/daa1d369d5b33e06b3bf2691a7b78068cb06b67013b0570118d02d7493b17bc3.jpg)  
Figure 39.2 (a) Micrograph in PPL of sediment from Manot Cave (Israel) with crystalitic nodules and rounded gravel size speleotheme fragment. (b) Reflection FTIR spectrum of rounded speleothem fragment showing the characteristic IR carbonate absorptions of sparite at 710, 878 and  $1536\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . (c) Reflection FTIR spectrum of rounded crystallitic nodule showing the IR phosphate absorptions of leucophosphite at 971, 1000, 1064,  $1124\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ .

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/91a8c476feb27d29c1c9fe8d9e624f2c6fa23a3745ad384c1c1ca4a3a0435551.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/e4b8cff4e2dd35bb1739884caebf8c04a30d8ed518db668435ebe2d8b5d1cd7e.jpg)

in slightly acid conditions, while iron phosphates (e.g., leucophosphite and crandallite) form in strong acid conditions (Karkanas et al. 2000; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004). Moreover FTIR microscopy provides molecular information suitable for characterizing the state of preservation of bone, ivory, tooth enamel and charcoal (Goldberg & Berna 2010; Weiner 2010). In turn, this evidence provides information on sediment geochemical conditions and site formation processes. Furthermore, FTIR is suitable to estimating temperature transformations of clay minerals, carbonates, phosphates and sulphates (Berna et al. 2007; Berna 2010; Weiner 2010; Weiner et al. 2015). Thus the coupling of FTIR microscopy to the micromorphological analysis of thin sections of archaeological materials, deposits, and features can be used effectively to identify and reconstruct ancient pyrotechnological activities. For instance at Wonderwerk Cave, South Africa, the integration of micromorphology and FTIR microscopy allowed the identification of mmsize heated bone fragments associated with ashed plant materials on stable surfaces containing in situ early Acheulean stone tools (Berna et al. 2012). In fact, upon reaching temperature of  $450 - 500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  the bone mineral (carbonate hydroxyl apatite) transforms into an higher crystalline phase of carbonate hydroxyl apatite with typical FTIR absorptions at 630 and  $3570\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$  (Figure 39.3). Similarly by FTIR microscopy it is possible to estimate

the temperature reached by clay minerals contained in pyrotechnological installations (e.g., furnace walls), discoloured substrata of combustion features and soil particles included in wood ashes. In fact, kaolinite breaks down at temperature of about  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  while smectites and micas break down above  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Farmer 1974). Considering that by FTIR microscopy it is possible to map the presence/absence of kaolinite and mica/smectite it is possible to locate particles and areas that were heated to temperatures below  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , comprised between  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and above  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Such analytical approach has been used for the study of Maya fired clay cooking implements from the Yucatan (Simms et al. 2012). This study identified the local origin of the raw material with inclusion of exotic volcanic glass and firing due to repetitive use in oven pits.

Despite the numerous potentials listed, the use of FTIR microscopy on petrographic thin sections has some limitations. For instance FTIR microscopy is not very well suited for the identification of the different kind of feldspars (e.g., plagioclase, k-feldspars), pyroxenes and iron oxides. For the identification and characterization of the latter class of minerals the use of micro Raman, micro-XRD and microprobe are best suited (see Berthold & Mentzer 2017, this book; Wilson 2017, this book). Another major limitation of using FTIR microscopy on petrographic thin section is the presence of epoxy and

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/710d00818a4fe258a11b10ec1668378c93587146906244e722657b0377b0d583.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/bf9b97952c671b30f7ec26282c5e9e629995fa14f8d9cacacf98f4f1b47d1a70.jpg)  
Figure 39.3 (a) Excavation 1, Wonderwerk Cave (South Africa). (b) Thin section scan from Stratum 10. (c) Micrograph of bone fragment lying on a palaeosurface. (d) Reflection FTIR spectrum of bone fragment shown in (c) showing IR bands at 565, 603, and  $1038\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$  typical of bone carbonate hydroxyl apatite and the additional IR bands at 630 and  $1096\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$  characteristic of bone carbonate hydroxyl apatite heated above  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/77188d3ba9642edad34156a6bf7a82b1cda43f82c91c3d7cb8fcc0983bd5e5bc.jpg)

polyester resins used conventionally for the preparation of the thin sections that compromises the identification of lipids, waxes and other organic residues. For the studies of these compounds in thin-section staining,

radioactive labelling and immunofluorescence techniques are probably best suited but yet only tested on archaeological materials processed in cross-section (Vagnini et al. 2008; Goldenberg et al. 2014).

# 40

# X-ray Microdiffraction  
Christoph Berthold<sup>1</sup> and Susan M. Mentzer<sup>2,3,4</sup>

' Competence Center Archaeometry - Baden-Wuerttemberg (CCA-BW), Applied Mineralogy, Department of Geosciences, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany  
$^{2}$  Institute for Archaeological Sciences, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany  
$^{3}$  School of Anthropology, University of Arizona, Tucson, USA  
4 Senckenberg Centre for Human Evolution and Paleoenvironment, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany

# 40.1 Fundamentals of X-ray Diffraction

# 40.1.1 Overview

In 1912, Max von Laue discovered that crystals serve as three-dimensional diffraction gratings for X-rays because the spacing of atoms in crystal lattices is of the same order of magnitude as the wavelength of X-rays (Friedrich et al. 1912). Bragg & Bragg (1913) formulated the so called 'Bragg law', which correlates the spacing of lattice planes in a crystal with the scattering angle of monochromatic X-rays:

$$
n \lambda = 2 d \sin \theta
$$

where  $n$  is the scattering order (a positive integer),  $\lambda$  is the wavelength of the X-rays,  $d$  is the spacing of the crystal planes and  $\theta$  is the angle between the incident beam and the scattering crystal plane (the so called 'Bragg angle').

X-ray diffraction (XRD) relies on two functional principles: (i) the elastic scattering of a monochromatic or polychromatic X-ray beam by the electrons in atoms in a periodic arrangement due to a crystal lattice and (ii) the constructive interference of the scattered X-rays under a certain angle. X-ray diffraction, as an analytical technique is used for studying the crystal structure of inorganic and organic substances. It is primarily suited to characterize crystalline components in materials, and to determine phase compositions of mixed materials. Information on amorphous content can also be obtained.

Due to its individual crystal structure and chemical composition, every crystalline substance yields a unique pattern in an X-ray diffractogram, where intensity of the scattered X-ray beam is plotted versus the diffraction angle.

The typical subject of XRD analysis in geosciences is the determination of the phase or mineral content of an unknown mixture of crystalline phases (minerals) in a rock, soil, ceramic and so forth by analysing powders or solid materials by powder diffraction. In powder diffraction, the scattered beams of the same d-value of lattice planes correspond to one diffraction cone (Debye ring) with a specific opening angle. If the crystallites in the analysed sample volume are statistically orientated in all directions, each diffraction cone will exhibit an even intensity distribution. Textured or too coarse crystallites with bad orientation statistics in the sample result in a nonuniform or spotty intensity distribution of the individual diffraction cones. The integrated diffraction patterns produced from an unknown mineral or mixture of minerals can be analysed and compared using databases, such as the PDF database from the ICDD (International Centre for Diffraction Data) or the COD (Crystallography Open Database). Other open-access databases are available (e.g., Downs 2006) or users may generate their own databases using personal reference materials.

# 40.2 XRD Instrumentation

# 40.2.1 Classical Powder-XRD

In geosciences, the most common application of XRD is in the analysis of powder samples. Several mg of a sample with grain sizes typically between  $1\mu \mathrm{m}$  to  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$  ensure good statistics for representative intensities of the peaks ('reflections'), which are indispensable for further interpretation of the measurement (see section 40.1.1). A typical X-ray powder diffractometer consists of a water-cooled X-ray tube, a detector for recording the intensities of X-rays scattered by the analysed sample,

and a goniometer that controls the angle between the X-ray tube, sample and detector. The sample must be precisely positioned in the goniometer centre (focus) to avoid errors in the measured peak positions. Sample rotation within the goniometer centre also aids in producing representative reflection intensities.

X-ray tubes always emit continuous Bremsstrahlung radiation, superimposed by different characteristic wavelengths depending on the anode material. For the calculation of d-values from peak positions using the Bragg law, however, only one defined wavelength is indispensable. Therefore, filters or primary monochromators are used to suppress the unwanted X-ray wavelengths. Alternatively, a secondary monochromator in front of the detector could be used to suppress both, exited sample fluorescence and the unwanted X-ray wavelengths generated by the X-ray tube. Nowadays, progress in detector technology also provides the opportunity to use energy-dispersive detectors for this purpose.

# 40.2.2 X-Ray Microdiffraction

Powder XRD measurements may be conducted on loose sediment samples collected from the face of an oriented block of archaeological sediment prior to processing for micromorphological analysis. For samples in thin section, or for very small sample amounts, the more appropriate technique is a variety of XRD known as X-ray microdiffraction ( $\mu$ -XRD).  $\mu$ -XRD allows the analyst to measure small sample volumes, perform high-resolution, locally resolved phase mapping of heterogeneous materials and directly measure uneven sample surfaces without sample preparation. Due to these advantages and due to the fact that stand-alone laboratory systems are now available,  $\mu$ -XRD today has applications not only in the field of geosciences but also in fields such as materials sciences and archaeometry.

$\mu$ -XRD differs from the classical XRD setup described above in a few key ways (Figure 40.1b):

- The diameter of the X-ray beam is adjusted using pin-hole collimator systems, X-ray optics (like mono- or polycapillary lenses), or X-ray mirror systems (like Montel optics). Using X-ray optics or X-ray mirrors for beam shaping is preferred over the use of pinhole collimators because the former approaches produce X-ray beam fluxes that are orders of magnitude higher (Berthold et al. 2009).  
- The sample stage component of the system is modified in  $\mu$ -XRD relative to classical XRD in that an optical microscope is used for sample identification and alignment of the target area in the goniometer centre.  
- The sample stage is motorized in three directions in order to facilitate locally resolved measurements and

phase mappings. Automated rotation of the stage about the vertical axes can be used to produce better crystallite statistics (see below).

- In a  $\mu$ -XRD setup, use of a large two-dimensional detector (termed '  $\mu$ -XRD²') is highly recommended because of the small analysed sample area and the consequently bad crystallite statistics in such a small volume. Only two-dimensional detector systems give a direct insight into the diffraction image, as well as the opportunity to evaluate whether the intensity distribution of each observed diffraction cone (Debye ring) is uniformly or spotty (Figure 40.1c), which is the key to avoiding misinterpretation of the measured diffractogram (see also sections 40.1.1 and 40.3).

Furthermore, due to the small X-ray beam diameter, air-cooled, low-power microfocus X-ray tubes can be used, which allows the development of mobile instruments. Further details about the technique of microdiffraction using two-dimensional detectors can be found in He (2011).

In  $\mu$ -XRD, the analytical area is variable and depends on the optics of the instrument and the sampling parameters. The spot size of the primary X-ray beam where it interacts with the sample, and thereby the local resolution, is easily adjustable by changing the X-ray optic or the pinhole diameter on the exit of the collimator/X-ray-mirror system. Depending on the incident angle of the X-ray beam, the spot has the shape of an ellipse with variable long axis length, which is related to the angle of incidence, and a fixed short axis length that is dependent on the spot size of the X-ray optic/collimator (Figure 40.2a). It is also important to note that the spot size varies depending on whether the sample is fixed in place or rotated. If a sample must be rotated to obtain random crystal orientations, the spot size will be a circle with the diameter determined by the length of the long axis of the ellipse.

In contrast to powder-XRD,  $\mu$ -XRD measurements can be performed without any sample preparation even on rough sample surfaces like rock fragments and archaeological artefacts.  $\mu$ -XRD is therefore perfectly suited for nondestructive measurements in an archaeological context. Only near-surface information is detectable, with an analysing depth ranging from smaller than  $1\mu \mathrm{m}$  up to tens of  $\mu \mathrm{m}$  depending on the material, the wavelength of the incident X-rays, the X-ray flux on the sample and the setup of the diffractometer (incident / diffracting angle, detector efficiency, etc.) and on the intensity of the particular reflection.

For micromorphological samples, it is possible to conduct  $\mu$ -XRD measurements directly on uncovered petrographic thin sections. In the event that the thin sections are covered, it is also possible to conduct measurements

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/cb08309b4e10b933264c6c143e779092bef14259a65e6c362631bdbd5c5f1adc.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/39027b3b8c7678d7e030880f351083847a55aecc08b7d568a363c7ec9ecd1fb7.jpg)

The positions and intensities of the observed reflections provide a fingerprint of the structures (minerals) present in the analysed sample. In this case, the whole X-ray diffraction pattern was produced from approximately  $6^{\circ} - 68^{\circ}2\Theta$  by integrating two slightly overlapping detector images, such as the one shown in (c). (b) This is a typical set-up of a  $\mu\text{-XRD}^2$  diffractometer with a  $50\mu \mathrm{m}$  polycapillary X-ray optic and a micromorphological thin section positioned on the instrument stage. The X-ray tube, which is located to the left of the monochromator, is not visible. The large, primary beam stable two-dimensional area detector shown here has a  $14\mathrm{cm}$  window diameter capable of detection over an area of  $2048\times 2048$  pixels and covers in this position angles of approximately  $40^{\circ}2\Theta$  and  $40^{\circ}\psi$ . (c) A typical example of a detector image from the 2D detector covering approximately  $6^{\circ} - 46^{\circ}2\Theta$ . The diffraction ring segments (Debye-rings) are sections of the diffraction cones from the certain lattice planes (see section 40.1.1). The integration of the whole detector image gives the typical diffraction pattern, as shown in (a). The arrow indicates an incomplete ring, which can result from preferred orientation of the crystals within the measured area. Intense single crystal spots are also visible in the middle and at the lower edge of the image (see section 40.1.1).

on the sliced face of a resin-impregnated sediment block, or on the chip used to produce a thin section. In this way, the micromorphologist can best integrate the results with other analyses conducted on the thin sections. Small amounts (down to a few  $\mu \mathrm{g}$ ) of sample material may also be drilled from resin-impregnated sediment, or other types of archaeological artefacts, but the exact context for the results depends strongly on the sampling accuracy. In all cases, when resin or other embedding material is used, for example to impregnate or fix the sample, one must keep in mind that short-range ordering of the embedding material could contribute to the measured diffractogram, resulting in a more or less intense background bump overlapping the sample information (Figure 40.2b).

# 40.3 Output and Analysis

The result of a single classical powder measurement is processed by a computer and summarized in a data table or represented graphically in a type of x-y plot called the 'diffraction pattern' or 'diffractogram' (see Figure 40.1a). In a diffractogram, the units of measure on the  $x$ -axis are the Bragg angle in  $^\circ 2\theta$ , and the  $y$ -axis gives the scattered intensity typically in units of counts per second. Taking into account the wavelength of the X-rays emitted by the tube and the Bragg law, the angle  $2\theta$  can be replaced by the d-spacing in nm on the  $x$ -axis. Scattered X-rays appear on diffractograms as peaks ('reflections') of varying intensity (see also section 40.1.1). It is important to note that this type of plot cannot be referred to as a 'spectrum' because the  $x$ -axis is neither a wavelength nor energy.

The diffraction pattern can be qualitatively analysed to identify the crystal structures present in the sample. Today, software packages with search-match algorithms

are available, which match the angle and intensities of peaks to those produced from references in, for example, the databases listed in section 40.1.1. Problems in identification can arise if many different crystal structures are present in one sample, due to possible overlaps of certain peaks. Furthermore, all computer-generated identifications must be confirmed by the analyst, who may need to consider the context of the sample when choosing the best of several possible matches.

Quantification of phase content within a phase mixture using relative peak intensities is possible using internal or external standard methods or, for example, using Rietveld software packages but it is important to keep in mind that many parameters must be considered for this purpose. The integrated intensities of the scattered X-rays represented on the  $y$ -axis of the diffractogram depend on the structure factor, the multiplicity factor, absorption, extinction, Lorentz and polarization factors, geometric factors and the displacement (temperature) factor. Additionally the intensities of the X-ray data can be influenced by preferred orientations of crystallites, as well as elements of the diffractometer setup. Therefore, it is impossible to quantify amounts of different substances only by comparing the individual peak intensities if the aforementioned factors are unknown. In addition, the detection limit of certain structures depends on these parameters. As a result, the detection limits for different components of a mixture are variable, ranging from below  $1\%$  up to several percent.

In addition to structure identification, the diffraction pattern yields other types of information about the composition of the sample:

- Peak shifts of certain reflections can be caused by substitution of elements in the crystal lattice (e.g., Mg versus Ca in calcite). Other factors that influence peak positions include strain / stress in the analysed sample, which causes changes in the lattice distances.

Figure 40.2 Analytical parameters. (a) Measurement area under four different conditions: (1) a  $500\mu \mathrm{m}$  monocapillary X-ray optic with  $300\mu \mathrm{m}$  pinhole with fixed sample (yellow ellipse); (2) Same X-ray optic with rotated sample (green circle); (3) a  $50\mu \mathrm{m}$  polycapillary X-ray optic with fixed sample (orange ellipse); (4) the  $50\mu \mathrm{m}$ -polycapillary X-ray optic with rotated sample (blue circle). Phosphatized endocarp of Celtis tournefortii from the Neolithic site of Aşkıli Höyük, Turkey. The white arrow indicates the outer edge of the endocarp at the contact with the surrounding sediment. The black area at the base of the image is a resin-filled void. Photographs taken with the camera of the microdiffractometer (oblique incident light, width of view approximately two mm). (b) Diffraction patterns with both X-ray optics from the fixed areas illustrated in (a). The broad hump (arrow) in the  $20 - 30^{\circ}2\Theta$  region of the diffraction pattern on the left is due mainly to the presence of the embedding resin within the measured area using the  $500\mu \mathrm{m}$  monocapillary X-ray optic with  $300\mu \mathrm{m}$  pinhole. The additional quartz peak (Q) in the left is due to the quartz within the sedimentary matrix sampled within the larger measured area. Theoretical quartz peaks (red) and hydroxyapatite peaks (blue) are illustrated. In the right pattern only the hydroxyapatite information is visible, due to the significantly smaller spot size using the  $50\mu \mathrm{m}$  polycapillary optic. In addition, the background hump is reduced, here caused only by small amounts of resin inside of pores, and possibly interference from the glass (see Baker et al. 2014). (c) Diffraction pattern (left inset) from an area containing two sand grains composed of single crystals of quartz (right inset; width of view approximately two mm) in fixed position. Analyses of single crystals, or multiple crystals with preferred orientation will appear as bright spots or incomplete diffraction rings (see also Figure 40.1b). Integrating diffraction images with these features yields correct peak positions but wrong intensities, compared to the theoretical quartz values (blue). The green arrows indicate the correct height (intensity) of the reflections. In such a case either sample rotation or enlarging the analysed area for better crystallite statistics could be helpful. Sample from Eland's Bay Cave, South Africa.

- Peak broadening can correlate with the mean size of the corresponding coherent scattering domain direction (often misleadingly referred to as the particle or grain size - Scherrer 1918). However, many other effects may also contribute to peak broadening, such as the instrumental setup, strain, chemical heterogeneities, and microstress.  
- Finally, information on a potential amorphous phase or glassy content can also be obtained by carefully checking the angle-dependent background. This process is best accomplished with comparison to a reference measurement.

The output of a  $\mu$ -XRD measurement is similar to that of classical XRD. If a two-dimensional detector is used ( $\mu$ -XRD²), a certain number of overlapping diffraction images (see Figure 40.1c) is produced depending on size of the detector and the  $2\theta$ -range to be analysed. The information from each must be integrated and they must be processed together into a single diffractogram (see Figure 40.1a). The peak positions and relative intensities can then be compared to those of references in databases like the Crystallography Open Database (COD) or the PDF database from the International Centre for Diffraction Data (ICDD). Analysis of a single crystal, or a small number of crystals will produce diffraction images consisting of either a single diffraction spot or a several spots depending on the individual orientation of the crystals (Figure 40.2c, see also section 40.1.1 and examples in Lange et al. 2010; Berthold et al. 2015; Bramble et al. 2015). The integration of such images will result in misleading intensities and hamper correct phase identification. The solution to this problem is either sample rotation about a vertical axis or enlargement of the analysed area, which will increase the number of individual crystal orientations and produce a more suitable diffractogram. In this way, both single crystals and polycrystalline aggregates may be analysed. The diffractogram will contain peaks associated with all mineral species in the analysed area (see Figure 40.2b). If multiple minerals are present, it will be necessary to identify each of these individually. Petrographic observations can be helpful for deciding whether multiple minerals are present, some of which may be identified using optical properties and their peaks eliminated from the resulting diffractogram (Figure 40.3). It may be necessary to conduct supplementary analyses, using  $\mu$ -XRF, microprobe, or SEM-EDAX and use the elemental composition to produce a list of potential mineral species (see Figure 40.3b and c). In the event that the peaks from the unknown mineral do not match any patterns in the digital databases it is also possible to compare the diffraction pattern to those in printed publications, including volumes of published standards (e.g., Swanson et al.

1953) or articles focused on the description of crystal structures of single mineral species.

Other types of complementary and supplemental analyses may be necessary to confirm phase identifications. Amorphous or poorly crystalline materials and organic compounds are difficult to analyse using both classical XRD and  $\mu$ -XRD. In micromorphological samples, amorphous materials include glass, opaline phytoliths, short range order clay minerals, such as allophanes, and plant and animal tissues. For these materials, molecular (vibrational) spectrometry conducted at high resolution, such as  $\mu$ -Fourier transform infrared ( $\mu$ -FTIR) spectroscopy (see Berna 2017, this volume) or  $\mu$ -Raman spectroscopy, can be employed. In a typical workflow for archaeological micromorphology, these types of microanalyses may be used sequentially to identify an unknown mineral. Many studies in soil science and geosciences more broadly incorporate a suite of complementary microanalytical tools, starting with optical petrography and utilizing  $\mu$ -XRD (e.g., Manceau et al. 2005; Soubrand-Colin et al. 2005; Singer et al. 2009; Lange et al. 2010; Corriveau et al. 2011; Izawa et al. 2011, 2012; Baker et al. 2014). For micromorphology, the choice of microanalytical methods and their order of application depends on the research question, timeline and budget. In the examples illustrated in Figures 40.3 and 40.4, initial  $\mu$ -FTIR measurements failed to yield mineral identifications. Compared to molecular spectroscopic techniques, which provide information about molecular bonds and symmetry of molecules in a material either crystalline or amorphous, XRD analyses provide information about the spacing of the lattice planes in a crystalline structure (mineral). Although  $\mu$ -XRD analyses may be more expensive, advantages of the approach include widely available databases of diffraction patterns that contain thousands of references. Furthermore, all crystalline substances (minerals) produce diffraction patterns.  $\mu$ -XRD measurements can therefore provide an important information when other techniques have previously failed.

# 40.4 Applications to Archaeological Micromorphology Samples

# 40.4.1 Mineral Identification

As described above,  $\mu$ -XRD is commonly used to identify individual minerals, mixtures of minerals, or other crystalline compounds when other optical or molecular analyses have failed. Analyses may be conducted as single point measurements, or multiple measurements along line transects or over grids. In micromorphological samples, mineral identification using  $\mu$ -XRD

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a77067dc0028cb5885a4652ae15a074a01bf3b94a5f88e5a126c5b52da423f90.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/cb2630b2e390244a0d5f48a8729667153a5f89827bddddb8446c8872abe12f5e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/27d96d82ff4df11147c0f920b053b37bcab1875cc0b1c504a81bce329ba8b679.jpg)  
Figure 40.3 Micro-XRD integrated with elemental analyses for mineral identification. (a) A diagenetic mineral inside of a soil aggregate, originally composed of clay and quartz sand. Petrographic analyses indicate that the mineral has a radial crystal habit and exhibits first-order grey to orange interference colours. Elands Bay Cave, South Africa. XPL. (b) SEM-EDS mapping performed on a carbon-coated, uncovered thin section indicate that the mineral is rich in phosphorus (red colour) relative to the surrounding clay and quartz. (c) SEM-EDS mapping also shows that the clay and the diagenetic mineral contain more aluminium (white colour) than the quartz. (d) The diffraction pattern resulting from  $\mu$ -XRD analysis ( $50\mu m$  polycapillary X-ray optic) of the altered area clearly indicates the presence of two phases: variscite (red lines;  $\mathrm{Al}(\mathrm{PO}_4)\cdot 2\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ ) and quartz (blue lines, Q). Measurement time for the whole diffractogram was  $4\mathrm{min}$ . Quartz peaks were excluded from the database search, which was optimized with knowledge of the major elements.

may be necessary under a variety of circumstances. Several applications of mineral identification are illustrated in Figures 40.3, 40.4 and 40.5 and described in detail below.

Small crystal or particle size can limit the ability of the analyst to observe optical properties using a standard petrographic microscope. This is the case in micromor

phology when studying components of the silt and clay fraction, or microcrystalline secondary mineral phases. For example, secondary phosphate and sulfate minerals may form in archaeological deposits as microcrystalline nodules and masses (Figure 40.5). Under these conditions, it can be difficult to observe crystal habits, which may be the only features that could allow distinction

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/bd841125d22d17b559b898b0876d603b2b44484418567c4ca793074c13963ecd.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b1da032b180045ec3daf2a7e0c8272371e0a6d8c2b51ab9015f7059ff8e2a14e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/17af33a862c7f899292cda2f30b8852a423c249a71e516999bc017f38f5e81b5.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/eaec8b71851c92c2198e2fee0679f41930283155911f5a6c962db7195da7a328.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/00ebf1568ae6131171151d49fed2fce88f46b0ff950278277c55cdf8005a49a3.jpg)  
Figure 40.4 Applications of micro-XRD to the study of bone diagenesis in thin section. (a) A fragment of bone in which the primary hydroxyapatite has been replaced by a diagenetic mineral phase. Al = altered portion of the bone. Un = unaltered portion of the bone. Diepkloof Rockshelter, South Africa. PPL. (b) The same bone as in (a) viewed using the instrument camera (width of view approximately 2 mm). The red dot indicates the position of a measurement taken without rotation. (c) Detail of the boundary between the altered and unaltered portions of the bone. The diagenetic mineral phase is evidenced by the increase in interference colour order under XPL and visible spherulitic microcrystals. (d) Micro-FTIR analysis of the altered area conducted prior to micro-XRD analysis revealed the presence of a carbonate mineral (peaks positions labelled) in addition to hydroxyapatite but the exact identification was uncertain. The remaining peaks are indicative of hydroxyapatite and resin. (e) The diffraction pattern contains peaks indicative of one expected phase (hydroxyapatite; blue) and the unknown phase (green), identified as kutnohorite  $(\mathrm{Ca}(\mathrm{Mn}^{2+}, \mathrm{Mg}, \mathrm{Fe}^{2+})(\mathrm{CO}_3)_2)$ . Further research revealed that the IR peaks illustrated in (d) match those of a kutnohorite reference (Chukanov 2014). The sharpness of the hydroxyapatite diffraction peaks suggests high crystallinity. This observation is consistent with a micro-FTIR spectrum collected from the lower half of the bone fragment, which contains the phosphate high temperature peak (arrow in (d) – see also Thompson et al. 2011), indicative of burning.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3d26db5d180f1ca6540b05a305698034bb6abbb494ac4817f10153ad68ba3a10.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/07504028fd0d56c857cbc4d8c6461bbb45eb23a0d39568894d98a4da2d0f8bdf.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/557fa5525b174dbc9464f36c04f27e666d552184f074cd865b5d165f5fd79c97.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/0df963826670f1b88c9043bb27e74ddac5cf0f042d9a2acba4b3c9ce681c6e57.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/b2300a6d8414ca44aa315022c1b1ee01829546476c9d7bfd320f9bdc2fc2e926.jpg)  
Figure 40.5 Applications of micro-XRD in the identification of mineral nodules and crusts. (a) A mineral crust formed on a fragment of quartzite cannot be readily identified using its optical properties due to small crystal size. Eland's Bay Cave, South Africa. PPL. (b) The mineral exhibits low order grey interference colours. XPL. (c) Autofluorescence under blue light PPL suggests that this mineral is phosphatic. (d) Other examples of this mineral in the same sample include nodules that have formed inside fragments of charcoal, a process that micromorphological analyses reveal has contributed to its mechanical breakdown and low recovery of the material during excavation. (e) The diffraction pattern perfectly matches to the mineral taranakite, specifically the potassium-rich phase  $(\mathrm{K}_3\mathrm{Al}_5(\mathrm{PO}_3\mathrm{OH})_6(\mathrm{PO}_4)_2.18\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O})$ . Slight differences in the observed intensities are due to coarse crystallite spots.

between two or more similar phases. Microcrystalline calcite and aragonite are also difficult to distinguish due to their similar interference colours. Positive identification of these phases can be useful for understanding the thermal or diagenetic histories of archaeological materials, such as shell. Clay minerals and clay-sized particles in the fine fraction of a deposit have crystal sizes that preclude use of optical properties for identification. Loose sediment samples could be prepared in such a way as to provide (001)-textured samples necessary for a clay mineral identification using classical powder XRD (e.g., Moore & Reynolds 1989). However, in archaeological and soil samples, information about the spatial distribution of clay minerals is lost in such bulk analyses. Using  $\mu\text{-XRD}^2$  for identification of clay minerals in micromorphological thin sections can provide information about sediment source (when present in the groundmass), pedogenic processes (when present in argillic pedofeatures), mineral weathering (see Soubrand-Colin et al. 2005), and human activities (when present in materials such as ceramics). In this case, it should be noted that the results of the analyses will be similar to those produced from powder analysis of an unprepared bulk sample (Izawa et al. 2012).

Furthermore, other factors may limit the ability of the analyst to use optical properties and crystal habits to identify minerals. Staining by secondary iron and manganese oxides or humic acids can mask optical properties of minerals and materials in thin sections. In the case of diagenesis, crystal habits may be misleading when a secondary phase is pseudomorphic after a primary phase. For example, gypsum crystals, which exhibit a characteristic lozenge form, are readily replaced by anhydrite, basanite, and calcite (Karkanas 2010, Figures 1-3; Mees & Tursina 2010; Poch et al. 2010, Figure 11). Another example of mineral replacement is provided in Figure 40.2a, where microcrystalline hydroxyapatite has replaced the primary aragonite in the seed endocarp of Celtis tournefortii without altering the morphology of the biogenic material. Finally, the optical properties of rare mineral phases may be unknown to the analyst, poorly described in the literature, or atypical of the mineral owing to the particular archaeological burial conditions (see Figure 40.4).

The successful identification of unknown mineral phases is therefore important for many different types of studies. In addition to the examples above, it is possible to reconstruct chemical conditions within archaeological deposits by documenting the presence or absence of certain diagenetic mineral phases (see Figures 40.3 and 40.4). These mineral phases can be used to understand past moisture and pH within the deposits (Karkanas et al. 2000; Karkanas 2010). Combined with observations about the spatial relationship of secondary minerals with

other materials in thin section (Goldberg & Berna 2010), it can be possible to understand the impacts of diagenesis on the preservation of archaeological materials (see Figures 40.4 and 40.5). For similar applications of  $\mu$ -XRD for the analysis of soils and mineral weathering, see Soubrand-Colin et al. (2005) and Baker et al. (2014). In addition, mineral identification can used to characterize anthropogenic materials in terms of source or production history. For example, mineral phases and their relative abundances can be used to identify sources of ochres and other pigments. Blasco-Lopez et al. (2015) strongly recommend the use of  $\mu$ -XRD as one step in the study of plasters, which are often present in thin layers that are difficult to sample as loose sediments. In the study of plasters, mortars and cements, the identification of hydraulic phases (Callebaut et al. 2001) and other inclusions can be informative about production history and ancient pyrotechnology (Karkanas 2007). Finally,  $\mu$ -XRD² has proven useful for nondestructively studying the mineral content of ceramic archaeological artefacts and pigments (Berthold et al. 2015, 2016), lithic tools (Drechsler et al. 2013), metal objects, crystallites in glasses or glazes (Gradmann et al. 2015) and adornments such as jewellery. In these materials, the identification of minerals combined with chemical analysis can provide information – for example, about sources for raw materials, manufacturing processes, utilization, and trade.

# 40.4.2 Additional Types of Information Provided by  $\mu$ -XRD

Analyses conducted using  $\mu\text{-XRD}^2$ , which employs a large two-dimensional detector (see Figure 40.1b) can yield additional information from micromorphological samples beyond mineral content. This approach can be used to determine the orientation of crystals in thin section. The detector image gives a direct insight into the intensity distribution not only along the  $2\Theta$ -range but also the  $\psi$ -range, which allows the analyst to document local texture and perform orientation analysis (Figure 40.1c, also Bramble et al. 2015). In geological samples, this type of analysis has proven effective for isolating the diffraction patterns pertaining to the clay fraction from those caused by coarser mineral inclusions (Lange et al. 2010). In micromorphological samples, similar analysis could be employed in the study of b-fabric in the groundmass, or pedofeatures, such as clay coatings.

$\mu \text{-XRD}^2$  can also provide information about crystallinity of materials within the analysed area. Coarse crystallites appear as more or less intense single crystal spots in the detector image (see Figures 40.1c and 40.2c). Measurements conducted using a large two-dimensional detector provide a fast and efficient means to differentiate between coarse and fine crystalline phases.

Furthermore, using automated movements of the sample stage, it is possible to map the spatial distribution of different crystallite sizes. Crystallinity may also be measured in a relative sense using aspects of the diffractogram, such as peak broadening (Puceat et al. 2004). These types of crystallinity measurements conducted on materials in thin section may be used for a variety of purposes. For example, geogenic and biogenic apatites can have similar optical properties; however, the latter typically have lower crystallinity. The variations in crystallinity of apatite in bone have also been linked to human modification, such as burning (see Figure 40.3d), as well as postdepositional diagenesis. Therefore crystallinity measurements of apatite in thin section can be informative about taphonomic processes in archaeological sites (Berna et al. 2004).

Unfortunately, some types of measurements applicable to archaeological questions (e.g., Weymouth & Mandeville 1975; Schmidt et al. 2012; Xu et al. 2015) cannot be conducted using this approach. These include quantitative calculations of crystal size and lattice stress and strain, which require homogeneous samples of statistically orientated crystallites distributed over a larger sample volume/area. This precondition is typically in contradiction to the parameters of  $\mu$ -XRD measurements and depends strongly on the crystallite size in relation to the analysed area. To overcome this restriction, one could analyse a larger sample area, or analyse powder obtained from a micromorphological sample by microdrilling.

# 40.5 Concluding Remarks

$\mu$ -XRD<sup>2</sup> is a very promising supplemental analytical approach to the study and identification of materials in thin section. The technique has several advantages over classical powder XRD, including rapid measurement times of seconds to minutes, minimal sample preparation and the ability to conduct measurements in very small areas. The latter two points are especially important for micromorphological samples, wherein small areas can be analysed with no modification to the thin section. Advances in instrumentation over the past decade have reduced spot sizes, and eliminated the need for external radiation sources like synchrotron radiation. Furthermore, large two-dimensional detectors are increasingly affordable, which is indispensable for locally resolved analysis of micromorphological samples. These technical developments will have the effect of making  $\mu$ -XRD<sup>2</sup> measurements more common in the future. The examples provided in this chapter illustrate several ways in which measurements conducted on thin sections may be used in combination with more traditional petrographic observations for studying postdepositional changes to archaeological materials, and mineral formation in archaeological sediments, including successful identification of mineral phases when other approaches have failed.  $\mu$ -XRD<sup>2</sup> is therefore a powerful tool to be used in conjunction with other types of microanalyses.

41

# Micro XRF

Susan M. Mentzer<sup>1,2,3</sup>

$^{1}$  Institute for Archaeological Sciences, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany  
$^{2}$  School of Anthropology, University of Arizona, Tucson, USA  
3 Senckenberg Centre for Human Evolution and Paleoenvironment, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany

# 41.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

X-ray fluorescence spectroscopy (XRF) is a spectral analytical method that measures the relative intensity and energy of characteristic radiation emitted by a substance exposed to X-rays. The method generates information about the elemental composition of a sample, with detectability and precision dependent on the mass number of the element and the instrument configuration. It is a common technique in the geosciences and archaeology, due in part to its wide availability, minimal sample preparation and low cost (Mantler & Schreiner 2000; Shackley 2011). Portable instruments have recently gained popularity in archaeological and museum settings, although there is debate over their reliability (Frahm & Doonan 2013; Shackley 2010). Analyses may be quantitative or semiquantitative, depending on the sample-preparation procedures and instrument calibration.

A typical XRF instrument consists of a source of X-rays, an area where the X-rays interact with a sample, a radiation detector, and processing equipment. The X-ray source generates a beam with high energies that can vary from  $20 - 60\mathrm{kV}$ , while instrument optics direct the beam onto the sample surface – typically a pressed powder but occasionally an intact material – under vacuum. The X-rays interact with atoms present within the sample, removing their inner electrons. When electrons from the outer shells of the affected atoms fill the empty spaces, secondary X-ray photons are emitted, a process referred to as X-ray fluorescence. Some of the photons produced during this process reach the detector. The spectrum produced following processing consists of photon energy on the  $x$ -axis (in keV), and counts on the  $y$ -axis. The peak energies of the ejected photons correspond to the difference in binding energies of electrons between the outer and inner shells. Elements

with higher atomic numbers have a greater number of electrons occupying a greater number of shells and thus the potential for multiple characteristic photon energies is greater. Furthermore, light elements (typically atomic number  $< 12$ ; Mg) have low secondary X-ray photon yields and cannot be reliably detected. The relative peak surface areas correlate to elemental abundance. Data are typically presented as counts per energy interval, which can then be converted into weight percentage oxides or other measures of concentration by means of comparison to standards.

X-ray fluorescence instruments vary in their sources and detector configurations, with some instruments using gamma rays or synchrotron radiation to interact with the sample. All produce data in the form of counts per energy interval, which are represented graphically as spectra. Typical analyses are conducted on pressed powders, which necessitates destruction of the sample. The analysis does not further destroy the powder, which can be potentially returned to an archaeological collection or archive, or subjected to additional analyses. Detailed information about XRF instrument function and sample preparation can be found in any number of popular introductory texts including Beckhoff et al. (2007), Cesareo (2010) and Van Grieken & Markowicz (2001).

Microscopic X-ray fluorescence spectroscopy (micro-XRF or  $\mu$ -XRF) is a technique that limits the spatial area over which the X-ray beam interacts with the sample. Laboratory-grade micro-XRF instruments differ from traditional XRF instruments in their X-ray focusing optics and sample chamber configurations. For the former, the use of mono- and polycapillary lenses enable the generation of a spot size that is of the order of several micrometers in diameter. This microscopic spot size, in combination with a motorized sample stage and two-dimensional mapping system, allows for elemental compositions to be measured and reported across areas as

single points, transects and maps. Micro-XRF analyses may also be conducted in specialized facilities using synchrotron radiation sources  $(\mu$  -SRXRF). These types of analyses are not considered here.

For micromorphologists the most important factors to consider when developing an analytical strategy are spot size, sampling volume and detection limits (Behrends & Kleingeld 2009). The spot size is determined primarily by the choice of X-ray tube and focusing optics, which limit the beam diameter to anywhere between 300 and  $25\mu \mathrm{m}$ . The sampling volume is determined by overall volume within which the beam interacts with the sample (a function of spot size and beam intensity) but, more importantly, the depth from which photons can escape. This "information depth" varies for every element and is determined, in part, by the generated photon energies and by the electron density of the sample. In general, the information depth is shallower for lighter elements compared to heavier elements. Conversely, the detection limits for elements vary according to fluorescence yield, which is highest for heavier elements. Detection of specific elements may be improved by adjusting beam intensity, or by using filters.

X-rays are also a byproduct of exposure of a sample to electrons. Thus, scanning electron microscopes (SEM) may be used to produce similar types of measurements, as applied in SEM-EDS analysis (Energy-Dispersive Spectroscopy) and in Electron Microprobe Analysis (EMPA, using a wavelength-dispersive detector system - see Newbury et al. 2003). For the purposes of this entry, it is important to note that all three types of analyses can be conducted in conjunction with a micromorphological study. Bulk analyses may be conducted on powders prepared from loose sediment samples collected from micromorphological blocks prior to resin impregnation. Scanning electron microscopes and EMPA have been used to conduct measurements on micromorphological samples, including thin sections (for more information and examples see Wilson 2017, this book); however, when using these instruments sample preparation methods, sample chamber size, depth of beam penetration, and scan times are different compared to micro-XRF spectroscopy. In general, micro-XRF analyses require less sample preparation than SEM-EDXRA, and micro-XRF instrument sample chambers can be large enough to accommodate museum specimens, which makes them well suited to analyze portions of micromorphological blocks.

Since the 1980s, studies that use micro-XRF analyses for applications in geosciences have grown in popularity and scope (e.g., Boehme 1987; Haschke 2014, pp. 270-283; Jones 2000), although at the time of writing, the most prolific and successful applications are in the field of drill core analysis, which utilizes specialized core scanning instruments (see Croudace & Rothwell 2015).

Researchers in this field emphasize in particular the strength of combining petrography with micro-XRF data (Shanahan et al. 2008a, b; Dulski et al. 2015; Neugebauer et al. 2015), an approach that has direct implications for archaeological micromorphology. Micro-XRF analyses have been conducted successfully directly on resin-impregnated archaeological sediment blocks (Mentzer & Quade 2013). These types of analyses may therefore, with careful sample preparation (Figure 41.1), be conducted within several millimeters of the features observed on petrographic thin sections. Moreover, the results may be presented at resolutions that are comparable to those of observations made using a petrographic microscope.

# 41.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedure

# 41.2.1 Sample Preparation

Table 41.1 presents several sampling options for micromorphological samples. For micro-XRF spectroscopy paired with micromorphology, the measurements are typically conducted on resin-impregnated sediment blocks that have been sliced with a rock or tile saw to obtain a flat surface. For the very best correlation with the micromorphological observations, measurements should be conducted prior to thin section production on the sliced chips of resin-impregnated sediment. Measurements may also be conducted after micromorphological analysis using the remaining portions of the chips that were used to produce the petrographic thin sections (see Figure 41.1c). In this case, the micro-XRF measurements will correspond to areas located within  $1 - 2\mathrm{cm}$  of the sediment visible in thin section. It is also possible to analyze other portions of the resin-impregnated block, including the corresponding plane, provided that the area of interest fits within the sample chamber. Sample chambers vary in size, with typical stage dimensions greater than  $15\times 15\mathrm{cm}$  with sufficient vertical space between the stage and the detector to accommodate resin-impregnated sample chips, slab from blocks, or large blocks sliced in half.

Although it is possible, it is not always recommended to conduct measurements directly on the thin sections, due to the fact that, depending on the beam energy and tube voltage, the X-ray beam may penetrate deeper than 30 microns into the sample. If a thin section of standard thickness  $(30\mu \mathrm{m})$  were analyzed, a portion of the signal could come from the carrier glass. As described above, the information depth for secondary X-ray photons depends on the density of the sample, and the energy of the X-ray line measured (for more information, see Haschke 2014; Fernandez 2000; Janssens 2000).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/068622455dc2d275f5ef6cb23a5f1efb0a026ef9956d85ef901fa36a39b87544.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/f9280147f36b2c1ddd8e68a841ce7723d2a6dbe717aea8187bb22835f5c98d4f.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/4b7d5c1b3653a535c88d320968dec98b25aeac79baa5bd67eb53cfc0cc2e4c60.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/7aa2255174e113ab93f4f307c8ac1578d77ec0a6f23de2940acca462d31b760f.jpg)  
Figure 41.1 Examples of measurement types and sampling configurations. (a) Incident light scan of a sliced sample. The yellow box indicates the available measurement area. (b) The available measurement area from (a) as viewed inside a micro-XRF sample chamber using the internal camera. The blue line denotes the position of a sampling transect. (c) Production face of a tile used for preparation of a thin section, positioned with a perfectly horizontal surface using putty (visible on the right and basal portions of the image). The red "x" indicates the location of a single measurement point on a fragment of bone. (d) Thin section that corresponds to the tile in (c) with the bone fragment (x). Incident light scan. (e) Spectrum from the bone fragment in (c) and (d). (f) Example of a map of the distribution of the element iron within a different sample that contains microlaminations. (g) Example of a map of the distribution of the element calcium in the same sample as (f).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/786d96a079deed28f71c2705a7e0720f7460229bbe91683493d9d56710b288c8.jpg)

Table 41.1 Sampling options for integrating micro-XRF measurements with micromorphology.  

<table><tr><td>Sample type</td><td>Relationship between the sediment in the analytical area and the sediment visible in thin section</td><td>Analysis timeline</td></tr><tr><td>Sliced block (with one flat face) or archive slab (with two flat, parallel faces)</td><td>As close as a few millimeters (if using the corresponding plane) to several centimeters away</td><td>Before or after thin section production and petrographic analysis</td></tr><tr><td>Sliced tile or chip used to produce a thin section, production face</td><td>Equivalent</td><td>Prior to thin section production</td></tr><tr><td>Thick section, opposite face</td><td>Several millimeters away</td><td>During thin section production</td></tr><tr><td>Leftover tile, production face</td><td>Several millimeters away</td><td>After thin section production and petrographic analysis</td></tr><tr><td>Thin section</td><td>Identical</td><td>After thin section production and petrographic analysis</td></tr></table>

Under certain circumstances, one can limit the information depth to  $30~\mu \mathrm{m}$  and obtain data for lighter elements from thin sections of standard thickness. Successful analyses of geological samples prepared as standard petrographic thin sections have been reported by Genna et al. (2011) following laboratory tests comparing the effects of sample preparation on the specific materials of interest. To avoid such extensive testing, which may not apply to heterogeneous archaeological samples, it may be preferable to conduct analyses on "thick" sections (including mounted sample slices prior to final grinding), or sections ground to  $50 - 100\mu \mathrm{m}$ . For examples of these approaches, see papers by Cuyen et al. (2010), Attaelmanan (2014) and Angelici et al. (2015). Using these strategies, measurement areas can be located within a few tens of micrometers from the sediment visible on the final thin sections (see Table 41.1).

Micro-XRF analyses are nondestructive to the micromorphological sample; however, large sample blocks or archive slabs may require additional cutting in order to fit the dimensions of the sample chamber. Aside from cleaning to remove dust and slicing debris, there are no additional treatments of the sample surface prior to analysis. Polishing is not necessary. Furthermore, vacuum conditions do not affect the sample negatively in any way.

# 41.2.2 Instrumentation and Configuration

Micro-XRF instruments are typically housed in a laboratory that has been secured, with access limited due to radiation safety precautions. The analyses are not dangerous to the user; however, radiation certification or supervision may be required. The tabletop instruments are attached to desktop computers that allow the analyst to specify the measurement parameters and process the results. Following sample selection and preparation, the sample is placed into the sample chamber of the

instrument and leveled to obtain a horizontal surface. Air may be removed from the sample chamber using a vacuum pump. The sample can then be photographed using the internal camera (see Figure 41.1b and c) and measurement points, transects or areas selected with the computer. Spot size, point averaging parameters, tube voltage (kV), X-ray beam current  $(\mu \mathrm{A})$ , vacuum conditions, and measurement time can be controlled by the user via the computer software. Combined, these factors determine the sample area, the elements that can be measured and the precision of the measurements.

Additional instrument configurations include exchangeable X-ray tubes, and filters. Individual point measurements can take as little as a few seconds for major elements. Longer dwell times are used to detect trace elements. Transects or maps covering large areas can take hours to produce, depending on the spacing of the points (step size) and the dwell time at each point. Decreasing the step size and increasing the dwell time increases the resolution of the resulting image but increases the overall time required for analysis.

When selecting sampling areas and data collection parameters, it is important to consider the limitations of the method. The most significant limitation is that, as mentioned previously, only elements with mass numbers above 11 or  $12\mathrm{(Na}$  and  $\mathrm{Mg})$  can be measured. As a result, micro-XRF spectroscopy will be ineffective for studying organic compounds in micromorphological samples, as carbon is not detectable. Furthermore, elements present in trace amounts will be undetectable or measured with lower precision. Another limitation is that the results for a single point are the composition of the target area on the surface, as well as a volume at depth. Thus, the analytical area can range from hundreds to thousands of cubic microns, depending on spot size. Within this area, there may be voids filled with resin, or heterogeneous sediment. As resin has its own specific chemical composition that can vary between batches or even within the

sample (due to leaching of soluble salts; e.g., NaCl – see Mentzer & Quade 2013), it is important to consider that a portion of the signal could be due to the presence of elements within the resin. In some situations, this "limitation" may be exploited in order to study void abundance and distribution. Issues of low elemental abundance and sampling volume may be addressed by adjusting instrument settings. In some cases, it may be advisable to devise a series of exploratory analyses followed by repeated measurements to ensure that the data are suitable to address the research aims. Finally, the analyst should consider of the size of the analytical area when interpreting results, and focus on general trends over areas rather than individual measurements. Interpretation of single points should be made with caution.

# 41.2.3 Results

The output of a mapping analysis (see Figure 41.1f and g) is a series of images that, in micromorphology, are best suited for direct comparison with petrographic observations. The images can be processed further to improve the spatial resolution. For point measurements collected singly or along transects, the output is in the form of a counts per energy interval, represented as a spectrum (see Figure 41.1d) and a data table listing the total counts for each element, as well as the measurement parameters. The counts are converted to elemental abundances using the instrument software. Elemental abundances measured using micro-XRF spectroscopy should be considered to be semiquantitative, unless further calibrations are applied (Adderley et al. 2007). For example, comparisons between bulk analysis using powder XRF spectroscopy and surface analysis using  $\mu$ -XRF spectroscopy on the same samples indicate that the results are generally correlative, but not identical (Genna et al. 2011). Furthermore, quantitative analyses require homogeneity of the sample within the interaction volume, which, as described above, can be much larger and irregular in shape compared to the spot diameter on the sample surface (see Janssens et al. 2000b). Homogeneity is not a characteristic of most micromorphological samples. The total counts may be converted to atomic percentage, or percentage oxides using models; however, it is important to note that percentage oxide calculations will always add to  $100\%$ . These results may be misleading when a significant portion of the sample is composed of elements that are not detectable using this technique (e.g., elements with mass number  $< 12$ ), or if single elements are present in multiple oxidation states (e.g.,  $\mathrm{Fe_2O_3}$  versus  $\mathrm{FeO}$ ). It is up to the analyst to decide whether data conversions are appropriate for addressing his or her research question.

The semiquantitative results can be used in several different ways. Figure 41.2 illustrates the pairing of micromorphological observations with spectra collected along a vertical transect. In this case, collecting data along a vertical transect allows the analyst to observe stratigraphic trends within a single micromorphological sample, or even spanning multiple samples. The transect spans several microfacies units comprised of layers of construction debris and dung. The units are distinctive in terms of the variance in abundance of Si and Ca, which can be correlated to the presence of materials such as plaster and phytoliths. This example shows that interpretation of the results is best accomplished using an integrated approach in which petrographic observations of the sediment in thin are compared to the elemental data, sometimes in combination with data from other microanalyses.

Statistical analyses may also be conducted using elemental data obtained from micro-XRF analyses. For example, simple plots can be used to observe positive and negative correlations in elemental abundance and test their strength of association (see Strawn et al. 2002; Mentzer & Quade 2013; Winkel et al. 2013). Cluster analysis (Figure 41.3) may be used to compare groupings of different types of sediment based on chemistry to those produced using micromorphological observations (i.e. microfacies types), which may be informative about the genetic relationships between different microstratigraphic units or sources of sediment. In the example provided, cluster analysis supports the observation that red sedimentary aggregates found in archaeological features are similar to those forming within recent geogenic deposits in the same cave. Alternatively, micromorphological observations may be used to define groups in advance and statistical analyses such as principle components analysis can be used to understand their similarities and differences.

# 41.3 Archaeological Applications

Micro-XRF applications in terrestrial sedimentology, soil science and archaeology that link elemental measurements with micromorphological or other petrographic analyses are thus far rather limited. A brief overview of the few studies outside of archaeology reveals several applications in understanding soil forming processes. For example, Manceau et al. (2004) and Voegelin et al. (2007) applied the method to understand the relationship of elements to pedofeatures, such as root channels, which were identified using micromorphology. Strawn et al. (2002) and Thorne et al. (2009) used paired micromorphology and micro-XRF analyses to document mineral and rock weathering processes in acid sulfate and lateritic soils.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/9f37a344cd425675109121d29b94381ee9fd45b85b7cfd99721cb8eb57b03182.jpg)  
Figure 41.2 Production and use of elemental intensity measurements along vertical transects. (a) As part of a broader study on dung inputs to archaeological deposits (see Stiner et al. 2014), a block of sediment was collected and processed into two petrographic thin sections. Under XPL, broad variations in the intensity of interference colors are apparent, and the laminated fabric of the dung is distinctive.  $\mu$ -XRF analyses were conducted along two different vertical transects on the cut block face. Each spectrum is the average of nine points distributed within a  $100\mu \mathrm{m}$  square, each having a  $\sim 40\mu \mathrm{m}$  spot size. Plots of the results illustrate vertical trends in the distribution of elements. Measured from the top of the transect, calcium intensities are lowest from  $0 - 4\mathrm{cm}$ , becoming highly variable from  $4 - 5.5\mathrm{cm}$ . From  $5.5 - 8.5\mathrm{cm}$ , calcium intensities are highest, with low intensities from  $8.5 - 14\mathrm{cm}$  depth. Silicon intensities are most variable in the uppermost  $9\mathrm{cm}$  of the transect, with the lower portion exhibiting high values with low variance. (b) Integration with observations from the thin sections: three photomicrographs correspond to three localities labeled in (a). Figure 41(b)1, from the region of variable Ca intensity, contains gravel- and sand-sized aggregates of carbonate-rich construction material (ca) with elongate vegetal voids derived from the decay of plant fragments used as temper (arrow). XPL. The coarse matrix contains abundant sand-sized fragments of volcanic glass (vg) and feldspar (f), which explains the high variability in Si intensities. Figure 41(b)2 corresponds to the region of highest Ca intensity due to abundant calcareous dung spherulites, with inclusions of calcium oxalates (ox). XPL. Figure 41(b)3 is dung rich in phytoliths (ph) and corresponds to the region of high and invariable Si intensity, PPL.
