Figure 41.3 Elemental measurements on different sedimentary components and statistical analyses of the results. (a) Thin section from an Upper Paleolithic anthropogenic deposit (Layer H), incident light scan. The sample contains a layer with abundant rounded red sediment aggregates in a matrix of loose calcareous ashes overlain by an intact ashy combustion feature. (b) The corresponding chip in the  $\mu$ -XRF sample chamber. Point measurements were conducted on 12 red sediment aggregates. Additional measurements were conducted along a vertical transect (red line). (c) The upper combustion feature contains dense ashes with well-preserved rhombic morphologies. PPL. (d) The corresponding spectra show high amounts of Ca, with minor amounts of P. This composition is consistent with ashes. (e) Red sedimentary aggregates in a nonanthropogenic layer (Layer A1, a recent deposit in the same cave that is stratigraphically higher than Layer H) are composed of red clay with silt-sized quartz. Aggregate development is due to intensive bioturbation. PPL. (f) Analysis under oblique incident light (OIL) reveals the presence of possible Mn- and Fe-oxide inclusions. (g) The red aggregates from Layer H that are present in the ashy matrix in the thin section pictured in (a) are similar in morphology and composition to the aggregates that are present within the nonanthropogenic deposit in Layer A1. PPL. (h) Same view as (g), OIL. (i) Cluster analysis conducted on a database containing measurements on 157 individual red sediment aggregates from all of the layers in the site (I through A1) was used as a preliminary test of the hypothesis that the red sediment aggregates in Layer H are similar to those observed forming today in Layer A1. Two clusters are distinguished primarily by the intensity of Fe, Ti, Ca and P. The graph shows the contribution of each element to the calculation of the clusters. Higher values along the x-axis correlate with higher importance in the calculation of the clusters. For comparison, layer membership ranks low on the scale of importance, although layer groupings were not used in the generation of the clusters. (j) A stacked histogram that illustrates the total number of red sediment aggregates belonging to each cluster, grouped by stratigraphic layer (x-axis). The aggregates from Layer A1, pictured in (e) and (f), and the aggregates from Layer H, pictured in (g) and (h), belong to Cluster 1. These, and other statistical analyses support the idea that the red sediment aggregates in Layer H are derived from within the cave. Future analyses will prioritize expanding the sample size.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d1bb17e0a4df016b55ce5bcc58bb36a0a600dd6ebf9a882a69a91c87c64913a6.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/8489a718fd90a156c6737bcd8bd573b897dbfbcf57bd2fb71f0b4d56057f1d80.jpg)

In a related field, Winkel et al. (2013) compared the results of elemental mapping to petrography of travertine samples in order to understand the relationship between crystal growth and trace elements. Published archaeological applications are exceptionally rare; however, Mentzer and Quade (2013) tested the method on plaster floors from a Neolithic tell and identified correlations between the elemental compositions of calcareous plasters and the carbonate source used to construct them. Adderley et al. (2007) also applied the method to thin sections of archaeological floors, with the goal of understanding chemical effects of the burial environment on bone. Furthermore, Figures 41.2 and 41.3 illustrate additional archaeological applications of micro-XRF.

Due to the similarity of the results, other potential archaeological applications can also be drawn from previous studies integrating micromorphology with SEM or EMPA. These applications include mapping of the distribution of Ca in burned features as a proxy for ash (Courty et al. 1989), documenting the elemental and mineralogical composition of a stabling floor crust (Macphail et al. 2004), understanding the relationship between phosphorus and other pedogenic features in Anthrosols (Schaefer et al. 2004) and identification of authigenic minerals (Karkanas 2010; Karkanas et al. 1999). Other possible applications in archaeological micromorphology range from mineral identification to characterization of sedimentary features. Applications in mineralogy that are relevant to archaeology or soil

# 41.4 Concluding Remarks

Micro-XRF shows much promise for studies that integrate micromorphological observations from thin sections with data regarding elemental composition of different sediments and materials. The strengths of the technique include minimal sample preparation, and its suitability for analysis of different portions of micromorphology blocks including large slabs. When carefully selected, analytical areas can be matched directly with sediment observed in thin section. The micromorphological observations therefore provide spatial context for the elemental measurements.

# 42

# Micro-CT Scanning

Dominique J.M. Ngan-Tillard<sup>1</sup> and Dirk J. Huisman<sup>2</sup>

<sup>1</sup> Section Geo Engineering, Department of Geoscience and Engineering, Delft University of Technology, The Netherlands  
$^{2}$  Cultural Heritage Agency of the Netherlands, Amersfoort, The Netherlands

# 42.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

X-ray micro-computed tomography (hereafter micro-CT) is a nondestructive technique that can be used to inspect the internal composition and structure of a wide range of materials, including soil samples. Over the years it has become an indispensable tool in the field of earth sciences (Ketcham & Carlson 2001; Taina et al. 2008; Helliwell et al. 2013; Cnudde & Boon 2013) and is increasingly popular in the field of archaeology for the study of organic and nonorganic artefacts (Bertrand et al. 2012; Tuniz et al. 2013; Tuniz & Zanini 2014). Micro-CT scanning has also been combined with soil micromorphology in earth science (Elliot & Heck 2007; Killfeather & van de Meer 2008; Tania et al. 2010; Tarplee et al. 2011; Elyeznasni et al. 2012; Bendle et al. 2015) and in a few cases, in archaeological science (Adderley et al. 2001; Huisman et al. 2014; Ngan-Tillard et al. 2016). The review presented here is intentionally limited to these latter publications. Studies performed in a nonarchaeological context should be consulted to get further insight into the more general potential of the technique.

In lab-based micro-CT scanners, a source of polychromatic X-rays illuminates the studied object, whilst a detector records the X-ray transmitted through the object. The object is rotated stepwise and at each step, a series of 2D radiographs is recorded. The scanning parameters (voltage and current of the X-ray beam, exposure time, number of radiographs per step and step angle) are selected to reach best compromise between spatial resolution, density contrast resolution and scan acquisition time (Sleutel et al. 2008; Tarplee et al. 2011). The radiographs are then processed with the help of sophisticated algorithms (Vlassenbroeck et al. 2007) to derive patterns of variations in attenuation for 2D slices perpendicular to the axis of rotation that is used during

radiograph acquisition. The reconstructed 2D slices have a finite thickness and consist of elementary building blocks called voxels. Each voxel has a specific grey value. The grey value is a measure of the linear X-ray attenuation coefficient of the materials present in that voxel. It depends on the local density and the chemical element(s) present in the voxel, together with the incident photon energy. The set of parallel 2D slices allows rendering the object in 3D.

Maximum sample size depends on detector size and X-ray source. It varies, together with the maximum sample mass and sample height, with the type or brand of micro-CT scanners. For example, up to  $120\mathrm{mm}$  width,  $200\mathrm{mm}$  height and  $1.5\mathrm{kg}$  mass, samples can be positioned with the Phoenix Nanotom S<sup>®</sup> micro-CT scanner (Zacher et al. 2010). But this is provided that the material is not too dense. The voxel size of micro-CT scans is about  $1/1000^{\mathrm{th}}$  of the sample width at maximum magnification. For extreme sample magnification (i.e., samples of millimetre and submillimetre size), the spatial resolution reaches the submicrometre level. It is governed by the size of the electron beam that hits the anode target within the X-ray tube used as source of X-rays.

Imaging errors such as beam hardening, partial volume, ring, metal streak, conical beam, motion and phase contrast artefacts (not to be confused with archaeological artefacts) are inherent to X-ray micro-CT. Beam hardening is caused by the differential attenuation of polychromatic X-rays through materials of variable density and thickness. Among others, it makes the edge of the scanned object to appear artificially more attenuating (Ketcham & Carlson 2001). Partial volume is related to the presence in a same voxel of materials with different attenuation coefficients. The materials of subvoxel size cannot be distinguished on the scan. Nevertheless the grey value of the voxel to which they belong is an average of their properties (Ketcham & Carlson 2001).

Ring artefact can result from differences in pixel sensitivity in the X-ray detector. It can also be associated with metal streak artefacts (Ketcham & Carlson 2001). Metal streak is generated when a relatively highly attenuating inclusion is aligned parallel to the X-ray beam during sample rotation. It manifests itself as a very bright star with a false void next to it. In the worst cases, false low density rings appear on the 2D slice containing the bright inclusion as well as on the adjacent slices (Ketcham & Carlson 2001; Tarplee et al. 2011). Conical beam artefacts affect 2D slices that are far from the centre of the X-ray beam. Motion artefacts are due to sample movement during scanning. They produce a blurred scan. A phase-contrast effect is caused by X-ray refraction at the boundary between materials with different attenuation coefficients. It enhances edges between these materials. It is strong in objects with a low attenuation coefficient that are scanned at a high resolution (Wilkins et al. 1996). Most micro-CT artefacts cannot be fully mitigated in complex heterogeneous samples (Ketcham & Carlson 2001; Tarplee et al. 2011; Cnudde & Boon 2013) and one has to be aware of these 'artefacts' to avoid misinterpreting the scans.

X-ray micro-CT can also be conducted at one of the few synchrotron facilities available worldwide. Synchotron-based micro-CT scanning is performed with the help of a high quality parallel monochromatic beam. Compared to lab-based micro-CT, synchrotron based micro-CT is faster and provides results with similar or often higher degree of detail. Moreover, it eliminates the beam hardening and conical beam X-ray artefacts mentioned above. Certain X-ray beam lines, in addition to attenuation contrast-based tomography, also allow phase-contrast-based tomography, which is particularly useful for imaging the structure of low-density materials such as organic objects and peat (Bertrand et al. 2012). However, synchrotrons are not as accessible as lab-based micro-CT scanners to the archaeological community. For large archaeological samples, medical or industrial CT scanner can be used at the expense of spatial resolution.

Micro-CT scanning equipment can also now be bought as an add-on to electron microscopes. Recently, micro-CT scanners that yield major element compositional data per voxel, have come onto the market (e.g., the Skyscan® 2140 micro-CT/micro-XRF). For both applications however, maximum sample sizes are much smaller than those for the standard stand-alone machines (e.g., less than  $10\mathrm{mm}$  for the Skyscan® SEM-mounted micro-CT and less than  $13\mathrm{mm}$  for the Skyscan® 2140 micro-CT/micro-XRF). Moreover scanning time is increased. Field versions of transportable micro-CT systems (Tuniz et al. 2013) are also in development.

# 42.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedures

# 42.2.1 Sample Types

Micro-CT scanning can be applied to various types of samples:

- directly onto undisturbed, untreated soil samples;  
- prior to impregnating, on soil samples in Kubiena tins or other types of sample boxes;  
- on impregnated soil blocks or slabs, before or after preparation of a thin section for micromorphological analysis.

For example, Figure 42.1 shows the results from a micro-CT scan employed to check the integrity of a vibrocore-derived sandy sample in an aluminium Kubiëna tin, prior to thin section preparation. Void distribution and sedimentary layering can be discerned clearly. It shows that a micro-CT scan can be used to select samples for thin sectioning and determine the position and the orientation for the plane of thin sectioning that will facilitate the micromorphological interpretation. While the production of thin sections is labour intensive and takes at least several months, a micro-CT scan of good quality can be recorded, processed and analysed in a simple way, within three hours. Multiple samples can also be scanned to check for representativeness.

# 42.2.2 Sample-Related Limitations

# 42.2.2.1 Organic Matter, Water and Resin

The X-ray attenuation contrast between pore fluids and organic matter is low (Quinton et al. 2009). Therefore organic tissues forming peats can only be discerned on micro-CT scans (Figure 42.2) when the samples have lost their free water as well as part of their capillary and bounded water. This, unfortunately, causes irreversible shrinkage and deformation to the fabric of the peat. It is possible, however, to visualize some peat tissues on micro-CT scans by doping the pore fluid, or by staining peat fibres with contrast agents (Kettridge & Binley 2011; van Loo et al. 2014).

Scanning impregnated blocks also has the disadvantage that organic matter has a similar X-ray attenuation coefficient as resin and therefore becomes almost indistinguishable from the resin itself. Carrying out phase contrast-based, instead of attenuation contrast-based micro-CT, in a synchrotron might, however, be more useful for distinguishing organic matter from resin.

# 42.2.2.2 Subsampling

Maximum possible resolution of the scans increases with decreasing sample (or object) size. In order to increase resolution it is sometimes necessary to subsample.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d8ef1e8e7419fd08558a573f43e0a36ea8e4c737a1e04a58e7062ef884445065.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/98be635717a30ae4d1a05ebf15671fd3cd12f9586cf2c2d17032dd84efa39f9e.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/c2f25e20fb595bbd937f306b9cb733db54aa7db7831cd70908e75669d08f959b.jpg)  
Figure 42.1 Example of micromorphological sample in an aluminium Kubiëna tin; sample from mechanically made coring (vibrocore). (a) Combination of two perpendicular cross-sections, showing a fine groundmass (silt – fine sand) with darker domains that are slightly more clayey. The aluminium Kubiëna tin is visible as white lines on the cross-sections. (b) Cross-sections and 3D rendered vesicles. Some layering can still be discerned in the groundmass. The vesicles are concentrated in subhorizontal planes. (c) Higher magnification, showing the vesicles are mostly slightly flattened, indicating they were formed as trapped gas bubbles by exsolution of dissolved gases due to depressurization during the mechanical coring process. Fluvial sand deposit in Roman vicus underneath nineteenth-century earth embankment. Scanning parameters:  $160\mathrm{kV}$ ,  $0.315\mathrm{mA}$ ,  $500\mathrm{ms}$  exposure, four radiograms stacked, one skipped, 1000 images, voxel size:  $32\mu \mathrm{m}$  and after reconstruction:  $64\mu \mathrm{m}$ , no physical filter, beam correction 9.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/cf1a94ffa10d9d4f742eed87ef06f0e052cb4e9aa993f081953ea05b64eca1a0.jpg)  
(a)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3a1221eebbcbd161beaea3ecabeb77401bf3831ddc94a250bd0ef5776be631f7.jpg)  
(b)  
Figure 42.2 Attenuation contrast-based micro-CT scans of a  $65\mathrm{mm}$  diameter reed peat sample. (a) At initial, waterlogged state, sample not fully saturated. (b) After partial desiccation (38% measured mass loss and 6% shrinkage). 1: empty macro-pore, 2: reed rootlet, 3: zone where water and plant tissues cannot be discerned.

The subsample can be a few millimetres wide provided it is larger than the representative elementary volume of the archaeological material. Impregnated blocks can be subsampled by sawing with a diamond-coated saw but the use of a hollow drill has the added advantage that cylindrical samples are produced, therefore limiting potential reconstruction imperfections and streak artefacts (Tarplee et al. 2011). It should be noted that dry core drilling may be necessary when water soluble material - e.g. PEG - has been used to impregnate the sample.

# 42.2.3 Image Processing and Analyses

Processed micro-CT scans form in essence a 3D model of the linear attenuation coefficient of the material in the sample. Dedicated software such as Fiji (Schlindelin et al. 2012), Morpho  $^+$  (Brabant et al. 2011) and AVIZO is subsequently used to visualize and analyse this model, allowing for multiple cross-sections with arbitrary orientations to be displayed simultaneously. The interior of a 3D data set can be viewed in real time (from any position) using an intuitive technique called volume rendering. This technique simulates light transmission through the object from the viewing point, making the voxels more-or-less semitransparent, so that they do not obstruct the view of the voxels placed behind them. Subsequently, images can be segmented into diverse components to allow a quantitative analysis of each component. Materials or features that are distinct because of their attenuation – like organic material, sand grains, bone fragments, shells and pores – can be identified, separated using segmentation tools and counted before being visualized (see Figures 42.3 and 42.4) and subjected to digital size, shape, and orientation analysis. Segmentation requires image thresholding, in other words, image conversion based on grey levels into binary images in order to select the phase (voids, groundmass, coarse fraction, inclusions, artefacts etc.) of interest. It separates discrete zones or object with the same phase into individual objects that touch each other in reality or appear visually to be connected on the scan because of lack of density or spatial resolution of the scan. At each step of the processing, including image noise reduction, the operator can choose between several (semi)automatic techniques, each technique requiring the setting of a few parameters (Brabant et al. 2011).

The results of the separation analysis should be inspected carefully as segmentation based on semiautomatic grey level thresholding and the application of denoising in images can be subjective and separation of adjacent items having a complex geometry can fail (Cnudde & Boon 2013; Maire & Whitters 2014).

Micro-CT scanning of soil samples or impregnated blocks in combination with micromorphology gives a

much-desired third dimension to microarchaeology (see Weiner 2010). If scans and slides come from the same sample, the three-dimensional shape and articulations of an object's aggregates, domains or pores/cavities can be studied (see Figure 42.2). In many cases, the addition of 3D spatial information helps in understanding structures and objects that are visible in thin section, whereas micromorphological studies help to identify materials and features distinguished in micro-CT scans.

# 42.3 Archaeological Applications

The combination of micro-CT scanning and micromorphology for archaeological research is rather new and only a few examples have as yet been published. At an early stage in the development of high-resolution X-ray micro-CT, Adderley et al. (2001) investigated the correspondence between 2D image analysis of conventional thin sections with, 2D and 3D image analyses of micro-CT scans, for samples from midden deposits. They also illustrated the possibility of conducting a 3D spatial analysis of soil constituents (pores, minerals and organic matter) for a more robust interpretation of archaeological deposits.

Huisman et al. (2014) demonstrated the usefulness of the technique for studying midden material that is rich in small bones or bone fragments. Thin sections can reveal the presence of such bones; however, species identification and spatial distribution of bones are hard to derive from a thin section, which is essentially a random cut through the groundmass. Alternatively, sieving such a sample would allow identification but, conversely, destroys microstratigraphic information. Micro-CT scanning of soil samples, however, in combination with surface rendering of high density material, makes it possible to identify small bones to family or species level (Figure 42.4) - even though secondary iron oxides were also included in the high-density material. It reveals context information, making it possible to discern for example food preparation refuse (dominated by fish scales) from tossed or discarded meal residues (dominated by larger fish bones). In the same context, dietary information can be gleaned by targeting bone-rich faecal material, identified in thin section, and recognizing the bones embedded within it. To reach the resolution needed for this, however, it may be necessary to scan a subsample cut from an impregnated block (see Figure 42.5).

Ngan-Tillard et al. (2014, 2016) demonstrated the applicability of a combined micromorphology and micro-CT approach for archaeological heritage management. They studied structural damage in archaeological remains subjected to mechanical stress, such as that

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/926c1778ec1376258d6497c7570b940123b16ad39389e3cde31539ac6ce1c146.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/781b40c60112db93b417070440e2c807cc3e9347c6b32c6d00d22ffbab7ce088.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/33a4c71e3bff04ec2ff2a27a0a6817ee3d5d4ba35a0df2fb3f0c852340b611ff.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/a6c3cdc98a134bfff1bf9d5c7e6e3c7acd28b989fc2db996d36045bd842b7d7a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/0d95e1e7aaa7562cd7277ef68b010d23f36c308e6f9b22ee4ee1f13e1f77a3da.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3db8339685832fe2cc31482a4bc64589c16f0149322caeb8515acc328e9b2095.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/94d61be29a17f67d8910a53be112e74a5d716bd444c51966b689a54510cd5cd4.jpg)  
Figure 42.3 Multi-step analysis of void distribution and properties in a clay sample. (a) Cross-section of the sample in XY plane showing a homogeneous fine groundmass (light grey) with spherical voids and elongated vugs (dark). (b) Voids selected with thresholding. Light blue voids are connected to the outside; the other coloured voids are constrained within the sample. (c) Same as (b), after filtering. Only voids  $>0.001\mathrm{m}^3$  that are constrained within the samples are left. (d) Smoothened 3D surface of the voids. (e) Semi-3D view of void surface without cross section. (f) and (g) Frequency distributions of length and width of the individual voids respectively. (h) Frequency diagram of the sphericity of the voids, showing one group of spherical voids (sphericity  $\sim 1$ ) and one with vugs (sphericity\~. 0.2). (i) Biplot of sphericity and size, showing small spherical voids and larger, nonspherical vugs. The former probably represents rounded diatoms, whereas the latter are fissures or root channels that have been deformed due to tillage. Swifterbant S4 Neolithic site, The Netherlands.

Figure 42.4 (a) Combined perpendicular cross sections of a  $53 \times 47 \times 26\mathrm{mm}$  subsection of a scanned soil sample, showing deposits with a finely laminated groundmass (grey banding) with voids (black) and embedded bones and bone fragments (white). (d) All segmented and volume-rendered bone fragments in the scanned sample, showing layering that represents separate dumping events. ![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ea05003103d373f6932c3be830d2dda5b30626fe5c6bc786cb46ab4fe737baa2.jpg)

which occurs underneath buildings or embankments. By comparing scans and thin sections from samples taken prior to and after loading (or outside and inside the area of loading), the approach can show changes in the soil structure and damage to objects such as charcoal and organic tissue. McBride & Mercer (2012) and Ngan-Tillard et al. (2014) found that fragile archaeological remains (like organic matter, charcoal and shells) embedded in sediment, remained undamaged under one dimensional stress, equivalent to a loading of several meters of soil. Ngan-Tillard et al. (2016) also observed some realignment and compression of noncharred organic tissue in micro-CT scans but found no damage at the cellular scale in the thin sections made after experimentally compressing the sample.

Since the start of the twenty-first century, the use of laboratory-based microtomography in geotechnology has already allowed many breakthroughs in our understanding of the mechanical as well as coupled processes taking place in soils (Viggiani et al. 2015).

# 42.4 Concluding Remarks

The literature cited in this chapter (albeit scarce) demonstrates the potential for a combined approach of soil micromorphology and micro-CT scans, for archaeological use: a wealth of information on past human activities can be derived from samples taken at

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/953db2543016efa6f487d7ef0a59e756a66d34d728cba0dd4b5333c4a69cf30d.jpg)  
Figure 42.5 (a) Bone-rich coprolite (arrows) embedded in phytolith-dominated groundmass; thin section from midden material. (b) Micro-CT scan of the impregnated block after the thin section in (a) was taken. Cross-section with superimposed volume-rendered bone fragments (note that iron oxide coatings in root channels are included as well and cannot be separated automatically from bone fragments). (c) Bone rendering in  $1\mathrm{cm}$ -wide subsample of the faecal material, indicated with circle in (b), including three fish vertebrae among many unidentified bone fragments. Middle Neolithic midden deposit, Swifterbant S4, The Netherlands (see Huisman et al. 2009, 2014).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/8e90d9356792364bf4234ae9ff1df1ca6eef73d21a8eb07e77e1ab818f665817.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/58d81e2b5750a063937a8971bbf761bad3b3b038919f7351a49ec059d93ced9a.jpg)

archaeological sites, using such a combined approach. In the future, the accessibility of micro-CT and its application in archaeological soil micromorphology are expected to increase. Specific areas in which the application of micro-CT has great potential include:

- identification of animal and plant species based on seeds, small fish bones, shells, and so forth, embedded in sediments (see Huisman et al. 2014);  
- quantification of taphonomic and soil forming processes like bioturbation through the combined analysis of thin sections and micro-CT scans (see Taina et al. 2010 for applications in soil science);  
- characterization of the 3D size, shape and distribution of voids, aggregates and so forth, to analyse buried soils and surfaces (Adderley et al. 2001) in order to study landscape, land use and human activities;  
- identifying and quantifying degradation of archaeological sites due to plant roots, soil fauna and so forth.

Micro-CT allows visualization and analysis of distribution patterns of specific materials in archaeological sites, but only for rather strongly contrasting materials (or after enhancing contrast with doping agents) with little information on the exact nature of these materials. Thin section analysis is a much more powerful method, with full identification possibilities and higher resolution, but has the limitation that it is only two dimensional. An approach in which both techniques are combined plays to the strengths of both techniques.

Future improvements of the micro-CT technique, like increased compositional information per voxel, phase contrast-based micro-CT (in synchrotrons) and portable equipment, are expected to extend the possibilities even further. Moreover, the considerable progress that is foreseen in the (semi)automatic fusion of 2D or 3D data sets of various sources – including from thin sections – with micro-CT scans (Otani & Obara 2003; Desrues et al. 2006; Alshibli et al. 2010; Cnudde & Bernard 2013) can only support further development.

43

# Electron Probe X-ray Microanalysis (SEM-EPMA) Techniques

Clare A. Wilson

Biological and Environmental Sciences, School of Natural Sciences, University of Stirling, United Kingdom

# 43.1 Principles of the Techniques

Scanning electron microscope-mounted electron probe X-ray microanalysis (SEM-EPMA) methods take advantage of the fact that the high-energy electron beam may displace an electron from an inner orbit of the atoms of the material being studied. When this happens, an electron from a higher energy outer orbit may replace the displaced one, leading to the release of an X-ray whose energy and wavelength is characteristic of the element to which the atom belongs (Brundle et al. 1992). SEM-EPMA includes two different techniques: energy dispersive (SEM-EDX) and wavelength dispersive (SEM-WDX) X-ray spectroscopy.

SEM-EDX (also referred to as SEM-EDS, EDAX, EDXMA, EDXA or XEDS) uses a detector that converts the energy of each X-ray collected to a voltage of proportional size. In this way, it simultaneously constructs a complete energy spectrum of all the X-rays produced from all the elements in the material being analysed. By contrast, SEM-WDX (also referred to as SEM-WDS or microprobe) uses diffracting crystal spectrometers optimized for the elements of interest. The two techniques have relative advantages and drawbacks as outlined in Table 43.1, but generally SEM-WDX offers better detection limits and higher precision and accuracy, whilst SEM-EDX is quicker, cheaper and more widely available (Leng 2013), and for many applications is more than adequate.

A large number of other microanalytical SEM mounted techniques may be applied to thin sections including PIXIE, XRF, Raman, and IR (Mentzer & Quade 2013). However, these are less widely available and often require specialized sample preparation (e.g., self-supporting sections, plastic mounts, or thick sections) in contrast to the minimal preparation required for SEM-EDX and SEM-WDX analysis.

# 43.2 Sample Preparation and Analysis

# 43.2.1 Sample Preparation

Thin sections are ideal for SEM-EPMA as their polished surfaces eliminate shadow effects (Leng 2013). For both SEM-EDX and -WDX analyses, the thin sections must not be cover slipped, while careful cleaning of the slide is recommended to avoid contamination from the lapping fluids or lapping plate (Figure 43.1). The size of a typical thin section is also a consideration, as, depending on the chamber size and stage travel of the SEM used, they may need to be cut to size to fit within the SEM (Mentzer & Quade 2013).

The application of a conductive coating may be required to prevent the buildup of excessive static charge. For microanalysis, carbon is generally used in preference to gold as it interferes less with the electron beam and the X-rays produced (Limandri et al. 2010). However, carbon cannot be used if it is itself of interest (Wilson et al. 2013a). Coating can be avoided altogether if the SEM is configured to operate at lower vacuum conditions as the introduction of air into the SEM chamber protects the specimen from charging. It is also advisable to carry out resin stability tests as higher electron beam energies can cause damage (Figure 43.2) to the sample surface.

Another consideration is the ability to locate the features of interest in the SEM, particularly if whole thin sections are being used. Images at multiple scales of the features of interest and whole slide scans of the section may be beneficial. Some modern SEM systems offer image navigation whereby features can be located from a scan of the thin section that includes three known points; metal tape, etchings or the corners of the thin section may aid in this process.

Table 43.1 Summary of the relative advantages and disadvantages of SEM-EDX and SEM-WDX (compiled from Brundle et al. 1992; Guillemette 2008; Leng 2013).  

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Property</td><td colspan="2">Technique</td></tr><tr><td>SEM-EDX</td><td>SEM-WDX</td></tr><tr><td>Detection limit (Wt%)</td><td>0.1%</td><td>0.01%</td></tr><tr><td>Elements</td><td>Modern system elements heavier than B, some older systems are limited to elements heavier than Na</td><td>Elements heavier than B</td></tr><tr><td>Number of elements per analysis</td><td>Full spectral analysis allowing retrospective identification of any element within the given detection limits. Lower beam energies may not excite heavier elements, but offer better resolution of lighter elements.</td><td>Typically limited to five preselected elements depending on the detector configuration of the system.</td></tr><tr><td>Speed (point analysis)</td><td>Minutes (subminute for modern large crystal systems)</td><td>Tens of minutes</td></tr><tr><td>Precision and accuracy</td><td>Lower precision and accuracy</td><td>Higher precision and accuracy</td></tr><tr><td>Availability</td><td>Widely available, almost standard on most SEM systems</td><td>Less widely available as a more specialized and costly system</td></tr></table>

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/3721eb66adac6dd9dfa3ed4fe7769875b0449a85f28155b2f7c811722368a1f1.jpg)  
Figure 43.1 SEM-WDX map of relative Fe concentrations in a soil thin section from a post-Medieval cultivated soil, Shetland, UK showing the accumulation of Fe due to contamination from the lapping plate in flaws in the resin and unimpregnated voids in charcoal fragments.

# 43.2.2 Sampling Considerations

SEM-EDX and -WDX analyses take two forms: point analysis and mapping (whereby the electron beam scans over the material surface). Point analyses can provide quantitative chemical data from individual points, grids or transects of points (Figure 43.3). Mapping provides semiquantitative data on element distributions across fields of view that are typically less than  $4\mathrm{mm}^2$  (Figures 43.4 and 43.5). As well as scanning across two-dimensional areas of the slide, it is also possible

to produce a line scan demonstrating changes in the relative concentrations of elements along a transect (Figure 43.6). The sampling strategy needs to be chosen carefully depending on the questions being asked and the nature of the material being analysed.

It is important to be aware of the volume of material that is being analysed (the interaction volume), which depends on the composition of the material and the SEM operating conditions. Typical interaction volumes are in the order of  $2\mu \mathrm{m}$  in diameter and depth (Walker 2014).

# 43.2.3 Data Considerations

Element mapping is not normally quantitative because each image pixel contains too few X-ray counts to enable quantification and the assessment of concentrations as both weight percentage and atomic percentage for each element. If optimization standards are used to account

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/c7de23c966c89dfc09973302187200b845509c7fb3a1592fcac3876fd5628dd9.jpg)  
Figure 43.2 SEM-BSE image of beam damage to cryptic resin caused by SEM-WDX analysis at  $15\mathrm{kV}$ $200\mathrm{nA}$

for beam drift, then point analysis on thin sections can be fully quantitative, particularly if sample-relevant external standards are also used to account for matrix effects and assess data accuracy (Williamson et al. 2004). Data acquired without the use of standards is typically normalized to  $100\%$ , but can be misleading as it excludes trace elements and will be influenced by the introduction of carbon and oxygen from the resin (Smith 2010; Falsone et al. 2014). However, even normalized data can be useful as long as element ratios are used in any comparisons rather than absolute values (Wilson et al. 2013b).

# 43.3 Archaeological Applications

Whilst still not routine, the complementarity of SEM-EPMA techniques to traditional micromorphological analysis has been long recognized (Bisdom et al. 1981; Jongmans et al. 1999). As opposed to bulk chemical analysis, SEM-EPMA analysis of soil thin sections allows specific soil components to be targeted and enables the chemistry of these materials to be considered within their undisturbed soil or sedimentary contexts. Thus, its value in archaeological studies has been widely

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/1edcbdea7b02d215b8c0399d6b16f35a0180af1ebadf7200a7c8a8fe966b4821.jpg)

<table><tr><td></td><td>Wt%</td><td>σ</td></tr><tr><td>C</td><td>47.8</td><td>0.6</td></tr><tr><td>O</td><td>29.4</td><td>0.4</td></tr><tr><td>N</td><td>11.4</td><td>0.9</td></tr><tr><td>S</td><td>4.6</td><td>0.1</td></tr><tr><td>Ca</td><td>4.0</td><td>0.1</td></tr><tr><td>Si</td><td>1.9</td><td>0.0</td></tr><tr><td>P</td><td>0.9</td><td>0.0</td></tr></table>

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/67a64c3051a8c38bf3813bba31754002907de1f4becc3ecddcbfb0014ac953ad.jpg)  
Figure 43.3 Semiquantitative SEM-EDX point analysis spectrum and concentration data from hair embedded in recrystallized  $\mathrm{CaCO_3}$  in a Bronze Age cist burial, Scotland (Lelong 2014).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/4b40027dc83aa09b5884e22ddb6b60cb8a1214ff818d432c91e0a6bea59e9b81.jpg)  
Figure 43.4 SEM-WDX map of relative Zn concentrations, showing post depositional enhancement within fish bone in a post Medieval cultivated soil, Shetland, UK.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/471df69c441cb131e7e6d5f7a6a07c855a9a42de0e6fe4f7823e3ab793d20540.jpg)  
Figure 43.5 Semiquantitative SEM-EDX map of Pb, Fe, Si, O and C distributions in sediments from an eighteenth- to nineteenth-century Pb ore-processing site, Tyndrum, UK.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/ad58d8283cec3e1b91b2051b7797f83aa1a0a4e3294b809f83533791a3b57eb3.jpg)  
Figure 43.6 SEM-EDX line scan across a Fe impregnated root showing the changes in the relative concentration of iron (blue) and oxygen (red).

acknowledged (Weiner 2010). Archaeological applications typically fall into three categories:

- the identification or confirmation of specific minerals or archaeological materials in thin section;  
- chemical characterization of the soil fine mass often with the aim of determining its source(s);  
- analysis of the taphonomic alteration of archaeological materials.

# 43.3.1 Identification of Archaeological Materials, Ecofacts and Minerals

The identification (or confirmation) of specific archaeological materials and minerals represents the main application of SEM-EPMA techniques to archaeological soil thin sections. Both point analysis and element mapping have been used to identify soil minerals (e.g., Tsatskin & Nadel 2003; Correa et al. 2013) and in phosphate rich archaeological contexts the analysis of secondary neoformed minerals such as vivianite, metalliferous spherulites and Ca Fe phosphates (Figure 43.5) has been particularly beneficial (Leguédois et al. 2004; Correa et al. 2013; Macphail et al. 2013). Not only have SEM-EPMA techniques been able to confirm the micromorphological interpretation of these features but the chemical data obtained has added to our understanding of the conditions of their formation, whether as vivianite in dark earths subject to groundwater saturation (Nicosia 2009), calcium phosphates forming crusts in the acidic, anaerobic conditions of stabling deposits (Macphail et al. 2004), as cemented iron calcium phosphate bands in ash rich Bronze Age pit fills subjected to wetting and drying (Nicosia 2009) or as coatings in dark earths with impeded drainage (Nicosia et al. 2012).

The microchemical study of a wide range of archaeological materials and ecofacts in soil thin sections has previously included SEM/EDX analysis of coprolites (Nicosia et al. 2012; Nicosia & Devos 2014 - Figure 43.7), faecal spherulites (Canti 1997), bone (Figure 43.4), pottery and pottery glazes (Lang, 2014) and charcoal (Davidson et al. 2007a). SEM-EDX can highlight the presence of Ca and P in suspected carnivore-omnivore coprolite fragments, as these are normally highly phosphatic (Bronniman et al. 2017, this book) and/or characterized by a hydroxyapatite cement (Macphail & Goldberg 2010), enabling a more robust identification (Figure 43.8).

SEM-EDX analyses have also been used to distinguish anthropogenic microcharcoal from opaque mafic minerals such as ilmenite (Davidson et al. 2007b), coal (Wilson et al. 2008) and humified organic matter (Davidson et al. 2007a) based on O:C ratios and trace-element concentrations.

# 43.3.2 Soil Chemical Characterization / Sourcing

Integrating bulk chemical analyses with microstratigraphic soil micromorphology has long been a problem for studies of archaeology (Shillito et al. 2009; Weiner 2010; Mentzer & Quade 2013). Even when coarser sedimentary units are examined (e.g., Devos et al. 2013; Kuhn et al. 2010) the difference in the scale of observation between the disturbed bulk samples and the strata and microartefacts observed micromorphologically can limit interpretation. SEM-EPMA techniques allow for greater spatial control in such analyses and the benefits for microstratigraphic analysis are clear, for example in the study of floor layers (Macphail et al. 2004) and to characterize specific microfabrics (Macphail et al. 1998; Maghsoudi et al. 2014) to aid in the interpretation of parent materials and site formation processes. Whilst the advantages of microanalysis are clear, the wider representativeness of the data must be borne in mind if it is to be used to identify wider land management practices such as identifying chemical signatures of manuring and land management practices in Norse Greenland, highlighting differences in nutrient loadings (P, K, Ca and Mg) between fields (Golding et al. 2015), or to characterize chemically and trace the movement of midden material in prehistoric field systems in Orkney, UK (Hamlet 2014).

# 43.3.3 Taphonomic Alteration of Archaeological Materials

SEM-EPMA analysis of soil thin sections is particularly suited for taphonomic studies of artefacts and organic matter (Falsone et al. 2014; Wilson et al. 2013a) as they are retained in an undisturbed state within the soil environment. Thus, interactions between the soil and the

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/1e6b04d01a914ea72ce4f74db0cfa0597702c0caddd6bf5fea680896be11b393.jpg)  
Figure 43.7 SEM-EDX as an aid in identifying secondary phosphates in a Bronze Age negative structure with traces of strong hydromorphism and abundant faecal material (see Nicosia 2009). (a) Accumulations of clay and phosphates in bands. (b) Detail view of one of the bands marked by arrows in (a), showing a dense complete yellowish infilling. This part has been analysed with an ESEM equipped with EDX. PPL. (c) Same as (b), XPL. Note optical isotropy. (d) EDX spectrum showing the relative abundances of P, Fe and Ca (secondary Ca-Fe phosphates). Si and Al derive from clays. Image courtesy of C. Nicosia.

artefact edges can be studied in much greater detail and it is possible to understand taphonomic processes within the context of soil structure, void space (Smith 2010; Falsone et al. 2014) and stratigraphy (Correa et al. 2013).

Studies on bone, for example, have examined both its decomposition (Estevez et al. 2014) and its postdepositional uptake of metals (Davidson et al. 2007a; Wilson et al. 2008), whereas Van Hoesen & Arriaza (2011) have

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/d02d30be386df5f2844af7e1fa577ffe61dfb8e34bd4a081aa4712436031f91c.jpg)  
Figure 43.8 Suspected carnivore-omnivore coprolite fragment, with traces of weathering and vivianite neoformation (arrows). The SEM-EDX spectrum was obtained in the area marked by the red rectangle and highlighted the presence of Ca, P and Fe. Carbon-coated sample from a Bronze Age ditch fill (site of Fondo Paviani, Italy - see Nicosia 2015). Image courtesy of C. Nicosia.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/42d4c236e5f67c86bbf1c8cd7b79bffb09a6a373fc9cb8b5c37c1325619586c3.jpg)

used SEM-EDX in combination with micromorphology to show the origin of sediments used to encase Chinchorro mummies in Chile and to understand the role of these gypsum and clay rich sediments in the

mummification process. SEM-EPMA analysis of bone in soil thin section may also enable study of the migration of elements such as P, Ca and Fe away from the degrading body through the soil (Usai et al. 2014).

# Reflected Light

Bertrand Ligouis<sup>1,2</sup>

$^{1}$  Institut für Naturwissenschaftliche Archäologie, Abt. Organische Petrologie, Eberhard-Karls-Universität Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany  
$^{2}$  Labor für Angewandte Organische Petrologie – LAOP, Eberhard-Karls-Universität Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany

# 44.1 Principles of the Analytical Method

Reflected light microscopy is used mainly in the petrographic analysis of thin sections in archaeological contexts to identify and classify the organic components. It is appropriate for determining the metallic minerals (pyrite, marcasite, hematite, goethite, manganese oxides, etc.), which are generally opaque in thin section (Picot & Johan 1982).

The methodology used to study the organic components is that of organic petrology, which was developed principally from the years 1963 to 1995 for the petrographic study of peat, brown coal, hard coals, source rocks and other rocks containing organic carbon (Diessel 1992; Taylor et al. 1998; Suárez-Ruiz et al. 2012a). The organic components, named macerals, are defined on the basis of their morphography (morphology, internal appearance), their physical properties (colour and reflectance, anisotropy, fluorescence, polishing hardness) and their chemical properties. The microscopy techniques of organic petrology therefore involve the combined use of reflectance and incident fluorescence microscopy.

Several maceral classifications exist but the most used and accepted is that of the International Committee for Coal and Organic Petrology (ICCP), which was originally developed for brown coals and hard coal (ICCP 1963, 1971, 1975) and then completed for organic-matter-rich sediments other than coal, including oil shales and petroleum-source rocks (ICCP 1993; Taylor et al. 1998). Three groups of macerals may be distinguished: the vitrinite group, which comprises macerals derived from plant tissues composed of lignin and cellulose (ICCP 1998); the liptinite group, which embraces macerals derived from hydrogen-rich plant remains such as pollen, spores, algae, cuticles, resins, waxes; and the inertinite group whose macerals are derived from the same precursors as

vitrinite and liptinite but have been subject to dehydration and oxidation by alteration, or by charring induced by wild fires (ICCP 2001). It is important to note that the optical properties of macerals change during coalification. This is clearly visible by comparing the plant tissues in soft brown coals (low coalified), which appear dark grey in reflected light and show dark brown fluorescence, with those in bituminous coals (more coalified), which are medium grey in reflected light and mostly nonfluorescent (Teichmüller 1989). The ICCP therefore established two distinct maceral classifications for the coalification products of humic substances (originated from lignocellulosic plant tissues) in brown coals (termed also lignites) and in hard coals: the classification of huminite (Sýkorová et al. 2005) and the classification of vitrinite (ICCP 1998). The classification of huminite is generally the most appropriate for petrographic analysis of humic substances in peat (Grady et al. 1993; Calder 1993), in soils (Jacob 1972, 1973) and, by extension, in archaeological soils and sediments (Stahlschmidt et al. 2015a). Other organic components, such as, for example, natural char, whose formation could be related to ground fire or surface fire and which occurs in coals and sediments, were not included by ICCP in the three maceral groups (Kwiecińska & Petersen 2004).

# 44.2 Sampling and Analytical Procedure

# 44.2.1 Sample Preparation for Organic Petrology Study in Archaeological Contexts

Although the organic petrological investigations are usually carried out on polished particulate mounts or on polished blocks (Ligouis 2006; Goldberg et al. 2009), thin sections employed by micromorphologists can also be

used to investigate organic components in reflected light. However, some rules must be followed to ensure the quality of petrographic analyses.

The block samples must be very well impregnated (if possible under vacuum) with nonfluorescing or low-fluorescing resin (for example epoxy resins) before the preparation of the thin sections. Thin sections must not have a thickness of less than  $30\mu \mathrm{m}$ . Thicker thin sections have advantages because they increase the cohesion and stability of the organic particles (especially charcoal and all tissues in general) and thus the accuracy of analysis. Thin sections can be prepared following the standard methods. However, the fine grind must be extended to a 2500 grit size SiC  $(8\mu \mathrm{m})$ , followed by a diamond  $(3\mu \mathrm{m})$  rough-polish step on a polishing cloth and an alumina  $(0.05\mu \mathrm{m})$  fine-polish step on a soft very low nap synthetic polishing cloth, in order to obtain an uniformly flat thin section with relief- and scratch-free organic particles (see also standard coal sample preparation procedures in Taylor et al. 1998). Rough polishing and fine polishing steps are performed with decreasing low pressures using wet polishing cloths. A lap speed of 150 to  $100\mathrm{rpm}$  is generally suitable. In the case of poorly impregnated or soft and fragile samples, the use of dry polishing methods is much more appropriate but more laborious. Thin sections cannot be covered because organic petrography analyses are carried out using oil immersion objectives.

# 44.2.2 Equipment and Analytical Procedures

To carry out the petrographic analyses, a reflected light microscope photometer equipped for fluorescence examination with the capability of switching to white light illumination and setup with oil immersion objectives  $20\mathrm{x}$  and  $50\mathrm{x}$  is used. The immersion oil used has a refractive index of  $\mathrm{ne}(546\mathrm{nm}) = 1.518$  (at  $23^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). The identification of organic components and the assessment of their optical properties are carried out in combined incident white light (reflectance) and UV light illumination (example of equipment: excitation filter  $355-425\mathrm{nm}$ , beam-splitting mirror RKP  $455\mathrm{nm}$ , barrier filter LP470 nm).

There are two types of methods: qualitative and quantitative. The qualitative method is descriptive and delivers, for example, a result expressed as 'present', 'absent' or 'abundant' (Stahlschmidt et al. 2015a). Based on morphological and optical properties (see above), the method enables to identify organic components or macerals of different origin. For example, the presence or absence of burnt tissues (fusinite) or of nongelified tissues (textinite) will provide information on the origin of the tissues as well as the processes that could have affected the mineral matrix in which they are cemented. Nevertheless,

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/bedc544e-6532-4e7f-9c47-701c6e06b76e/60c1119cae0a99eac0bb174a726b9d03cfd72762a2ce0c69a4315f8b2dfc5088.jpg)  
Figure 44.1 The map shows the deep interior chamber of the Hohtle Fels Cave (Swabian Jura, Germany) and the location of the samples HF 10 and HF 14 whose organic petrology results are shown in Figures 44.2, 44.3, 44.4, 44.5. The cave contains a stratified sequence of occupation spanning the Middle Palaeolithic to the Magdalenian (Goldberg et al. 2003). The Gravettian-aged 'burnt-bone horizon' 3cf has been interpreted as the result of anthropogenic 'dumping' of combusted material (Schiegl et al. 2003).

qualitative analysis will permit the detection of organic components, to determine their origin, to assess their preservation grade, and to use them in the reconstruction of the depositional conditions of the sediment (Figures 44.1 and 44.2; Table 44.1).

Two quantitative methods can be used in the case of archaeological applications: maceral analysis and reflectance analysis.

Maceral analysis is the quantitative estimation of the proportions of macerals in any sediment (Taylor et al. 1998; Schneider & Göthel 2001). This can be performed on either a mineral-free basis or mineral-containing basis. It is carried out using incident white and blue light illumination, in combination. The microscope should be set up with an oil immersion objective  $50\mathrm{x}$  and oculars  $10\mathrm{x}$ , one containing a cross hair. An automatic point counter is fitted to the microscope stage and connected to a PC. The analysed lines run perpendicular to the bedding. It is recommended to count 1000 points. The spacing of the points or step length is chosen in order to ensure the coverage of the surface of the thin section. The step length along each line must be identical to the spacing between the lines. When the grid point coincides with a maceral, this is determined and recorded. Maceral analysis results are expressed in

Figure 44.2 Organic petrology analysis on polished thin sections. Scanned images of the thin sections showing the spatial distribution of charcoal (fusinite, inertodetrinite) and of other organic constituents (sporinite, funginite, huminite), of combustion residues from bones (partially burnt bones, bone char, fat-derived char), of permineralized root tissues, of phytoliths and of anthropogenic lithocomponents (microclasts of Lower Jurassic bituminous shale). The inertodetrinite-rich microlayer recorded in thin section HF 10-2 (b) can be interpreted as an undisturbed hearth basis, which is supported by the presence of fusinite. However, fusinite tissues can disappear completely by permineralization (c). The origin of the root tissues observed in thin sections HF 10-2, HF 14-2, HF 14-3 ((b), (c) and (e) respectively) is not clear because of their permineralization. The presence of fungal remains in thin sections HF 14-2 and HF 14-3 ((c) and (e) respectively) indicates that fungal degradation took place in the sediment and that a part of the original organic matter has been degraded. The identification of microclasts of Lower Jurassic bituminous shale of anthropogenic origin in all thin sections was made possible through the use of fluorescence. These microclasts are wastes produced by the extraction of jet from oil shales, which were introduced in the cave. Jet artefacts have been effectively recorded in several caves in the Swabian Mountains, including Hohe Fels Cave (Conard & Uerpmann 1999).

Table 44.1 The results of organic petrological analyses may be ideally summarized in data tables. Table 44.1 integrates the results of the qualitative petrographic analysis complemented by observations (particle frequency, particle size, fluorescence colour and intensity of liptinite macerals, state of preservation of organic particles, heat-affected particle or not, mineralization, etc.), the results of the reflectance analysis of charcoal (fusinite) and of the different types of fat-derived char based on their colour (dark grey, middle grey, light grey) and heterogeneity or homogeneity. This representation has the advantage of allowing easy comparison of samples. Thus, we note that fusinite is much more frequent in the sampling locality HF 10 and especially in the sample HF 10-2, which contains the burnt bone layer. The fusinite is characterized by a relatively low reflectance of about  $0.95\% \mathrm{R}$  (low reflecting fusinite), which has been recorded in both sampling localities. It is assumed that this fusinite was formed at temperatures lower than  $400^{\circ}C$  (see our unpublished data from fire experiments; Jones et al. 1993; Bustin and Guo 1999). Parameters such as preservation state and size of charcoal tissues and fat-derived char particles can be taken into account to determine if a sequence represents in situ burning or if the combustions residues have been reworked or trampled. The mixing of unburnt and burnt organic components may also suggest their redeposition. In addition, the results indicate that the samples are poor in fusinite and rich in fat-derived char of the categories middle grey and light grey. The size of the fat-derived char particles is very heterogeneous and the particles show a great variety of char textures (low-porosity char, char with large vesicles, homogeneous char, heterogeneous char, fissured char). Phosphatization processes have affected numerous tissues (Figures 44.4c and 44.5g), as well as fat-derived char particles. It can be concluded that bones were the principal fuel and that the burnt bone layer probably is not an in situ burning event.

volume percentage (percentage of the total of points counted). Triangular diagrams can be used for example to visualize the relative proportions of the three maceral groups huminite (H), liptinite (L), inertinite (I) or to depict the variability in petrographic composition (the corner points H, L, I represent samples consisting of pure huminite, liptinite or inertinite respectively) or for the classification of microfacies types.

Reflectance analysis is an important tool in organic petrology and is employed to determine the rank of coal and the maturation level of the sedimentary organic matter (Jacob 1980; Taylor et al. 1998; Volkmann 2003; Pross et al. 2007). Reflectance is measured on a single maceral of the vitrinite maceral group in coals and on individual vitrinite particles in sediments. In the study of archaeological soils, reflectance analysis is a useful tool, on the one hand to distinguish between huminite and low reflecting charcoal particles and on the other hand to characterize the charcoal particles (Figure 44.3; Table 44.1). Reflectance measurements on huminite and charcoal particles are conducted as described in Taylor et al. (1998) for the determination of vitrinite reflectance. A  $50\mathrm{x}$  oil immersion objective is used and a small measuring area (about  $2\mu \mathrm{m}$  in diameter) is exposed to the standard monochromatic light  $(\lambda = 546\mathrm{nm})$ . The random reflectance (Rr) is measured in nonpolarized light. The photometric system is calibrated using standard materials of known reflectance. The whole surface of the thin section is scanned and Rr readings are recorded for each huminite and/or charcoal particle depending on the aim of the study. However, particles smaller than  $5\mu \mathrm{m}$  in diameter and those showing strong polishing relief, scratches or impurities are ignored. Only one reading is taken for each particle. For each thin section, the results are given as mean random reflectance. The form of the reflectance histogram, the standard deviation and the number of readings therefore refer to the distribution of the reflectance in the whole thin section and to the abundance of the measured organic constituents (Figure 44.3, Table 44.1). Details of the instrumentation and the analysis procedure of reflectance measurements are described in the International Handbook of Coal Petrography (ICCP 1971), in Taylor et al. (1998) and in Borrego et al. (2006).

# 44.3 Archaeological Applications

Methods of organic petrology can be used for a wide range of archaeological applications. Organic petrology has proved especially useful for the study of sediments containing artefacts in order to confirm or infer the existence of occupational surfaces including hearths (Stahlschmidt et al. 2015b). In this work, macerals and organic microfacies based on maceral composition have been used as pal

aeo-environmental indicators for the reconstruction of the depositional environment of a fresh-water swamp, which was supposed to be the location of hunting.

Similarly, the determination of organic microfacies of archaeological sediments and soils using organic petrography in most cases allows the original organic matter (for example, the association of charcoal and natural char from wildfire occurring together with huminitic plant remains and funginite) to be distinguished from the anthropogenic organic components (charcoal associated with fat-derived char, bone char and partially charred bones – Figures 44.4 and 44.5), and therefore provides information on anthropogenic activities (Goldberg et al. 2009; Villagran et al. 2013; Clark & Ligouis 2010). However, it is important to take into account the alteration and degradation processes (oxidation, bacterial and fungal degradation) which could be responsible for the poor state of preservation of the organic matter or its disappearance.

Furthermore, in recent years, organic petrology has proven to be an important tool for the characterization of hearths (Figures 44.2, 44.3, 44.4 and 44.5). Organic petrological analysis of hearths allows, on the one hand, the identification and classification of combustion residues (Ligouis 2006) and consequently the determination of the fuel used. On the other hand, it provides information on combustion conditions (combustion temperature, air humidity, fuel dryness, oxygen supply, etc.).

Reflectance analysis can therefore be used to classify precisely the plant remains encountered in archaeological contexts, either in the huminite group (huminitic plant remains, unburnt; Sykorova et al. 2005) or in the inertinite group (charcoal - burnt plant remains) (Stahlschmidt et al. 2015a). This method can also be used, for example, to determine if bedding has been burnt. Schiegl et al. (2004) used reflectance analysis to characterize all burnt plant remains encountered in hearths at Sibudu (South Africa) and to compare the hearth units. The following organic components were identified and characterized in terms of reflectance: fusinite tissues, gels from cell fillings (secretinite; ICCP 2001), natural char (Kwiecińska & Petersen 2004) and fat-derived char (Goldberg et al. 2009).

Microphotometry in incident light (e.g., measurement of microreflectance or reflectance) also offers the possibility of estimating the temperature of formation of charcoal calculated from its reflectance value (Jones et al. 1991, 1993; Guo & Bustin 1998; Bustin & Guo 1999, Jones & Lim 2000).

Another field of application of organic petrology is the identification of the nature and the provenance of organic-rich sediments (sapropelic coals, oil shales, sapropelites, bitumen-rich brown coals), which have been used in Celtic Europe to sculpt objects such as armlets, beads

Figure 44.3 The reflectance histograms of the middle grey and light grey fat-derived char from the samples of both sampling localities illustrate the broad distribution of the reflectance values within the burnt bone layer (0.89 to  $1.59\% \mathrm{R}$  at HF 14-2; 0.82 to  $1.56\% \mathrm{R}$  at HF 10-2). However, reflectance values range as well as mean values (1.27%R and  $1.21\% \mathrm{R}$ ) show a good correlation, which indicates similar characteristics of the fat-derived char at both sampling location and therefore probably similar conditions of combustion. Reflectance of fat-derived char is therefore a useful tool to compare hearths with each other.

Figure 44.4 (a) Fusinite (centre) showing fractured cell walls, which are pushed into one another ('star' structure) embedded in the calcareous clayey groundmass (right). Polished thin section HF 10-2, incident white light, oil immersion. (b) Inertodetrinite (tiny fragments of fusinite; centre), fissured and fragmented fat-derived char particles (centre) and a slightly burnt bone fragment (top right) within the calcareous clayey groundmass. Polished thin section HF 10-3, incident white light, oil immersion. (c) Partially permineralized fusinite showing a well preserved tissue structure. Most of cell cavities are filled with phosphates. Polished thin section HF 10-2, incident white light, oil immersion. (d) Bone char. Note the general elongated shape, the homogeneous appearance, the porous structure, the numerous fissures oriented parallel to each other, and its high reflectivity. Polished thin section HF 10-1, incident white light, oil immersion. (e) Partially burnt bone (bottom centre). Note the fine heterogeneous appearance, the dense structure and its weak reflectivity except along its margins (transition to bone char). Fissured and fragmented fat-derived char particle (upper centre) and fusinite particle (right). Polished thin section HF 14-1, incident white light, oil immersion. (f) Same field of view as (e) but in incident light fluorescent mode, oil immersion. Note the dark fluorescence of the central part of the bone and the absence of fluorescence (black) on its periphery. The nonfluorescing fat-derived char particle and fusinite particle can be easily identified. (g) Very dark grey, fat-derived char particles. Note the irregular distribution of the vesicles, their variable size, the numerous fissures and the low reflectivity of the particles. Polished thin section HF 10-1, incident white light, oil immersion. (h) Middle grey, fat-derived char particle. Note the fine heterogeneous appearance, the irregular distribution of the vesicles, the irregular outline showing convex and concave shapes, the numerous fissures and the medium reflectivity of the particle. Polished thin section HF 14-1, incident white light, oil immersion.

Figure 44.5 (a) Light grey, fat-derived char particle. Note the homogeneous appearance, the irregular distribution of large vesicles, the irregular outline showing convex and concave shapes, the numerous fissures and the high reflectivity of the particle. Slightly burnt bone fragment (left). Polished thin section HF 14-2, incident white light, oil immersion. (b) Same field of view as (a) but in incident light fluorescent mode, oil immersion. Slightly burnt bone fragments and a light grey, fat-derived char particle can be easily identified in the upper left corner. Polished thin section HF 14-1, incident white light, oil immersion. Polished thin section HF 14-2, incident white light, oil immersion. (g) Phosphatized root tissue (cortex). Polished thin section HF 14-2, incident white light, oil immersion. (h) Microclast of Lower Jurassic bituminous shale of anthropogenic origin embedded in the phosphate-rich calcareous clayey groundmass. The microclast cut perpendicular to bedding shows numerous bright yellow fluorescing filamentous algae and pyrite framboids (small dark spheres). Polished thin section HF 14-1, incident light fluorescent mode, oil immersion.
